THE RED PAGE.
J.M.M. : Carnegie’s offer of £7OOO to build a
new library in Hobart has led to the usual squabbles as
to site and the like. The squabbles have brought about
the resignation of the book committee, and that has ex-
cited the hopes of those who watch the early struggles of
Australian literature with sympathetic interest. Whether
the works of Ogilvie, Lawson and the others are or are
not in any given public library, is, from one point of
view, a small matter. But, so far as my observation goes
the Australian author occupies but little space on the
shelves of most of the libraries supported by the Aus-
tralian taxpayer. It is strange that the Tas. national
library should not contain the books which the man who
would write an essay on Australian verse or Australian
fiction must consult. Let every man who thinks the
'life of his native land should find its reflection in books
see to it that the volumes which mirror some phases of
that life, are lodged on the shelves of the public library
with which he is most nearly concerned. And let Aus-
tralians generally express a little contempt for book com-
mittees who ignore native authors.
l IV E YOUR LIFE!
There is a conspiracy among the underfed to
palm off the emaciated for the ethereal. We cringe to
words; we fawn before proverbs: we are the paid
sycophants of Mumbo-Jumbo. We are ruled by the
senescent and the obsolescent. Men are afraid to
violate. Virtue is a papier-mache monument that
Impotence has erected over the grave of Hope. At most
there is a thin, piping “No,” and a scamper to cover.
Men seek to do the “'proper thing”—which is generally
the improper thing. Most laws are obeyed through fear
—and presto ! we have the “ virtue ” styled obedience.
The Ideal is the Cockayne of the lost. The weak
man dreams his darling sin, and calls, it “Heaven.”
The strong man enacts his darling sin, and the world
cries “Bravo!”.— sometimes and another “virtue”
comes to being. “ I)o the thing you are afraid to do,”
Emerson tells us. Shock the decorous. Defy the cus-
tomary, and let us raise altars to the rebels! It is in-
ability that wears the mask of patience, and we are
ruled by the unfßttest. Conscience?—the tribute that
weakness pays to capacity.
Strong men and their consciences must part. Each
original act smashes a scruple.. The highest Man is not a
moral being, but an a3sthete. Life for hini is a spectacle,
not an aspiration. What we call progress is but the
primitive love of the novel. We are dying of an overdose
of “ moralie acid.”
Benjamin De Casskres, in The Bookman (N.Y.)
THE MUSIC OF ODOURS
Certain investigators have discovered the music
of odours. Octaves of odours exist just as octaves of
notes. Certain perfumes harmonise well together, like
the strains of certain similar instruments. The odours of
the almond, of the heliotrope, and of vanilla, harmoni-
ously blend and produce almost the same impression
upon the nostrils. The bindweed, the orange rind and
the vervain, also associated together, form a higher
octave. The analogy of perfumes and of sounds is com-
pleted by the existence of odorous half-tones. For
example : the rose has the red geranium as a half-tone.
In bringing together odours of a natural origin, one
may obtain odorous scales of which the following is an
example:
KEY OP son. KEY OP FA.
Fa civet. Re tuberose.
Mi vervain. Do rose.
Re balm-mint. Si cinnamon.
Do pineapple. La tolu.
Si peppermint. Sol sweet peas.
La lavender. Fa musk.
Sol magnolia. Mi iris.
Fa amberfjiis. Re heliotrope.
Mi cedar,- Do geranium.
Re bergamot. Si carnation.
Do jasmine. La Peru balm.
Si pepper. Sol pergulaire.
La Tonka bean. Fa castnreum.
Sol seringa. Mi rotang.
Fa jonquil. Re clematis.
Mi Portugal. Do sandal.
Re milk of almonds. Si clove.
Do camphor. La storax.
Si aurora. Sol frangipane.
La fresh hay. Fa benzoin.
Sol . orange flower. Mi ; gillyflower.
Fa tuberose. Re vanilla.
Mi acacia Do patchouli.
These scales permit the composition of harmonious per-
fume melodies. One may make odorous accord without
employing false notes. An accord in sol, for example :
sol, sol, re, fa, sol, si. Sol pergulaire, sol sweet peas, re
violet, fa tuberose, sol orange flower, si aurora.—H. L.
BlanOiion, in La Revue.
THE AVERAGE PARSON
Our objection to him is not that he is senseless ;
this—as it concerns us not—we can patiently endure.
Nor that he is bigoted ; this we expect, and have become
accustomed to. Nor that he is small-souled, narrow, and
hypocritical ; all these qualities become him well, sitting
easily and gracefully upon him. We protest against him
because he is always “carrying on.”
To carry on, in one way or another, seems to be the
function of his existence, and essential to his health.
When he is not doing it in the pulpit he is at it in the
newspapers : when both fail him he resorts to the social
circle, the church meeting, the Sunday-school, or even
the street corner.
No sooner does a young sick-faced theologue get
safely through his ordination, as a baby finishes teething,
than"straightway he casts about him for an opportunity
to carry on. A pretext is soon found, and he goes at it
hammer and tongs ; and forty years after you shall find
him at the same trick with as simple a faith, as exalted
an expectation, as vigorous an impotence, as the day he
began.
Everything is fish that comes to his net. If a discovery
in science is announced, he will execute you an antic
upon it before it gets fairly cold. Is a new theory
advanced—ten to one while you are trying to get it
through your head he will stand on his own and make
mouths at it. A great invention provokes him into a
whirlwind of flip-flaps absolutely bewildering to the
secular eye ; while at any exceptional phenomenon of
nature, such as an earthquake, he will project himself
frog-like into an infinity of lofty gymnastic absurdities.
In short, the slightest agitation of the intellectual
atmosphere sets your average parson into a tempest of
pumping, like the jointed ligneous youth attached to the
eccentric of a boy’s whirligig. His philosophy of life may
be boiled down into a single sentence : Carry on and you
will be happy.— Ambrose Bierce.
**
••TRAITOR AND HERETIC”
Ernest Crosby, the writer of Swords and Plow-
shares (Grant Richards ; 6-s.), is a rebellious rhymer who
dedicates his verse “To the Noble Army of Traitors and
Heretics.” He stands up for the rights of man and the
rights of labour, denounces war and w arriors, exults in
love and dreams, and revolts against the Conventions
generally. Sometimes he puts a strong emotion into
rough, vibrant speech ; but often his note is a note of
violence, and always his power of expression is limited.
Yet he is homely, racy, honest ; and more polished work
would be less effective for the multitude. But to reach
the multitude his book should cost a shilling only.
The following sonnet exhibits Crosby at his poetical
best—
OM DURBAN.
Army of ghouls, defilers of the tomb !
Since king and clergy rent the rotting clay
Of England’s greatest ruler, has the day
Beheld a loathlier crime ? Beneath that dome
Lay a brave Nubian Cromwell; one, of whom
The prophets—David, Solomon—might say,
“ He is our brother who has passed away ;
Receive and do him honour in our loom."
Remember, if in days that come apace
You see the rabble's devastating lust
Snatch from the sepulchre before your face
Your gray-haired queen and drag her in the dust —
Remember, while you blanch at such a doom,
Her lords and gentlemen before Khartoum.

Here, permitted people go to shows on the uod.
In Paris they go “a l’oeil”—on the wink! One of the
chroniclers tells how the pestered Mieheau, manager of
the was approached the other day by his
tobacconist (!) for a couple of stalls. “ But certainly .
—and he wrote the order. Then he put a box of cigars
under his arm, and walked off. “ Hola ! the cigars, 51.
Mieheau! You’ve forgotten to pay.” “But” —return-
ing—“you haven’t paid for your stalls?” “Oh! that s
.. . that’s different.” “ A little : your cigars cost fifteen
francs; my stalls twenty-five; you’re ten francs to the
good.” “ Oh, no : I’d sooner return the stalls.” “ Don t
bother. Here are your cigars. I merely wished to
suggest an idea.” (The tobacconist hasn’t asked for
seats since.)
A side-light on Russian thought. The readers
of the Novosti Duja (Moscow paper) recently voted these
the greatest men of the age :—Tolstoy, Edison, Gorky,
Marconi, Bontgen, Ibsen, H. Spencer, Mommsen. Not
one of the Kitchener tribe among them ! Should we get
a list so reasonable in Australia ?
“If a book is wise, and seems to age me, I
dodge it,” says J. W. Biley. There’s age in the confes-
sion. When a man stops to think “ Will this do me
good ?” instead of taking by instinct what eye and heart
lust after, the worms are gnawing him already.
Victor Daley, our most poetical figure, waved
good-bye to Sydney from s.s. Ovalau on Ist July. His
holiday voyage, to last about seven weeks, will give him
glimpses of Lord Howe Island, Norfolk Island, some of
the New Hebrides group, and the far northern Solomons.
The verses in another column, to which it ishoped others
will be added, may be presumed to represent Daley s
reflections upon quitting Australia for the first time in
twenty years.
Sidney Lee, as Queen Vic.’s biographer, says
that Marion Crawford, not (as advertised) Marie Corelli,
was the lady’s favourite author. Now ... a nice point. . .
is that disclosure more damaging to Crawford or to
Corelli ?
Another nice point is suggested by this story,
told in the Random Recollections of Charles Brookfield, the
American actor. “Tennyson invited my great-uncle,
Henry Hallam, to be godfather to his first boy, to which
he readily consented. As they were walking up the
church-yard side by side, the historian inquired of Ten-
nyson, ‘What name do you mean to give him?’ ‘"c
thought of calling him Hallam,’ said the poet. ‘ Oh, had
you not better call him Alfred?’ modestly suggested my
great uncle. ‘Ay!'replied the naive bard, ‘but v hat
if he should turn out a fool?’"' Supposing We had to
name anew our honest Gee-Gee, would we call him. ..
Alfred?
If Lytton comes to replace Tennyson he will
preserve the line of literary descent. The first Baron
Lytton and the first Lord Tennyson could n’t agree by
any means ; and it was only after calling Bulwer ‘ the
padded man who wears the stays” that Alfred discovered
that “ the noblest answer unto such is perfect stillness
when they brawl.” The fishwife got in her nasty left
before she drew the dignified toga round her! The
second Baron Lytton—“Owen Meredith” —was a clever
plagiarist, and something more. H e fell out with Swin-
-burne. “ She was a harlot, and I was a thief,” he wrote ;
and Swinburne footnoted a malicious “ Which is
The third Baron Lytton is an unremarkable chap of 2,,
and his published work is merely domestic —one of
Longfellow’s “living poems”— alias baby.
Tell us a neater repartee than that attributed
to an amateur actress, among whose bouquets a cabbage
was thrown. She picked it up, read the accompanying
card, advanced to the footlights, and said, “It gives me
great pleasure to know that Mr. M— — has enjoyed my
performance. I hoped that the audience would like n ,
but I never expected for a moment that Mr. M would
so far lose his head as to throw it upon the stage ! ”
George Reid, for plaintiff, retorted prettily in a
Sydney breach of promise case the other day. Defendant,
a clothing manufacturer with a leaning to mackintoshes,
remarked that he was shocked at the false swearing
against him. Then Beid (with a great show of sympathy):
“ How repulsive it must be to you ! And you m the oil-
skin line , too 7 ”
Lond. Financial News has been exposing the
wav in which The Times allowed its credit to be used in
selling an out-of-date edition of The Encyclopedia
Britannica. One purchaser threatens to bring an action
at law on the ground that he was induced to buy by a
misleading description. He “is prepared to swear that
when he signed the contract of purchase by instalment
pavments he was under the impression that lie was
dealing direct with The Times, and that he would not
have made the purchase had he known that he was
dealing with an American syndicate of booksellers.
Between the tale of Yankee book-agents to whom Uie
Times credit has been “ transferred,” and the tale of
the inadequacies of an encyclopiedia dating from 18/or
thereabouts, The Financial News is making things un-
pleasantly warm for Printing-house Square.
“My poor organ!” said Auguste Wiegand
when he was leaving Australia; “what will become of
it V” In the months that have passed we have had ample
time to realise what Sydney lost in losing Wiegand. And
his magnificent organ? Given over to tinkers, ainateuis,
and others, the protbg6s of this alderman or that, it has
shrieked protest from every pipe ; and in the end it has
been so injured that possibly an expenditure of hundreds
of pounds will be needed for refitting. Lemare, coming
from America under special engagement, found the instru-
ment so far out of tune that a week’s labour made little
impression on it. In this condition it must have been for
a long time ; yet nearly every week “ recitals ” have been
given, and “talented performances” have been duly
"praised in the daily papers. Wiegand knew .
As for Lemare, he was well advertised, and a
large audience mustered in response to the advertise-
ment. They heard —and went away—and all the ad-
vertisement in the world will not bring them back again.
Those who'listen' d to Lemare may better appreciate
what Sydney lost in losing Wiegand. Lemare plays
well, of course : he is a good executant as ex-
ecutants go ; but the man lacks power. . tem-
perament : instead of passion, he gives sentiment,
having no passion to give. Wiegand, whatever his faults
and concessions, was more than a technical musician : he
was a musician by instinct; and he was a lusty giant
who chuld dominate his organ—a physical task—and
inspire it to render surging harmonies dormant until he
came to rouse them. For Lemare the Town Hall organ is
too big—or for the organ Lemare is too little.
Musical technique is cheap nowadays—and
advertisement is cheaper. But temperament and
power are as rare as ever they were. It is
temperament that makes the difference between
Kreisler and Kubelik, among violinists ; or—with
a wider interval—between Paderewski and Ham-
bour<>', among pianists. When Hambourg came to
Australia first the coterie of newspaper critics extolled
his prestidigitation. There was musical genius for you-
to hit all those notes in a minute, and hit them cleanly .
When Hambourg came to Australia last, with some loss
of youthful sparkle, some acquired sense that there is such
a thing as interpretation, and that great composers—like
everything else—give in proportion as you give to them,
the critics talked of his sanity, his cultured seventy.
Vet, alike in his riot and in his restraint, Hambourg
W s comparatively barren. An accomplished player, a
fr.ayer with plenty of nervous strength to give his playing
force, yet a cold interpreter—merely the son of a pro-
fessor of music who had been devoted to the profession
of music.

THE QUEST.
A moment, by the crowded quay,
The stately vessel veers and sways ;
The next, with gallant head flung free.
She seeks the shining Eastern ways.
Then what to me are tender cries ?
And what to me are ivaving hands t
The pennon that above us flies
Thrills, like my heart, for distant lands!
Among the glimmering faces there,
So pigmy grorni, 1 cannot find
The face of Her beyond compare ,
The Rose of all fond Woman-kind.
For many a year, in many a guise,
Through many a day of hope and dread
And many a night of tears and sighs,
1 sought one proud and golden head.
My Blanchelys ! No sooner lent
Than snatched afar by cruel Fates . . .
But yonder in the Orient
For me, for me, a woman waits t
Upon her island shore of bliss,
All gravely .sweet, and darkly calm,
She spurns her island lover's kiss
And hoards for me her lips’ red balm ;
And, with the draughts sad longings past
And bitter dreams shall find surcease f
And 1 shall be content at last,
My anguish perished in her peace.
Speed on, 0 gallant ship, speed on !
Leap o'er the tossing leagues of foam !
My heart yearns for thebenison
Upon her heart to be at home.
For all my days of ashen strife
1 nothing reck, if come but this —
That day of proud, exultant life
With her, my bride, my Brunelys l

F.P. DUNNB.
AS SEEN BY MK. DALEY
It was a golden morning, opulent with sun-
shine. I went into the breakers at Manly and was half
smothered in silver.
“ Glorious ! ” said an acquaintance to me, as we rose on
the crest of a roller.
“ Glorious it is,” I replied.
“ Washes away your troubles,” he remarked, diving
under the mane of another white horse.
“ Wish I could think so—wish I had some of my
creditors here.”
He laughed, with his mouth full of salt water. “ Don’t
trouble about that,” he observed. ‘‘ Let them trouble.”
We came out together, and dressed at the same place.
Then I knew him. He was my biggest creditor in the
village. A most genial fellow when you meet him outside
his shop.
“We’ll talk about that little matter later on. Take
your time,” says he.
You see, in face of the great forces of Nature
it is only the poorest kind of creature who will think of
his own paltry business. Yet Many years ago W.
H. L. Bailey—very burlesquel.v notable in The Bulletin at
that time—and John Farrell and I went out to Bondi.
We gazed on the Pacific for a few minutes.
“ Keats,” says Farrell, rolling out the quotation—
Or like stout Cortes when, with eagle oyes.
He gazed on the Pacific, and all his men
Stared at each other with a woild surmoise,
Soilent—upon a peak in Darien.
“ What’s your opinion, Bailey ? ”
“ It gives you a devilish fine appetite,” says he.
I dressed myself, drank a hurried cup of tea,
and went out to face the music. No doubt about it this
time. It had to be done. And promptly. As I was
rushing to catch the boat I noticed how cheerfully the
sunlight shone on the red roofs of the houses in Darley-
street. And, standing clear like a high-born lady beyond
the ruck of pines and gums, was a Silver Beech. Its
exquisite exotic grace charmed and lured me. I walked
up the hill and inspected it.
It was not a silver beech, after all. Spotted gum, they
called it ; but with its leopard-like markings it was well
worth going to see...
The boat had gone. What matter? The affluent air
was as a sapphire robe around me. Birds were chirping
cheerfully. I thought of John Lemmone and his flute. I
met Lemmone in Grafton last year. He was with the
Mane Narelle company. There was in the hall a man
sitting beside me with a breath like a breeze from Gol-
gotha. “ What do you think of the playing,” I said,
reaching for my hat.
“Might charm snakes,” he said ; “but by ! it
don’t charm mine.”
J*
t was no use in paltering with the matter.
I had to face the music and get money without delay. I
was in full charge for the boat when I met Miss , a
charming girl and old friend of mine.
“ W hat is the reason of this hurry ?” said she. *
“ Hurry’s over,” I replied, coming to a gallant full stop.
‘ Get us sit down here. I have something to tell you.”
We sat on a bench on the Esplanade.
■ “I’m going to be married next month.”
1‘ Oh ! ”
It was a blow to me. I do not know whether the
feeling is general or not, but I do know that, in my own
case, I dislike to hear of my women-friends getting
married. I prefer that they should remain single and
work slippers for me. Not that I care for slippers, for I
never wear them, but the sentiment is there. Any man
with a trace of delicacy in his nature must feel annoyed
when lie knows that some strange, coarse, male person is
permitted, even with the sanction of Law and Gospel, to
kiss and . . . hug his lady friends. Of course, he cannot
marry them all himself, but that is no reason why they
should marry anybody else.
“And my veil is to be of Limerick lace. It’s just
lovely. But you must come to the wedding. Arthur
says so, and I say so. And you must make a nice little
speech at the breakfast.”
I nodded gloomily.
“ And you must say that he is—Arthur, I mean—the
most envied man you know. And wish us happiness,
because you are sure we deserve it."
“Any more?”
“ Yes. You must say that you know from your own
experience that it is better for a man to be married than
single, because when he is married he has somebody to
work for, and somebody who will look after his money,
and ”
“Yes?”
“ And you might add—for you know it is true, for you
told me so yourself—yon might add that you were a
ne’er-do-weel and a dissolute person, who' used to drink
and gamble and come home at all hours of the night and
the morning, until you found a pretty wife who could
look after you, and take you to the theatre, and to
evening parties, and to the races, and wear nice dresses
and hats to please you.”
“ Well, it will be'mostly lies,” I said, reflectively.
“ That won’t trouble y on,” she remarked, cheerfully.
We shook hands, and I rushed again to the boat. There
was not a moment to spare. When I was within about
forty yards of the pier I met a man, who stopped me to
tell me about the catching of an alleged shark.
“Tried the brute with pork,” he said, “but he
would n’t'look at it. But a bait of beef got him all right.”
“ How do you account for his refusing pork?”
“ Was n’t a shark at all. He was a giant jew-fish.”
«£*
I caught the next boat. Time, 1.50 p.m. The
music had to be faced. It is a long way from Manly to
Sydney, if you are in a hurry. Fortunately, I met a
friend. We had a most interesting conversation. But
we differed. The argument was about the relative brain-
capacity of brachycephalic and dolichocephalic skulls.
As my own skull is brachycephalic, I naturally cham-
pioned that side.
“When we leave the boat we’ll go right up to the
Anthropological Society’s rooms and settle the matter.”
When we arrived I was shown a lot of skulls, and casts
of skulls. Cast of the Neanderthal skull. Casts of the
skulls found in Southern France. Scores of skulls.
“Look at that,” said my friend, tapping the latter
fondly. “That belonged to a man who travelled long
before Abraham came away from Ur of the Chaldees.
He saw Atlantis, did Bob (they have pet names for all
their skulls), long before the poor little parvenu moderns
whom we call ancients ever dreamt of it. And he saw it
with fine observant eyes, you may he sure. See those
ridges over the eyebrows 1 I should n’t be surprised to
learn that Bob had been the Shakespeare of his day.”
I forget how the argument was concluded, f think
that it was a drawn game. But time was flying, and I
had to face the music. And I had to work for the money
which had to be raised. No nonsense. Money earned
without work is of no use to anybody. I made a bee-line
for the office. And then
“What' Petruchio!” I exclaimed. His name isn’t
Petruchio ; but he’s rather a pet of mine, so I call him
that for variety. Petruchio had recently returned from
Andalusia and Tuscany. He smelt of Velaszquez, and
Rafael, and garlic. Told me that he had a Spanish cloak
which, daring the Peninsular War, had belonged to the
famous guerrilla general Mina. It looked like it. I saw
it. But you would have been delighted to have heard the
fine bull-fight, and guitar, and sombrero and mantilla
talk we had.
“I did n’t travel in Northern Spain,” says Petruchio.
“ Too gloomy. Gothic. Andalusia for an artist I’’
We went out of the office and walked up the street a
little way. A musician with fire in his eye met us. “You
must,” he exclaimed, “you must hear this —my very
latest ! ” And he took ufc into a little room in Paling’s,
and played what I suppose was a rather fine piece.
“ What do you think of it? ” he said.
“ School of Brahms,” I answered sententiously.
“ And you, sir? ”
“ Don’t think of it. Know it, and like it.”
“ Where did you hear it before ?”
“ In Seville—in a zarzuela.”
This was so obviously like the probable fact that I
thought it better we should leave. Besides, time was
flying, and I had to face the music.
When I arrived at the office the doors were closed.
Everybody was gone. I returned home that night with
some free passes to the circus, given me by a friend in
that business. That was the result of my day’s work. I
called to see my landlord, and was informed that he was
dead. His son says he will wait. A fine, tolerant young
Australian this 1
V.J.D.

Various Verses.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A Tryst.
When all the hills are touched with gold,
And every wind fresh perfume brings—
When dancing buds and blossoms hold
The rapture of a thousand Springs;
If it shall be that I am laid
In that still spot beside the sea
Where we have watched the daylight fade,
And all the sunset’s mystery,
Come thou and kneel awhile apart
By my low bed, with pansies rimmed;
Yet smile —I would not have thy heart
Sad, nor thine eyes’ soft splendor dimmed.
I will not tell thee that my dust
Will wake and quicken at thy tread ;
All shall be silence, save for just
Some bright bird whirring overhead.
No misty wraith at eve shall rise
To greet thee as the moments pass;
No shade save where the moonlight lies
In silver broid’ry on the grass.
But, watching from the heights of God,
My soul may lean from that far place,
And, in the paths we two have trod,
Hold tryst with thine a little space.
S.A.
A.M.B.R.
A Ballade of Regrets.
Of all joys once I owned this chief :
To be beneath the bending trees
What time the gently-falling leaf,
Enamored of the scented breeze,
Would dance amid the hurrying bees.
There would I lurk, like Ariel.
Alas ! for youth’s rare rhapsodies :
Gone are the days I loved so well !
And then, again, ’mid June’s cold, brief,
But happy, days, when frosted frieze
Would hang like glittering tears of grief,
Which hardly could the sun appease,
I welcomed winter’s legacies,
Rejoicing in the icy spell.
My heart no longer claims such fees ;
Gone are the days I loved so well !j
With many maids I’ve played the thief,
Excusing kisses with Love’s pleas,
And holding hearts in reckless fief.
Professing much, with thoughtless ease
—Oh, chief of Cupid’s Pharisees !
The tale of tales I’d often tell.
Now have I learned Life’s stern decrees;
Gone are the days I loved so well!
Grim gaoler Time, oh ! turn thy keys,
Unlock this dismal prison cell
Of age. In vain ! I drink the lees ;
Gone are the days I loved so well !
N.S.W.
S. ELLIOTT NAPIER.
Laburnum and Boses.
The gold laburnum droops its head,
The lilac swells its scented sprays,
And roses open, dewy red.
Anemones their furls have spread,
And fresh, beside the dusty ways,
The gold laburnum droops its head.
The hyacinth is shrunk and dead,
The budding honeysuckle sways,
And roses open, dewy red.
The violets blue have shyly fled—
But high in gorgeous gold displays
The gold laburnum droops its head.
The lilies of the valley shed
Their sweet scent over sunny days,
And roses open, dewy red.
One blossom dies, one blooms instead—
So weaves Earth’s many-sided maze:
The gold laburnum droops its head,
And roses open, dewy red.
M.L.
AHAU.
Sweet Anne.
Thy doves are dead in the cote, Sweet Anne,
I have twisted their little white necks;
The star is sunk that thou sangest, Sweet Anne,
Thy roses are rain-riven wrecks.
A cloud hath covered the moon, Sweet Anne,
And the golden show in the sky ;
Thy love is drowned i’ the stream, Sweet Anne,
It was time that thy love should die.
No one and nothing save I, Sweet Anne,
May remain to brighten thy way ;
Thy roses and doves are dead, Sweet Anne—
Will the dawn be desolate grey ?
No one and nothing save I, Sweet Anne,
Can live for thy heart’s delight;
O sing as you sang to the star, Sweet Anne —
Be a thrush in my breast all night.
Vic.
HUGH MCCRAK.
They Call it Peace.
They call it Peace ! The land is bare,
The hearth—a wild hyena’s lair;
The stalwart men whose courage high
Said : “Freemen live or Freemen die !”
No more their breasts the battle dare.
A weary look the mothers wear
And yet, whene’er ye wander nigh,
They still the drooping children’s cry—
We call it Peace !
The dreary plains that once were fair
Now bake and smoke beneath the glare
While hireling bands encamped lie,
Or sweep in sullen thunder by,
While beasts of prey the homestead share—
They call it Peace !
Q.
P.L.

PAINTERS’ COLIC
EVIDEIVTCE
VALUABLE TO THE TRADE.
The Case o-F
Mr. F. DAVISON.
(by our reporter.)
The annual exhibitions of pictures painted
by local artists receive more appreciation
from the public every year. The gene-
rous support accorded to such ex*
hibits serves as a graceful acknow-
ledgement of the advancement made by
students. The persistent and painstak-
ing efforts of our painters have an educa-
tional effect upon their numerous admirers,
who are agreeably made familiar with de-
pictions of typical Australian life and
scenery. Another division of painters to
whom appreciative recognition is due are
those who embellish and decorate our
private residences and business establish-
ments. Mr. Francis Davison, of No. 27
Wyndham-street, Alexandria, belongs to
this class, in connection with which he had
something to say of an interesting charac-
ter :
“ I think,” said Mr. Davison, “ that the
unhealthy materials used in my trade had
something to do with giving me years of
suffering. Painters are especially liable to
colic, which inflicts the most indescribable
pains in the bowels. My cause of trouble
was principally in the side ; at least, it was
there I was attacked first. The ag .ny was
so great that I used to break out in a state
of perspiration through sheer excess of pain.
Often when walking down the street I was
suddenly seized with such dreadful parox-
ysms, which completely doubled me up,
making it impossible for me to walk for
several minutes.”
> “ How long were you thus afflicted ? ”
“ For fully five years I did not know
what it was to be free from pain, which
afterwards appeared in the stomach with
increased violence. Indeed, the piercing
Bhafts of torture which assailed that region
made me moan with pain, from which I
hardly ever experienced a respite. I
rapidly became so exhausted and worn out
that my friends were anxious about my
condition, and my family were in great dis-
tress whenever they heard me groaning. A
little relief was obtained through the ap-
plication < f hot fomentations and various
kinds of poultices; but the ease only lasted
about a minute, and then I could feel the
pains gradually coming back, until its full
force burst upon me again with increased
severity, and prostrated me with its
violence. I was often incapable of going
to work, and even when I did go I was
afraid to undertake the perilous positions
which painters have sometimes to occupy.
I avoided as much as possible ascending
ladders, in case of a sudden seizure of my
trouble rendering me powerless to hang
on.”
“ You appear to have had a bad time ! ”
“ Yes, I had. There was a feeling of un-
certainty and anxiety in my mind every
morning as to what the day would bring
forth. Quite unaccountably, I could not
discard from my thoughts the notion that a
dreadful accident, or some impending evil,
was in store for me.”
“ Perhaps you are naturally nervous ? ”
“No ; quite the reverse. Before my
sickness came on I did not know wh?t fear
or nervousness were. I could go out in the
dead of night and confront any danger with-
out the least hesitation. But now my
nerves were completely shattered. I was
afraid of my own shadow. Naturally
enough I sought medical advice, a doctor
visiting and prescribing for me regularly.
This occurred when I was living in Queens-
land, and I don’t mind telling you that by
this time I was reduced to a skeleton
through my inability to eat, which I very
seldom did. I had no appetite whatever ;
but, knowing that if I did not eat a little
to keep body and soul together I would
soon die of starvation, I used to try and
brace myself up when meal times came
round, and take my seat at the table.
Directly the food was placed thereon I was
overcome with a tsrribi® sickness si the
stomach. Hurriedly vacating my chair, I
used to go outside, where continual retch-
ing brought on insufferable headaches and
giddiness. By way of variety my stomach
occasionally wanted food. I felt as though
I could really enjoy a little, but a few
minutes after swallowing anything I had
severe pains in the chest, and swelling, be-
sides acute sensations under my shoulders,
ifc was a very rare thing to have a night’s
sleep, although I went to bed every evening
feeling quite exhausted and done up. I
was accustomed to rise in the morning with
a nasty taste in the mouth, after turning
restlessly about nearly all night, with my
limbs aching with intense weariness, and
the pains in my side still gnawing away,
As though to a id their quota to the evils I
have already mentioned, my throat was
parched and dry, and constipation was
another trouble. A feeling ot drowsiness
never left me, and, combined with my
general weakness, I appeared like a man
half dead. The prospect of getting through
a day’s work in this condition was not a
very bright one, and many a time I had to
come home unable to continue my work. I
really thought I was done for.”
“ What gave you that impression ?”
“Because my doctor could not cure me.
I then came to New South Wales, and en-
gaged another doctor, under whose care I
remained with the same unsatisfactory re-
sults till my hopes were exhausted. The
medicines prescribed by both doctors were
utterly useless in every respect.”
“ Did you consult a third doctor ? ”
“No; I managed without. One of my
mates, who had suffered as I was suffering,
told me he was cured by Clements Tonic,
even when his medical adviser had failed.
That hint was good enough for me. I
bought some Clements Tonic, and when I
had taken three bottles I felt quite a
different man. My pains in the sole were
much easier, and all the other ailments bad
been quite cured. Appetite, sleep, and
strength returned in a short time, and de-
spondency gave way to cheerfulness Three
more bottles of Clements Tonic cured me in
every detail, and I have not been troubled
with my old sickness since, and I give you
my authority to publish these remarks
simply out of gratitude, for I trust a relation
of my sufferings and cure may help other
poor sufferers on the way to recovery.”
STATUTORY DECLARATION.

THURSDAY, JULY 16, 1903.
An Australian Navy.
The case against the Australian naval tribute only
showed its full force when Premier Barton came
forward with a statement of the reasons which
influenced him to give it his support. Mr. Barton
was able to advance no single argument, economic
or patriotic, for the plan which he proposed; was
able to, refute the objections which have been
urged against it in no essential particular. From
whatever point of view—lmperial or anti-imperial
—the scheme is considered, its only logical defence
lies in the theory that Australia is a poor God-
forsaken country without administrative brains
courage,, enterprise or intelligence, not fit to have
any dignified part in its own naval defence, not
to, be trusted with'any weapons lest it should
misuse them, worthy only to drudge for the
money which a higher and more capable power
shall spend ; and to provide the lob-lolly boys, the
slushies, and the deck-swabbers, whose efforts a
higher power shall direct or control. (That is the
only excuse, unless it is assumed that Australia is
a treacherous, violent, and revolutionary land
winch ean t be trusted with a navy lest it should
join _it to, the Russian fleet and bombard
hondam) Premier Barton was not able, with
all Ins casuistry, to, conceal this unpleasant view
of tne case-, so. he let argument for the proposal,
into which lie was entrapped by Chamberlain
go, by default, and, in a cloud of inept platitudes
and irrelevant generalities, put forward, rather
than advocated, the Naval Agreement Bill.
* * *' •
The precise terms of the agreement, as embodied
in a schedule of the Bill, are :
The British Admiralty will place 11 ships on the Aus-
tralian station. The ships are to be “placed" here, but
their range of effort is to be from the North Pole on the
north to the South Pole on the south, over all the waters
of Asia (including the Persian Gulf), along the East Coast
of Africa as far as Madagascar, and over the Pacific
to somewhere adjacent to the coast of M&ido-in fact, as
far abroad as a warship of ordinary coal Capacity can go.
There is no provision that in either peace or war time the
vessels shall remain in Australian waters. It is even
possible that, given a sanctimonious admiral, they may
be off Madagascar or Kamtschatka while the Melbourne
Cup is being run.
The vessels are to be directed by the Admiralty and
officered by the Royal Navy. But Australia and Maori-
iand may find brass-polishers and deck-scrubbers and
stokers for four of the vessels-three reserve drill ships
arid one active service ship— at special rates of pav. In
what way the rates are to be special, specially- high or
specially low, is not stated. J “
Austrah-a and Maoriland are to pay £240,000)a year to
the British Admiralty.
Australia and Maoriland are to be given the nrivileee
of finding 10 “ cadets" for the British navy each year.
These cadets, after being trained in Britain and; taught,
to say haw with the proper accent, and, to snub a
beasUy colonial with due hauteur, may become officers off
the Royal Navy, and be flogged by their British superiors
by private,and informal court-martial.
The Australian .Naval Reserves ,are to be disbanded as
Australian reserves and to be enrolledlas reserves under
the direct control of the British Admiralty.
The Imperial Government, is to ‘“recognise ” (here
comes a trace of the influence of the frozen meat patriot),
the advantages to be got by buying coal and meat and
flour for its fleet in these waters, fromiAustralasia.
Taking these conditions seriatim the first confers
no benefit, but takes away whateven-advantage is
derived under the present agreement! from having-
the Australian squadron permanently stationed
in peace time in these waters, to lend distinction.
to our race meetings and to increase the profits of
rbrewers. Th'e second is directly insulting in
its implication that the Australian is only fit for
ttie dirty menial work of a navy—the work of
tidying up the weevils which have escaped from
bls cuits and .making up the English officer’s
f i I/ 16 third is a plain robbery by confidence
trick, the exaction of a tribute on the plea of
rendering services which are not be to rendered.
“ u , r “ . 1S not , ln any sense an advantage,
seeing that Australia already has the “privilege”
o nominating cadets and doesn’t exercise it to
anything like the allowed extent. The fifth is
our own P ow ers of self-defence.
S 1X +u the appearance of being a concession,
but, as the British Admiralty would probably buy
coal at Newcastle and its frozen meat at Christ-
r™rch, anyhow, for vessels in these waters, its
practical benefit is nil.
* * * *
It is, in all, a poor sort of agreement to defend
before an Australian Parliament. The only really
cogent arguments which might be ad vanced in its
favor Mr. Babton did not dare to mention. He
might have said that it was dangerous for the
itmpire to allow Australia to have a fleet of its
own, or even naval reserves of its own. That
idea is, no doubt, at the back of the mind of many
a British politician, and from the point of view of
the man who is for the Empire first and Australia
second it is if capable of being established as a
fact or as a reasonable theory—valid enough. But
m view of the recent remarkable display of
wdhngness on the part of Australians to back
up the Empire ’’ even in the performance
of a brutal burglary aggravated by whole-
sa!e homicide, it would be a public display
of beastly ingratitude for such an argument to be
mentioned openly by the British authorities.
Australia showed the excess of loyalty of a party
politician at the time of the S’African war, being
*2, support the Empire “right or wrong.”
. lh ® -kmpu-e reciprocates by deciding that it
is not safe to trust Australia with a naval gun.
but does not publicly mention the fact; nor does
Mr. Babton. Mr. Babton might, alternatively,
have borrowed from the Freetrade party the old
familiar argument that the Australian is a poor
fP rt .°^ l c^ e ?' ture > not fitted by Nature to exercise
tne lull rights of a citizen and to have manufac-
tures and a fleet of his own, but necessarily
dependent on some people of a higher mental
type for those equipments of greatness. But he
was silent, too, on that question. His client had a
poor case that was best left without argument
There was, however, in the K.C.M.G. and the
ho ping-to-be K. C. M. G. element in Parliament, a
good friend on the jury,” and that made a bad
cause safe. For the Naval Tribute Bill does seem
unhappily, to be quite safe.
* * • •
But in spite of the almost hopelessness of the
effort—the Australian Parliament being, seem-
ingly, bent on entering into a compact which is
as economically foolish as it is sentimentally
degrading— The Bulletin Would like to put the
case against this Tribute scheme again, not the
case as it would appear to the Australian con-
sidering his own country first in all things and
the Empire” second, but as it would show
to a strong, sane Imperialist, a man regarding Aus-
tralian interests as necessarily bound up with the
Empire, and ready to take any course which seems
best for the Empire—provided that it does seem
best after careful examination, and is not merely
assumed to be best on the assertion of somebody
The alternative to the Naval Tribute proposed by
Mr. Babton, is the expenditure of a like sum of
money, or, if necessary, a much larger sum of
money, on an Australian navy, which would be
organised on the same lines as the Australian
army, and would probably be, for some time at
least, commanded by a British Admiral and to a
very considerable extent officered by Britishers.
I his navy would, in times of peace, be used as a
training squadron for Australian naval men
In times of war it would be available for
the defence of Australia and, there is no doubt
for the assistance of Britain in other waters if
that .were. called for. The service to “the
Empire in merely maintaining the coaling and
victualling stations in these waters, in providing
and keeping up a base for the British forces, and
in protecting the huge amount of British capital
invested in Australasia, would be immense In
addition there is not the slightest doubt that it
would, whilst the present temper of Australia
continues, be available as a volunteer fleet to
assist Britain in any trouble abroad. Further
by setting up an independent, though sym-
pathetic, organisation it would give a stan-
dard of comparison and of emulation now
wanting. In the S’African war Australian
soldiers were able to give valuable points to
British Tommies in cow-stealing, farm-burning
and concentrating, as well as in other and more
dignified branches of the art of war. It is reason-
able to suppose that an Australian navy would
like an Australian army, develop points in tactics
and in management which would be of value to
the Britisher. The Lords of the Admiralty are
supposed now to be perfect—as the War Office
was supposed to be perfect five years ago. But it
is possible to imagine that there are faults in the
navy as there were in the army, that a fine tradi-
tion has been allowed to congeal into a dead and
apathetic routine, and that a naval war would
§Jiow as alarming faults in Britain’s sea power as
the last campaign showed in its land forces,
lhe payment by Australasia of £240,000 a
year would do nothing to correct those ’faults
a ® exist: but the gradual evolution of an
Australian fleet, based on the British model but
not bound by sacrosanct tradition to follow that
™°del in every detail, would provide an invalu-
able standard of comparison and of criticism.
The free-and-easy Australian soldier has had his
reforming services to the British army very freely
acknowledged. That acknowledgment and an
unfulfilled promise of a billet on the trams are
the chief rewards he has reaped for his volun-
teering. It is more than possible that, with the
growth of an Australian navy, a future generation
of Britishers might be expressing equally devout
thanks for free-and-easy Australian naval men,
and some of them might also be promised a billet
■on the trams.
* • • *
That is the case from the Imperial point of
view m favor of an Australian navy and against
the proposals of the Imperial Government.
Opposed to it, Mr. Babton can only place the
sanction given to the scheme which he advocates
by a British administration which has put on
record its policy as “blundering through some-
dare not P lead a Possible disintegration
ot the Empire from the existence of an Australian
«eet, for that would be, on the present facts,
absurd. He dare not plead that the Aus-
tralian is only fitted to be a slushy, and
must have a Cecil for an admiral, and a
younger Cecil for a captain, and a Cecil’s
second cousin for a middy. The Naval Tribute
scheme proposes no effective defence for Aus-
tralia ; it is to be an eleemosynary sort of guard,
;given like a penny to a beggar, if there is no better
use for the coin elsewhere. An Australian navy
would give some effective defence. The Tribute
scheme suggests no additional protection for the
Empire; it only provides that Australia is to
contribute a.dole to the funds of the Admiralty.
An Australian navy would give new ships, new
men and new ideas. The Tribute scheme will
help to entrench the conservative and out-of-date
methods which are more than suspected to be
eating into the British navy system. An Aus-
tralian navy would provide some check on that
conservatism, crystallising into inefficiency
under the mismanagement of titled noodles,
who are naval officers because they were
born in certain beds—not because they possess
certain capacities. Spain was once the greatest
naval power of Europe. Its navy began to
crumble when the tendency was to promote by
title and not by worth. The same tendency now
infects the British navy, and is, perhaps, already
infecting it with dry rot. The foundation of an
Australian navy would provide some little check
against that drift. . The truest friends, alike of
Australia and Britain, are those who speak in its
favor. If not desirable on behalf of Australia
it would be on behalf of Britain.
The "Federal Extravagance” Lie.
All over Australia there is a casual babbling by
ignorant men, and uninformed political women,
incapable or wilfully misleading papers about
Federal extravagance/’ and the lie grows weari-
some through too much repetition. If there was
any strong evidence to support it the tale would
be less remarkable, but there is ; little or none. It
is based on the assumption that the Common-
wealth Government should make bricks without
straw, should do its work without clerks or
officers, should draw no salary, should hang out
in a bark hut, and in a general way should cost
nothing and yet pay everybody. As one horrible
example of “Federal extravagance” the Laun-
ceston Examiner recently announced that the
Federal Treasury department, which does busi-
r over Australia, was worked at a cost of
£6170 for a complete year; which sum is less than

m the single State of N.S.W. When the Com-

and didn fc grow up in a night. And a very large

Freetrade section which, under George Reid’s

m the Constitution, and which demanded that the
Barton Government should be expelled from
office because its bush capital wasn’t established
during the first session of Parliament—now de-
mands that the Barton Government shall be ex-
pelled for proposing to establish it at all. Yet this
party makes no definite proposal about abolishing
the clause which gives New South Wales possession
of the Federal city under these conditions. If
such a proposal were made the chances are that the
same party would preach revolution.
* * * *
The Australian Government started under
many difficulties. In the last days of the
old order of things various States gaily
and lavishly raised the salaries of the officials
to be taken over by the Commonwealth—and
left the Commonwealth to pay the bill. Victoria
went further $ almost its last act as a “ sovereign
State was to enact that each one of its officials
taken over by the Commonwealth should receive
the highest rate of wages paid to any officer of his
class in.any part of the Commonwealth—in other
words, it handed over the most costly civil service
in all Australia. Also Victoria reduced its inland
postal rate at the last moment, and left the Austra-
lian Government to make the best it could of the
transferred postal department under the loss.
Then the Australian Government is expected to
pay out of revenue for all postal, telegraph and
telephone works, which the States charged to
loans, and yet it is expected to return the States
more revenue from these sources than the States
received under the old system. The States, though
they have handed over to the Australian Parlia-
ment a vast amount of the work of Government
insist on keeping up the old pomp and glory—the
six full-blown imported Governors, the Agents-
General, the Upper Houses, the Government
House establishments,.the Parliamentary flunkeys
and dancing masters; even the Assemblies haven’t
been seriously reduced, and the Ministries are
mostly as strong as ever. And though the Com-
monwealth Government has relieved the States of
about 3J millions of necessary expenditure, the
aggregate expenditure of the States is almost
exactly what it was before they were thus relieved.
The lamentation about Federal extravagance helps
to cover up these things. The States represent the
busted prodigal sitting among his swine and weep-
ing over the extravagance of the prudent brother
who remained at home.
* * , *" •
The Federal Government ran its whole business
for the year ended June, 1902, at a cost, in new
expenditure, of rather under £276,000. That
was the cost of the two Houses of Parliament, the
Ministry, the necessary officers, clerks, depart-
ments, Auditor-General, &c., the Governor-
General, and all the other fixings. Also
it covered payment to the States for the
travelling expenses of Federal members—one
item which the States seldom include conspicu-
ously in their own cost of government, though the
item is there all the time. In the year ended
June, 1903, this very small expense was reduced
by intense economy, to about £256,000. In every
State (bar only S.A.) which closed its accounts
on 30th June, it was mentioned with glad sur-
prise- that things were much better than was
expected because the Commonwealth Govern-
ment’s intense economy had enabled it to return
to the State Treasurers an amount which
exceeded the largest anticipations. The Com-
monwealth Government, said to be wallowing
in ever-increasing extravagance, returned to the
States for the financial year just closed
£832,320 more than it did in the pre-
ceding year. And the States, which fail to
meet their obligations even under these favorable
conditions, have to do something, or say sorne-
tlnng, to divert attention from their own in-
capacity and iniquity.
.•* * •
It is time that the tables were turned and that
-b ederal politicians commenced a crusade against
State extravagance. The cry of the younger
brother who lives sumptuously in a palace, that
his dimculties are caused by the extravagance of
nis elder brother who hangs out penuriously in a
gunyah, is becoming a public scandal as well as
* 0- Ihe position may be condensed thus:
otate politicians, though they have now much
less to do than formerly,. don’t want to reduce
their numbers, pomp, dignity or privileges ; they
don t want to abate their old lavishness in buying
public support with roads and bridges; and they
don t want to make themselves unpopular by
imposing new taxation.. Therefore the deficit
which has been chronic in Australia for 20 years
is just as chronic as ever. But, for the first time,
there is an Australian Government to lay the
blame on, and, of course, politicians and the
papers they control or influence, and the political
organisations whose wires they pull, lay the blame
there enthusiastically. And the unreasoning
• n ° o women of both sexes, which is
incapable of independent inquiry, and which
jva ■> k understand the figures of the case if it
did inquire, follows suit. The cry of “Federal
extravagance ” directed against almost the only
genuinely economical Government that Australia
possesses, ranks, as a popular delusion, with witch
craft, the Boer war craze, prayers for rain, the
magnificent natural resources of this great
country, and the idea that Jews sacrifice
children at religious festivals.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Victoria’s Unpatriotic Bankers.
The Bankers’ _ Magazine (Melbourne) is a
monthly publication run by bankers, and may be
fairly taken to express the sense and patriotism of
a class which is much given to putting on airs of
superior knowledge when questions of public
finance are involved. The following is dug out of
its latest issue:—
Abolish the Income Tax Altogether.
Now there is no occasion for the increased tax, if in
this bad year there is to be a surplus of £lOO,OOO. Next
year, with a good season, may bring a thumping surplus,
but do you think, reader, it will be devoted to remitting
taxation ? Your cynical and heated reply shows that
you understand the ways of Australian Governments.
You have never heard of a tax being taken off, you
say, once it was imposed. Well, we think vou will at
least see a good light made against the reiniposition of
the Shiels Income Tax, and we think a good case can
be made out for the abolition of the old Income Tax as
well. It was only a temporary measure, George Turner
said, so we want it taken away now, and hidden some-
where until we have a war or some other disaster on our
hands. When an emergency shall arise we have now
actually nothing fresh to tax ; we are in the position of
a young country, without internal or external war,
fairly prosperous, but gasping under all the taxes
invented by the ingenuity of worn-out, old civilisations
racked by terrific wars. If we had war or famine here,
we would have no fresh tax upon us. The present is a
most opportune time for all public bodies, such as
Chambers of Commerce and the various institutes, to
resolutely fight the Income Tax. It can be shown to be
unnecessary even under existing circumstances ; it can
be proved to be utterly unnecessary after all our
promised public economies are introduced, and, finally,
it can be demonstrated to be a deadly injury to our
financial connection with the British capitalist. The cry
at the next elections should be, Down with the Income
Tax!’
If the above represents the sense of the banking
community, then it is a dangerous thing for
Australia to leave large financial institutions
in the hands of men who are so shamefully and
childishly ignorant, and so wretchedly narrow,
mean and unpatriotic. Victoria has had one
surplus. It would take nearly three such sur-
pluses to pay off last year’s shortage, but the
bankers, evidently, don’t care for that. It would
take nearly 20 such surpluses to pay off the
accumulated deficit, but the unpatriotic bankers
plainly don t care for that either. The surplus
was all made by starving the railway repairs, and
another year or two of such economy will leave
the country with practically no railways at all,
but the bankers, apparently, don’t care for that
any more than for the other things. There are
■£4,000,000 of unproductive debt to be paid off and
£50,000,000 of total debt, but the bankers care as
little for that as for the rest —all they want is to
reduce their own share of taxation. And they lie
magnificently to bring this about. It is a lie that
Victoria “ gasps under all the taxes invented by
the ingenuity of worn-out old civilisations racked
by terrific wars.” Victoria is almost the only
country in the world where all the cities escape
land-tax; in Victoria £12,000,000 worth of land
is taxed _ and £100,000,000 worth escapes.
Victoria s income-tax even now is (averaging the
rates charged on different classes of incomes) not
much more than half that of Britain. (When the
Boer war was over Britain’s first care was to re-
sume its payments to the sinking fund for the
abolition of the National Debt, and the miserable
cowardice which makes the reduction of taxes the
first consideration wasn’t even dreamt of in tlie
Cold Country.) Victoria pays no house-tax ; and
it pays about one-quarter the average rate of
municipal taxation levied in England. As for the
theory that Victoria—lightly-taxed Victoria—has a
special right to expect light taxation because it has
no war debt, that is either a direct falsehood
or a dreadful example of the bankers’ ignorance.
In the main these war-debts are a handy myth.
The following table shows, as closely as possible,
the debts of a number of countries when the value
of State railways is deducted from, the liability:
Debt per Inhabitant Not Covered by Value of State-
owned Railways.
Victoria .. .. £lO 10 0 Roumania £4 4 0
Europe in general 10 2 0 Servia .. .! 310 0
Turkey .. .. 810 0 Russia .. ..300
Austria .. .. 80 0 Germany .. 2 6 0
United States .. 518 0 Bulgaria . 10 0
Belgium .. .. 614 0 Denmark .. " Nil
Switzerland .. 4 6 0 Sweden & Norway Nil
Most of these countries have telegraphs, public
estate or other assets to set against the above
debt, and quite a number of them have more
to show in that way than Victoria has. And when
an accumulated deficit of £2 per inhabitant
is added to the Victorian debt, the less its
bankers say about other country’s war-liabilities
the better; Tory misgovernment is a vast amount
more ruinous, in many cases, than war. With
Victoria in this condition—its huge unproduc-
tive debt, its enormous accumulated deficit, its
hideously decayed railways, its utter lack of a
sinking fund, and its very low taxation—the con
duct of its banking crowd in demanding less
taxation and a general letting of the country slide
is a shocking evidence of lack of patriotism. That
habit of preaching the doctrine of cowardice ami
helplessness, and promulgating the lie that we
are a specially taxed, burdened, crushed, ami
down-trodden people, is the worst service it*
press has done to Australia, and the Bankers
Magazine has got the disease badly.


The Australian Defence (Land) Bill.
Compared with the egregious folly of the proposed
system of Australian naval defence, the military
scheme suggested by the Defence Bill is a glorious
example of common sense. Examined strictly on
its own merits, however, it is found to be infected
a good deal with Forrest’s frill and Hutton’s
contempt for civil rights. The Bill has some good
points and some very bad ones. Among the good
points are provisions for the promotion of rankers
as officers, for the conscription of the whole male
population in case of need, and for punishing
any person who, by wearing the uniform of the
Defence Force, brings it into contempt. This
last clause will, if administered properly, operate
rather drastically on the “ officers and gentlemen ”
—some of whom are members of Parliament—
whose military enthusiasm is confined to strutting
about in a popinjay uniform. It is not likely that
General Hutton will administer it in that spirit,
but the clause is a handy thing to have about the
house for future use. Objectionable features of
the Bill are that it leaves so much to be fixed by
regulation; that it empowers the Governor-
General to force members of the Permanent
Forces (though not of the Citizen Forces)
to serve outside the Commonwealth (this
should be fought letter by letter; Australia
should never recruit a single man for compulsory
service abroad); and that it gives the military
authority in peace time an extreme degree of
power over railways, ferries, punts, water and
road ways, etc. The billeting of troops on private
persons is authorised even in peace time ; and that
clause should come out. Sea or road traffic may
be stopped anywhere to allow of military practice;
that needs to be very largely modified, though
some power of securing manoeuvring and heavy-
gun practice facilities is, of course, needed. But
it is in regard to courts-martial that the Bill is
most arbitrary. In addition to a general clause
giving such courts all the powers and privileges
attaching to British courts-martial, there are
these provisions:—
85. Any person who wilfully interrupts or disturbs the
proceedings of a court-martial, or uses insulting language
towards the court or the members thereof, or who by
writing or speech uses words calculated to improperly
influence the court or the members thereof or the wit-
nesses before the court, or to bring the court into dis-
repute, shall be guilty of contempt of court, whether the
act committed was committed in the court or outside the
court.
94. No proceedings of any court-martial constituted or
appointed under this Act shall be set aside or deemed
void for want of form, or be removed by certiorari or
otherwise into any civil court.
Under clause 85 it would be possible for a court-
martial to summarily convict and punish a civilian
(to the extent of three months 1 gaol) for a real or
fancied offence. A newspaper editor speaking
disrespectfully of General Hutton’s rooster-
feathers orhiscuss-words(which, compared with the
artistic creations of our own bullockies, are poor,
tame and trivial) might be sent to gaol for “ using
insulting language ” about him as a member of a
court martial. And there could be no appeal to a
civil court; so Clause 94 says. It would be an
exceedingly dangerous experiment to invest
courts-martial with summary and unappealable
jurisdiction over civilians in times of peace, and
that section of the Bill should be very thoroughly
amended. In peace time the court martial is only
needed as a tribunal for trying the military
offences of military people; it should have no
■jurisdiction over civilians and its decisions should
be in all cases liable to review before a civil court
by mandamus or prohibition. Australia wants to
set no man in a gaudy coat with braid trimmings
above or below the civil law of the land.

A Husband at Any Cost.
At Melb. General Sessions a few days ago, his
Honor dealt tenderly with the case of a matured
spinster who married against the wishes of her
parents, and took to forgery to support herself in
her new and blessed state. Up to the time when
matrimony wrecked her character, Kate Kinder
was a harmless, respectable schoolmistress, with
no more idea of forgery than a babe unborn. She
didn’t know much about it after marriage, but
her knowledge sufficed. The bridegroom she had
taken to her hungry bosom needed a new ng-out,
so Kate, at his instigation, signed the name of a
parish priest to sundry cheques, and bought
pants, shirts, boots, etc., for her darling with the
risky documents. Her darling seemingly con-
tributed no means of support to their honeymoon
existence. He just lived on his wife until she fell
into the care of the police, and then he left her
to her fate in a delicate condition. All
that Kate Kinder seems likely to get out of
matrimony is a brief experience of dubious hap-
piness, a stigma of crime and an offspring—
possibly twins. A multitude of other Kates in
this land of declining marriage rate are ripe and
ready for a similar foolishness. The Law, which
talks casually of taxing bachelors who have
arrived at a time of life when they can’t be easily
drawn into a prudent alliance, might try to devise
some means of protection for the no-longer-young
female whose natural desire for marriage has gone
too far to be reasoned with. It is one of the con-
ventional lies of our civilisation that virtuous un-
married Kates are never old enough to know
better, as regards matters amorous, until they
have arrived at an age when their better judg-
ment is almost reduced to ashes by a consuming
fire. Respectability in a spinster gains no wisdom
as it matures. Her only hope of safety from the
attractions of a bad marriage, Should the chance
offer, lies in a little disrespectability meanwhile.

State “Eights.”
The State Rights craze rises again (S.M. Herald
leader, 9/7/’O3) :
The question whether the site for the Federal capital
is to be summarily acquired by the Commonwealth with-
out reference to the wishes or intentions of New fjouth
Wales, has been recently decided by Mr. Lyne, and the
Melbourne Argus has just added its endorsement. This
State is apparently to have no word in the choice of a site
if Federal Ministers and Federal critics can help it. In a
leader last week we urged that the Constitution gives no
power to the Commonwealth to lay urgent hands upon
any site it may desire, and that within reasonable limits
New South Wales has an undoubted right to say what
portion of her territory will be handed over. Otherwise
we should be without a remedy if it was determined to
establish the Federal capital, say, at Wentworth. The
Melbourne people are, perhaps, prepared to push argu-
ment to extremes with something of the sort in view ;
but in this section of the Commonwealth any effort to
settle so vital a question on the stand-and-deliver prin-
ciple will be resisted to the uttermost.
And the Herald, which is a reasonably moral
paper on the whole, probably quite • believes this
outrageously silly trash. As matters stand, New
South Wales has a larger voice in the selection of
the capital of all Australia than any other State
possesses, because it has the largest representa-
tion in the Australian Parliament and the largest
share of representation in the Australian Min-
istry. But, in addition to this, the State Rights party
wants the State Government of N.S.W. to have a
decisive voice while the other State Governments
have none, though the capital is to be as much their
capital as that of N.S.W. —otherwise “we should
be without a remedy if it was proposed to fix the
capital at, say, Wentworth.” The fact of the other
States being without a remedy under the same
painful conditions apparently doesn’t matter,
though it would be quite as horrible for a
Westralian to legislate at Wentworth as for a
N.S.W. man to do the same thing. N.S.W.
possesses a peculiarly silly parochial party, which
always assumes that the State’s 32 Federal
members are foreign enemies, and that, unless
John See’s local Ministry is allowed to put its
meddlesome local finger into purely Federal
concerns, N.S.W. is wholly unrepresented. Yet,
as a matter of obvious fact, the State Government
of N.S.W. has no more right to meddle with the
selection of the Australian capital than has the
State Government of Tasmania. If it is a griev-
ance that a capital and a Federal territory should
be cut out of N.S.W. without the purely State
Government of N.S.W. being consulted as to
details, then N.S.W. shouldn’t have brought the
grievance on itself by demanding that that capital
and that territory should be carved out of its
State. It can’t turn its own greed into its own
grievance. If the Australian Parliament does its
duty, it will decide solely on its own responsibility
to the place and size of the Federal territory,
and take that area and no other. The desire of
mere State politicians to meddle in the decision of
purely Federal affairs is a species of parochial
bumptiousness that can’t be too definitely re-
pressed.

Fitzroy Dock.
The report of the Royal Commission upon the
Fitzroy Dock (Sydney) is a dismal commentary on
the evils of Owe’Sullivan devices. This dock is a
national property —a * ‘ permanent and reproduc-
tive work.” In order to give the principle of
State ownership a fair chance —to keep it from
being dragged in the gutter of failure and dis-
repute—it was essential that the Dock should be
worked on honest business lines. The State paid
fair wages, and it was the State’s business to get
fair value for these wages. It didn’t want the
Dock to earn 10 per cent, dividends, but it did
want it to pay interest on the cost of construe-'
tion, cost of maintenance, and something over
to extinguish the debt upon it. There is
nothing in .any scheme of State socialism about
making any public work a charitable institution,
a home for the aged and incapable, a refuge for the
friends of politicians, and a place where unem-
ployed are pensioned off and stand around draw-
ing wages, but doing no work because there is none
for them to do. And the Fitzroy Dock has been
all these things under the despicable regime of
ward politician O’Sullivan. The superintendent
seems to have had no real authority, as to dis-
missal, as to rates of pay, as to sick leave, or as to
allowances. The finger of the politician was
always in the pie. One young man, it was alleged,
could never be sacked “so long as Mr. Brunker
was alive” (presumably the saintly cow-merchant
from the Hunter River whose halo of white hair
made godly the Reid Ministry). It was quite a
common thing for a man on being sacked to “see
a member” and be reinstated, or for an employee
to sit on . the doormat of the Minister until
tvt a r * se one shilling a week in wages.
Nobody could be a better friend to private enter-
pnse and. private boodling than the corrupt gang
of politicians who thus bring State enterprise into
contempt. Moreover, there is another aspect of
the case. New South Wales pays its Ministers to
look after large affairs of State, and the Minister
who is always grubbing in the mire of small details,
doing office-boy’s work, interviewing Brown, who
wants 2d. more wages, and Jones, _who thinks
he has been done out of has no time for large
affairs of State. He isn’t a Minister in the real
sense of the term —merely an opener of bridges
and tea-meetings, a babbler about small details, a
rouseabout, odd jobber and busybody, a.fetcher
and carrier, a pottering combination of
curate, old woman, small, fussy benefactor
and soup kitchen. The making of a Greater
Sydney, the establishment of local govern-
ment throughout the State, the restoration
of the finances, the graduated land-tax and absen-
tee tax, the State Bank problem—all these and a
dozen other large questions are hung up year after
year while the Ministry’s time is filled with
wrestlings over the claim of Patrick Hoolan to
Id. an hour “dirt money.” New South Wales
needs a Ministry that has time to deal with larger
questions than “ dirt-money.”
