The Socialistic Victorian Farmer.
The very last resolution carried by the recent
Farmers’ Convention at Colac (Vic.) was to the
effect that “ as the permanent settlement of the
farmers of the northern area of the State depends
on the utilisation of the Murray and other rivers,
the Premier be urged to bring before Parliament
without delay a Bill authorising a loan to carry
out a comprehensive scheme of water conservation
and irrigation.” This resolution of the begging
farmers—the men who had just applauded
Irvine’s windy declamations against Socialism,
and who have helped to plunder the civil servant
and sweat the city worker —is really a matchless
piece of impudence. The resolution was moved
by one Bedwell, of the Murray Valley Irrigation
Trust, and supported by Geo. Russell, of
Numurkah, Pagan, of Tatura, and Morton, of
Kerang. The Bulletin gives these names because
it is significant that the Irrigation and Water-
works Trusts they represent are just those
that most shamelessly swindled the State Trea-
sury when the Turner-Peacock Government, in
’99, relieved the Irrigation Trusts of their liabili-
ties and passed them on to the shoulders of the
city workers and posterity. At that date
Tatura, Numurkah, Shepparton and Trago,-
wel Plains owed £509,639 besides interest,
of which they calmly passed on to the
State no less than £502,048. Tragowel,
which lies between Kerang and the Murray,
was the c(joicest .specimen of lazy incompetence
or rascally repudiation of the whole lot; it owed,
at June 30th, ’99, =6158,418 of capital and £80,141
of interest, and repudiated every penny of interest
owing and £123,418 of the capital borrowed.
That is the record of the bodies which, after
barracking for private enterprise and deriding
Socialism, coolly ask the State to borrow several
millions to water their private lands and pass the
bill on to the rest of the community, or posterity,
or any body it likes —their troubles! And not
only did they never repay the money they
borrowed or a cent of interest thereon,
but, though the value of their holdings
was increased three and even five-fold, they
seldom, if ever, raised the municipal valuations
(Rodney, for instance, was loaned £282,500, and its
valuation grew from £44,182 in ’B7 to but £53,399
in ’01). They kept their rates down to the lowest
possible figure that would serve to keep the bailiff
out (Rodney, Numurkah, and the rest still pay a
Is. rate, while, in the city, Footscray and Bruns-
wick pay 2s. 6d. and 2s. 4d. respectively). At
the same conference these beautiful farmers—who
preach individualism and practise Socialism—had
been listening to addresses, and presumably
assimilating knowledge, from agricultural experts
paid by the State, one of whom gave them
valuable information obtained by him from ex-
perimental plots worked and tested at great
expense by the State, while another explained
how the profitable butter industry, started and
nursed by the State, could be further improved
and expanded, if only they would learn the
lessons the State was trying to teach. Just here
it is pertinent to remark that while the starting
and expanding of the butter export trade has put
thousands of pounds into the pockets of the Vic-
torian farmer it has not made butter any cheaper
to the city worker. On the contrary, the increasing
regularity of export tends to keep the price up.
And these f ar.mers ask that millions more be spent
on them ! (The Waranga Basin scheme now in
progress will run to nearly a million before it is
finished.) Does the State provide experts to teach
the city factory hands ? Does it run experimental
factories? Has it given bonuses for manufac-
tures? (It offered one for beet sugar at Maffra,
and the fat stock-raisers were too lazy to grow
the beet.) Does it subsidise the city munici-
palities? Does it carry manufactured goods at
unpayable rates? Does it give tools free, as it
grants seed wheat and rabbit-proof fencing ? The
fact is that the farmer is a good deal more of a
socialist, in that he leans on the State for aid in
his industry, than any other class of worker in
Victoria. And he is more than a socialist. In
addition to his other qualities, he has the assur-
ance to regard the fact that he repudiated previous
loans as a reason why he should be trusted with
more money.
A Presumption of Law.
Nobody, perhaps, whose sympathy is worth
having has any for Grand and Jones, who went
to the gallows at Darlinghurst last week.
That, however, does not affect the rotten-
ness of the presumption of law which hanged
them, and hanged them in spite of the jury|s
recommendation to mercy. This presumption is
that, when two or more men are engaged in an un-
lawful act, and murder is committed, all are
guilty of murder. This matter was warmly
debated once before in New South Wales, both in-
side and outside of Parliament, in connection
with the crime for which Scott and Rogan were
hanged in 1880. Parkes, at that time Premier,
then said, summing up the whole position—“ The
report that this man or that man fired the fatal
shot is a quibble—they all fired the fatal shot.”
And that was worse than a quibble—it was a
lie. See how it works out. Grand and and
very -likely a third man went to a hotel to
do a “ job ” for which they deserved, say, seven
years. If they had been caught without shooting
anybody, that, no doubt, is about what they
would have got. But ®ne of them —it may not
have been either Grand or Jones, and it certainly
was not both —usjed a weapon. There is no proof
that the man who did not shoot approved of or
assented to the shooting; those who did not shoot
were, possibly, ignorant that there was a weapon
in the crowd. Nor has the fact that the men were
there for an unlawful purpose any real bearing on
the case. They were there for one unlawful pur-
pose, but not for another; and it is a most
unwarrantable assumption that because they were
both willing to commit one crime they were both
willing to commit any and every other in the
calendar. It is as unwarrantable as it would
be to assume that because a man is will-
ing to do one [good deed he is willing
to do every other that could be thought of.
A crowd might rush down to the end of a
pier to help a man out of a raging sea; but it
would not follow that the whole crowd was
capable of jumping into the water, and risking
its life, because one single member of the crowd
did so ; and if anybody proposed to reward the
whole crowd with Humane Society medals for
saving life, there would be a loud guffaw, and yet
no louder a guffaw than the circumstances of the
case would call for. Again, when a conspicuous
act of bravery is performed in war, the whole
army isn’t held responsible and awarded the
Victoria Cross, notwithstanding that the whole
army is supposed to be capable of doing brave
things. It is supposed to be capable of doing
some brave things, that is to say; but if one man
does a thing out of the ordinary programme, he
personally, and not the whole army, is rewarded ;
and, if he can’t be found, nobody is rewarded.
Yet when, in other circumstances, one man does
a thing which isn’t in the programme—does a
thing of whioh, possibly, the Others are not
capable— and when that thing happens to be a
bad thing, all are punished; and that is exactly
as unfair and absurd as the other would be. It
was on this perfectly sound ground that the jury
recommended these two men, G-rand and Jones,
to mercy; but as that recommendation amounted
to a reflection upon a presumption of law of great
antiquity, it was disregarded. There is, therefore,
only one course open. If the people cannot re-
flect hard enough upon an absurd and unfair pre-
sumption of law through the jury, they must work
through their Parliament, whose views cannot be
flouted as those of a jury so often are.
♦
Sharks,
The ravings of one A. St. Ledger at the meeting
held in Brisbane to form a “national defence
fund ” for the purpose of downing Laborites and
supporters of Barton and Kingston at the
Federal elections: —
The Federal Parliament had out-Heroded Herod by
proclaiming to the world, both by deliberate legislation,
and still more deliberate administration, that white men
of our own race and blood were social lepers, whose pre-
sence should not be endured on our shores, and should
not be permitted to contaminate our people. It has told
the world that the British artisan was a species of pariah,
that his labor and his industry were a menace to Aus-
tralia and his fellow-workman, and that no employer
should be permitted to engage from outside us even a
unionist artisan at unionist wages, unless an Australian
trades union shall certify that such labor cannot be ob-
tained amongst us.
No, St. Ledger or St. Cash-book, or whatever it
is; the Federal Parliament hasn’t quite tpld the
world that. What it has told the world is that
there are sharks in Australia who don’t care a
straw how they get rich so long as they do get
rich. Some of them s have already got rich by
swindling the Customs, others by promoting bogus
banks, others by importing cheap colored labor
while white men of their own race were near
starving for want of work. And the Fede-
ral Parliament, which knows these sharks,
has told the world that, if they could
make sixpence out of it, they wouldn’t hesitate to
import British artisans under bond to serve them
at wages out of all proportion to the cost of living
here. The Federal Parliament hasn’t cast any
reflection on the British artisan. All it has said
is that there are sharks in Australia, and that it
thinks too well of the British artisan to permit
him to come here bound so that the sharks may
have him at their mercy, and the sharks don’t like
it, and that is what all the trouble is about. Lamb
liked babies—boiled. The Australian shark loves
the British artisan —trussed.
When Wilkinson, M.P., moved a resolution in
the House lately in favor of a bonus on ginned
cotton, his proposal was jumped on heavily by
many farmers’ representatives. These individuals
have doubtless been told that Protection doesn’t
benefit the farmer, and have accepted the yarn as
gospel. Some day, perhaps, they will learn
better. If the Australian farmer sends to Parlia-
ment none but solidly Protectionist (high tariff,
not revenue tariff) representatives, there will be
some hope of Parliament giving a bonus for the
ginning of cotton, and that should mean, in a few
years, a thousand or so workers growing cotton
and ten thousand more manufacturing it—even as
is done in Manchester to-day—and every one of
these gould support a wife and family who could
pay the farmer for his wheat. And this applies
not to cotton only ; it is so with the great iron and
steel industry, the ship-building business, the
linen and woollen industries, and many others.
All the people employed in these industries will
buy the farmer’s produce here, giving him full
value for it. If Australia doesn’t establish manu-
factures on a large scale, then there won’t be
sufficient population here to buy all, or nearly all,
the farmer’s produce, and he must send a vast
quantity to England. Then he doesn’t get full
price; he gets the price less freight for 16,000
miles, insurance, exchange, and the charges of
half-a-dozen middlemen —also, the price he gets
is the lowest price at which his cheapest nigger or
other competitor can supply at. If the farmer
doesn’t want Protection, it simply means that he
doesn’t want customers. >
Sydney Daily Telegbaph finds with pained sur-
prise that foreigners own most of the fruit shops
and nearly all the fish shops in the city; and it
quotes the officer in charge of the Department of
Labor:—
The boys [employed at these shops] come to this coun-
try under some form of indenture which appears to place
them practically in servitude to their masters. In some
cases the inspectors have found lads worked up to from
90 to 100 hours per week. To protect them effectually is
almost impossible. In abject fear of their masters, they
refuse to give information, or, if put in the witness-box,
will commit perjury rather than betray them.
But what about it ? ■ The Telegbaph doesn’t pro-
pose to interfere with that, does it? The very
issue in which the report is made denounces the
Government half-a-dozen times over for “inter-
fering with the capitalist,” and “ destroying free-
dom of action on the part of the employer.”
Ip we must hang people the least we might do is
to carry out; the loathsome business on some
scientific principles. It seems that the JST.S.W.
hangman, who presumably is not a scientist, fixes
the length of drop and the other ghastly details
“on some principles of his own;” and he fixes
them so badly that, pretty often, instead
of instantaneous death coming to his miserable
victim, the poor wretch dangles, still living, at
the end of the rope for five, 10, and even 15
minutes. Three of the Mount Rennie boys were
slowly strangled in this way. Williams (of
Williams and Montgomeby) was suffocated after
one of the most shocking exhibitions ever witnessed
at a gallows; and now Gband has writhed under
the drop for upwards of five minutes, eventually
dying, not the swift death that follows dislocation
of the neck, but the slow death that comes from
suffocation. When a£s culvert is to be built,
the garden variety of workman isn’t trusted to do
the job unaided ; the trained, scientific, profes*
Ham upon the File.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
President Roosevelt has sent a message to the Ozar
imploring him to protect the Jews from further outrage.
A negro was burnt, last week, in Alabama, by a lynch-
ing mob.
—Recent items m the press news.
“ Don’t persecute the Hebrews! ” said the Yankees
to the Czar —
They’d just anointed Sambo with a bucketful of
tar;
They’d just saluted Sambo with a load of language
vile,
And escorted him in fury to a savage-looking
pile.
“ Don’t raid the sons of Jacob in the Ghetto and
the den”—
The Yankee wrote the message with a diplomatic
pen,—
The curling smoke kept rising in the distance all
the while
From the bed of luckless Sambo—as he perished
on the pile.
“ Remember we are Human all ” —the Yankee
wrote again—
“ The Jew is Man and Brother —though he loves
another fane.”
The Yankee’s other brother —you could hear him
near a mile
As he called aloud for mercy to the wolves about
the pile.
“ You hurt the finer feelings of the Syndicate and
Trust
Whene’er your people ravage in the ways of Loot
and Lust! ”
The Yankee’s finer feelings—they were holding
forth in style
On the blessed Reformation of a Race upon the
pile.
“The Land of Wooden Nutmegs begs to tell the
Russian Bear—
’Tis wrong to harry mortals as a hound would
hunt a hare.”
But the Bear he sniffed and snorted —then he
muttered with a smile—
“ls the Yankee basting beef , or is it Ham upon the
pile?”
“ The woes of Russian Hebrews make the Yankee
nation sob,
So kindly curb the fury of your fierce Slavonic
mob.”
“By yonder shrieks,” replied the Bear—“ I guess
the versatile
American is sobbing out his soul about a pile ! ”
“I know,” the Bear continued, with a kind of
surly growl,
“The Slav’s a slinking forest-wolf whene’er he’s
on the prowl;
But, if ye want a tiger full of cruel hate and
guile
Commend me to a Yankee man who yells around
a pile! ”

When Hall Caine dies and his spook comes
to stand behind the pearly spikes to answer for
sins committed in the body, what plea shall he put
extenuation of having written the
Christian ? Of course, no plea will avail him
tor having created a John Storm and a Holv
Gethsemane, so he will probably be fastened down
in Hades with a Manx cat on his chest and an
Hye, wearing the wild expression that John Storm
Used to preach with, fixed upon him forever. Then
when Bernard Espinasse and J. C. Williamson
give up their ghosts, will they be judged guilty as
accessories after the act? “It is a great moral
question,” as the darkey, engaged in rob-
bmg a hen-roost, replied, when asked by
another Sambo if it were a sin to steal'
chickens, “and dis am no place to argue
I*- lh .e “Christian” spent Thursday and
f rid ay nights on the stage of Sydney Royal A
longer visit would have paganised the city. John
Trvn'rr s , cre . ed ls neither fish, flesh, fowl, nor good
Hill-Catholic. He is a sort of Mohammedan
Baptist. Bat there is something of the early
Christian martyr about him—the “martyr” who
funked at sight of the lions and asked the Roman
guard the nearest way to the temple of Apollo (or
any other old thing in the idol line), and then
funked at sight of the idol and decided to go
back and have another look at the lions. Cuyler
Hastings, as John Storm, made up to look the
exalted and exulting fanatic to the life, but all his
art couldn’t bring the character into sympathetic
touch with the audience. Glory Quayle, that
cross between a dairymaid and a “tart,” was
capitally played by Miss Chevalier. Also, Athol-
wood, as Canon Wealthy, was the sour and gold-
plated churchman to the last button—the one
that was put in the plate.

Ihe humans, as distinct from the rodents
in “Mice and Men,” which opened the Musgrove
season last Saturday at Sydney Royal, are a
vastly polite crowd. They “vow and protest,”
and one is so ungenteel that she “positively does
;not know how to swoon.” A considerable part of
Act 1 goes about as smartly as a centipede wearing
heavy boots on all his feet. Talk, talk, smothered
mouthing, or gabbling—the elocution of the
majority of the men has not the charm and
carrying quality of the squeaking of the mice.
When Nellie Stewart comes in, the centipede
takes off his boots, and doesn’t resume them
save for some short intervals, and the play runs
brightly after that. Nellie, as the foundling
wench named by the parish, “Little Britain,’’
really looks the 10-year-old she is supposed to be
She is gawky, all hands and feet and raised
shoulders, but after two years of adoption by a
philosopher and scientist she re-appears a charm-
lnff Eaisy Dimple person, with nothing of her
early hoydenishness remaining save a tendency to
run abo lit the lawn barefoot. On Saturday night
JNelhes feet were immediately put under the
lens Everyone who had a glass levelled it: those
people wno had no glasses borrowed, or snatched
glasses from people who themselves wanted to
inspect Nellie s feet, and who were enraged at the
deprivation. There is nothing to criticise in those
supports, save that there is too much make-up on
the toes. So Nellie looks as if she had bad chil-
blains.

Tears still flow at Sydney Criterion, where
Miss Lashbrook completes a pathetic little storv
by dying nightly at five minutes past 11 o’clock,
and where Miss Beatrice Holloway is given away
every 24 hours to an astonished burglar, who
receives her with gratitudeand thegreatestsurprise
of his life. It is only once in a long while that an
actress comes along young and strong enough to
play the chubby hero in “Two Little Vagabonds” ;
and if the burglar had any idea how rare is the
actress who can fill the bill as well as Miss
Hollowa,y does it, he would want a lot more than
£lOO to release her. The only remark which
seems called for, m addition to those made last
week, is that J. Hesford, who is Dido Bunce, the
foolish burglar’s mate, affects a most artistio
stammer. There has been an epidemic of stutter-
ing on Sydney stages during the last few weeks,
(generally this stage stutter has proved to be the
last resort of a play-writer bankrupt of wit, and
the poor little trick has deserved nothing but
silent contempt or a brick; but, anyhow, Hesford
makes the best of a poor job. The piece, which
has been drawing good houses, will run another
week.

** * *
The movement to work up a big testi-
momal benefit to that good and popular old-time
manager and scenic artist, Mr. W. J. Wilson
grows rapidly. All the companies at present in
Sydney have promised their assistance, and the
affair will be under the patronage of Governor
and Lady Rawson, also it will be aided and
beamed upon by all that is worth mentioning in
soma! and political circles. Miss Essie Jenyns
(Mrs. Wood), whose Rosalind and many other
characters gladdened the tail-end of last century,
is among the cordial supporters of the movement,
and it has the approval of Dan O’Connor.
#* * #
At Sydney Tivoli Rudinoff shows what a
versatile amuser he is by singing in a fine baritone
voice—suggesting that of the late lamented
Charles Ryley, with a tinge of Brownlow in the
low notes. His whistling continues to be admir-
able and his smoke pictures wonderful, but a
lightning sketch of Sydney Harbor which he
attempts is emphatically not a success. It
suggests the subject neither in form nor in color.
Miss Maud Perman and the two Colliers, new
arrivals, add some brightness to the Tivoli enter-
tainment.

Melbourne rolled up mulfcitudinously to
■^ ei L.-^Ai es^y,s Saturday to take the measure
of J.C.W.’s musical comedy co. in the opening
of a six weeks’season with “Belle of New York.”
!nu 0 verdlct was nofc ver J pronounced either way.
There are numerous distinctions between “The
Belle’’and the orthodox musical comedy, which
confines itself to two set scenes and avoids all ap-
Searance of endeavoring to take itself seriously.
lungs are different in “The Belle.” The story
has various moods and several scenes, and the two
principal girls are understood to choke with
emotion on acoount of a contrite young man
who works out his salvation at a candy
store. They are strictly impossible girls—one
of them starts her stage life as the centre orna-
ment in a wedding cake, and the other plays a
lone hand with a Salvarmy tambourine—yet they
aspire to realism as regards Ichabod Bronson’s
prodigal son. Also, the young man is supposed to
feel deeply for both of them. Ichabod, his parent,
likewise has oases of pensiveness in his desert of
frivolity, and a Polite Lunatic, who ambles in and
out of the narrative seeking the Bronson he would
destroy, is another candidate for sympathy when
he puts his hand to his addled head and dimly
remembersthat hedoesn’t know what he’sthinking
about. “ The Belle of New York’’ bristles with
peculiarities, amusing and otherwise. A pair of puer-
ile Portuguese twins are quite foreign to Australian
notions of humor. A stereotyped tragedian,
named Mugg, might drop out of the performance
and never be missed, which remark applies to an
irrelevant Kissy Fitzgarter. The loss of even
Blinky Bill and his donah could be borne without
a pang of regret. But the music of “ The Belle ”
is sweet and lively, and the story contains a kernel
of human kindness, and the fact that two or three
of the characters have become classics in musical
comedy literature must be counted for virtue in
the author’s work. Ichabod Bronson and the
Lunatic are creations. The girl in the poke
bonnet is a personage. She was the first female to
shed a pure serene light upon the eternal hug—in
comic opera.
** * *
The best-selected company in the world
c ° u ld give a more or less delightful performance
of “Belle of New York.” Each of the principal
parts is an elastic opportunity for the artist who
plays it. The co. came to Melbourne as strangers,
but in its collective form it struck the present
writer on his bump of disappointment. Miss Rose
Musgrove didn’t rise to expectations. Fred
Graham’s sketch of Bronson, sen., looks like a
small and labored copy of a previous Ichabod
drawn from other people’s memories. Arthur
Crane is a pleasant Bronson, jun., with a good
tenor-baritone voice ; and Miss Lilian Digges is a
greatly improved edition of Maud Chetwynd. She
has a dainty way, a note of feeling in her voice, and
the promise of doing betterpresently. Missßlanche
Wallace, although she can’t sing or dance to speak
u 18 <f compensation for the awfulness of
the “Queen of Comic Opera,” whom Melbourne
suffered aforetime. Miss Olive Lenton threatens
to be rather charming in a more congenial charac-
ter.™ an Blinky’s donah, and the other people are
satisfactory, with one or two exceptions. The
good-looking chorus might show further intelli-
gence and vivacity. On Saturday it was a chorus
that often shaped itself like a horse-shoe on the
outskirts of the entertainment and wearily
■wagged its head when the conductor shook a stick
at it. One gets so tired of the chorus that wags
and nods its head.
** * *
At Melbourne Princess’s six nights and
one matinee of “The Fortune Teller” are
keeping Musgrove’s Opera Co. fully employed
until Friday. The strong points of “The For-
tune Teller ” obtrude themselves as before, also
the weak points appear again, and the general
gorgeousness has lost a little glitter since the first
time Melbourne heard the big brown Sandor sing
to his little gipsy sweetheart. All the same the
alleged Hungarian opera offers much splendor for
the money, and the music is rather impressive,
and the difficulty in discovering which of two
girls is being impersonated by Madame Slapoffski
at any given period of the enigma provides the
public with healthful mental exercise. No other
comic opera put such a severe strain upon popular
intelligence. Amidst the tumult and stress of the
grand finale to the second act it is hard for any
ordinary person to remember that one Madame
Slapoffski is being mistaken for two distinct girls
and a twin brother simultaneously. Next Satur-
day (18th) brings “ The Serenade ” to light for the
first time in Australia. “The Serenade” is a
three-act opera by the musical author of “ The
Fortune Teller,” and the characters have rich
Spanish names, which occasionally begin with Y
and frequently end with Z.
The Westminster Abbey Glee Party’s first
Melbourne concert came more than up to expec-
tations. Really choice concerted singing, blending
smoothly in a delicate vocal mosaic, is none too com-
mon in a country where choirs and choral societies
are apt to impress by loudness rather than quality.
The “Abbey” warblers, numbering: ten only (five
men and five boys), supply enough sound to satisfy
a Town Hall audience, and better sound, at that,
than the ordinary choral bulk-harmony. Avoiding
the showy on the one hand and the mean on the
other, the choristers interpret in excellent style
ancient and modern English glees and ballads,
sound words set to staunch music. Their words
are distinct, their intonations true, and, in its own
way, one could scarcely hear anything more
pleasing than their “St. Michael’s Bells,”
the boys’ fresh voices accounting for the little
tinkling bells and the men attending to the
booming of the big ones. The five trebles’
treatment of “It was a Lover and his Lass”
came as a blessed relief after the cruel wrong that
Shakspearian doggrel suffered at the accents of
two small Austryiians, in recent production of
“As You Like It. ” As soloists, the members of
the Glee party are good enough, but not astound-
ing. Little Leonard Hubbard lilts Cowen’s
“Swallows” in tones clear and fresh as those of a
thrush. Director Branscombe, on Saturday,
made every word tell in a quaint old ballad’

At Melb. Opera House, the Boissetts are
perpetrating their third pantomime—“ The Even-
ing Party”—which will bring them to a conclu-
sion on the 23rd inst., unless they revert to a
previous panto, for their farewell frivols. Two
new turns _ at the Rickards house are Lilian
-Bishop, a lively stranger with a pair of effective
legs, and Dan Crawley, who is said to imitate a
certain Dan. Leno, the idol of the London’alls,
h or Australian purposes Crawley promises to be
about as serviceable as Tom Wootwell, for if his
songs are not better than the average red-nosed
ditty, Daniel’s way of talking them is quaint and
cheerful.
#* * *
The “ Midsummer Night ” has brought all
Adelaide to the Royal, the pleasure of seeing a
variety entertainment, and, at the same time,
getting credit for patronising Shakspeare, being
the most subtle of all baits for the public. The
wonderfully built-up woodland banks of scenery,
the Mendelssohn music, the fairy spectacle, the
knock-about business and rollicking farce, all these
things combine to make an irresistible show, and
business has boomed accordingly. Denny as
Bottom, and Dorothy Marsden as Puck, perhaps
take chief honors (after the stage-manager), but
there is a solid all-round level of excellence in the
acting. With two small exceptions, the cast is the
same as in Melbourne. In face of this heavy
competition, the Tivoli struggles along with a
fairly good bill. Crawley, whose humor seemed
at times rather too fruity on closer acquaintance,
has passed on, and Fields of the eccentric jugglery
replaces him. Also, a very fine bass singer, in
the person of Ernest Fitts, has come into the
programme. The Branscombe Concert party
made itself extremely popular by staying to sing
at a Liedertafel “ smoker,” but it has gone at
last, angelic choristers, “society entertainer,”
and all.

The same old “ Drought is Breaking.”

The profits, though better than those for 1902, are rather
below the average of the last few years ; yet, considering
the general state of things in Queensland and Australia,
they are quite as good as could be expected. The
position compares with that of 12 months’ ago

This shows an improving business. Certainly cash and
liquid securities held have been somewhat reduced ; but
against that, there is not only the increase in ad-
vances, but the unprofitable interminable 4J per cent,
debentures have all been bought up. The cash and
liquid securities held are still equal to 33 per cent, of the
total liabilities to the public, which is a figure that few
institutions reach. In fact, if a very much larger bank
kept that much in its safe,.it would be reckoned waste-
ful. Still, more than half of what the North Queensland
has is working ; this half includes £25,750 invested in
Government stock. What with being expected to main-
tain a larger proportion of reserves than the big concern,
and what with being almost necessarily less economically
worked, the small bank cannot, however, show very
alluring profits ; on the shareholders’ funds at commence-
ment of half year (capital £lOO,OOO and reserves £15,182),
they work out at £3 14s. Bd. per cent, per annum. This
compares with past results in this way :

It is small; but a very solid proportion of it goes to re-
serves, and the customer is better secured than in many
a big bank, for the capital in proportion to the business
is much larger than the big bank’s average.
* * * *
Various British papers have warmly congratu-
lated Irvine and his Raven on the Victorian surplus—
evidently it was simply cabled to them that there was a
surplus, and in their simple British way they regarded it
as genuine. The fact that the ordinary Australian State
surplus of £150,000 is made by stealing £200,000 or
£300,000 of sinking funds and loan funds, and using them
as revenue, is evidently not yet fully realised over there.

f [Out of a sum-total of £142,000 already raised by
the Queensland Income Tax, some £40,000 has been
raised by the Poll Tax. That is to say some 80,000 poor
wretches with an income of less than £lOO (many of them
with much less than £5O) have had to contribute half-a-
sovereign apiece out of their bread-monev.
* * * “ *
Westralia is going to the bad over its metro-
politan as well as over its goldfields water-supply. Present
charges give the Perth water-supply a revenue of under
£37,000. The capital cost was £430,000 ; and 4 per cent,
interest and a sinking fund of only 1 per cent absorb
£21,500, leaving something under £15,500 to cover work-
ing costs. And working costs for the 11 months ending
May 31 amounted to £26,905. At this rate, the under-
taking isn’t really paying interest, not to mention sinking
fund. s

At the commencement of the half-year the shareholders
funds amounted to £515,754 (£456,927 capital and £58,827
reserves) ; on this the earnings were equal to £3 10s. 2d.
per cent, per annum. This compares with recent results
as follows:

Dec., 1900 .. 317 9 „ Dec., 1902 .. 3 810 „
June, 1901 .. 319 3 „ June, 1903 .. 310 2 „
Dec., 1901 .. 319 6 „
The paid-up capital continues to grow a little ; on Dec.
31 last it was £456,927, and on June 30 it was £457,354
or £427 more. This is apparently the result of the
gradual issue of the authorised preference shares.
How much of the capital is represented by preference
and how much by ordinary shares is, however, still
a mystery. But, though authority was got to raise
£300,000 by preference shares, very few appear to have
been actually issued. On the entire paid-up capital at
the commencement of the half-year £6853 would have
paid a 3 per cent, dividend. The amount actually paid,
allowing 3£ per cent, for the preference shares, is only
£3O more than this ; so preference capital issued seems to
be £12,000. It would be better to distinguish between
the two issues in the balance-sheet. Principal figures
compare as follows with those of 12 months back

This exhibits a rather serious contraction in business
generally ; nevertheless, as compared with the work of
the half-year which ended on Dec. 31 last, there is an im-
provement in respect of both advances and deposits.
Cash and liquid assets are lower than usual. Including
£59,918 Government stock on hand, they are equal to 34
per cent, of total liabilities to the public, as against 36
per cent, six months ago, and 37 per cent. 12 months ago.
It is, nevertheless, a very solid reserve still.

Every State in the Commonwealth, barring
only Tasmania, closed its financial year on June 30 ; Tas-
mania’s year ends on December 31. The full and final
figures which show the deficit, or *how the deficit has
been -dodged, are not yet available ; but there is enough
to go. on .with, and it is not likely that anything more
definite will be forthcoming for some time.
New South Wales does not pretend to have a
surplus. The position is something like this

This is not really a correct statement of the case as
regards 1903, for a large expenditure is still “in sus-
pense,” and when it is adjusted and brought into the
accounts things will be vastly worse than they look now.
If the rents for the Harbor foreshore properties (not yet
by any means all paid for) are included, the position can
be made to look a trifle better ; but, though the Treasurer
goes on including these amounts, the operation is utterly
wrong, and the effect produced by that juggle is simply
an illusion. Nevertheless it is give® here for what it is
worth

But, while the amount stated as revenue includes really
more than revenue, the amount stated as expenditure for
the past year does not include all the expenditure.
Money actually spent, but not yet formally authorised,
is not included, on the ground that until the expenditure
has been authorised it is not certain which amounts
should be charged to revenue and which to loans. If the
whole of this unauthorised expenditure were charged
to revenue, the total expenditure would amount to
£12,624,954. The total Treasury receipts, including
advances (£845,246), amounted to £12,377,379 ; so, on this
fairer basis, the deficit was £247,575, as against a deficit
of £84,595 on the same basis in the previous year. This
presentment of the case is complicated by the item of
“advances to the Treasurer”; these advances were in-
creased by £278,705 during the year. A large amount of
thisls f however, no doubt recoverable. If it is not, then
the deficit is more than £247,575, even when the whole of
the Harbor rents are improperly included as revenue. To
this cash shortage must be added the shortage of
£236,781 inherited from last year, making a present
deficiency in the cash of £484,356. And Premier
Owe’See says that things are very satisfactory indeed!
First this country had deficits, and was ashamed Of them,
and said they shouldn’t occur again. Then it had more
deficits, and, being still ashamed of them, tried to cover
them up. Now it has more deficits, and openly confesses
the fact, and, so far from being ashamed, declares that
the position is quite satisfactory. It is a shocking state
of mind for a country to drift into.
The N.S.W. position would, however, be a good
deal worse than it is but for the unexpectedly large refunds
from the Commonwealth. .In June alone, £375,514 was
received from this source, or £243,860 more than in June,
1902. For the whole year the refunds were £667,227
more than for the preceding year. It isn’t the Owe’See
Government’s fault that the deficit is not a quarter-of-a-
million more. It is even less New South Wales’ fault;
for New South Wales did a good deal more than its share
in sending a low-tariff crowd to the Australian Parlia-
ment, and in subsequently howling for the remission of
the fodder duties.
The N.S.W. loan expenditure looks fairly
respeccable compared with that of 1902. This is owing,
mostly, to the great difficulty in getting loans during the
past six months :

The position of Victoria is somewhat less de-
finitely stated; until the alleged fact that there is ai sur-
plus has soaked well into the people who will presently
be asked to advance a few miHions, there are severa l
good reasons why the position should not be> statedl de-
finitely. Taking the figures as issued by the Treasury,
the result is:—
Revenue. Expenditure.
1902 .. £7,006,333 £7,407,781 Deficit £401,448
1903 .. 6,951,980 6,798,781 Surplus 153,199
Dec. £54,353 Dec. £609,000 Gain £554,647
How much of the alleged revenue was true revenue, and
how much of it was looted from sinking funds, &c.,
cannot yet be stated. The revenue was, moreover,
increased by Commonwealth refunds £234,000 in excess
of Treasurer Turner’s estimate. So far as the depart-
ments controlled by Irvine, Bent, Shiels and Co. are con-
cerned , Victoria would thus have had a deficit of about
£Bl,OOO in spite of the manipulation of trust accounts and
of the extraordinary slaughter on the expenditure side.
As to the latter item, the chief “ savings ” have been
made by paying the railway staff short time while often
working it long time (£172,654); and by letting the
engines rust and the permanent way fall to pieces another
£111,799 has been “ saved.” The old-age pensioner’s poor
dole has been squeezed down £70,000 further ; and this
squeezing process is still going on. The other “economies”
have been effected by starving the road-vote, by spending
little or nothing on repairs, by demoralising other ser-
vices as well as the railways, and by neglecting to put in
the stitch which saves nine. To the extent, then, that
Victoria’s isn’t a fraud surplus, it is a fool surplus.
South Australia olaims a surplus, too ; this one
was acquired by hanging round the office of the trustees
in the estate of the late George Scarfe, and getting a little
on account. Still, although S.A., too, has been letting
its public works fall to pieces rather than show a deficit
by using revenue for the purpose of keeping them in
repair, S.A. has not done so badly. It has had a
good deal of luck ; it has had a better harvest than
usual, got better prices for its products, and therefore
collected mere income and land-tax. Moreover,
12 months ago S.A. did make an attempt to stop the
drift by increasing both these taxes, and the result has
been an addition of £81,541 to the revenue. The Trea-
surer estimated that he would get £65,000 from probate
and succession duties, which the previous year gave
£61,000; he actually received £104,027, ineluding £36,000
which were the fruits of the visit to Scarfe’s trustees, just
before the clock struck July 1. The total revenue for the
year was £2,472,266 against £2,428,560 in the previous
year—an increase of £43,706. For the first nine months
of the year the expenditure was £1,820,029, which was
£7185 less than three-quarters of the estimated total for
the year. It is pretty certain that the expenditure was
not largely increased during the last three months, so
that the estimate of expenditure was not exceeded.
Taking the actual revenue and the estimated expendi-
ture the position would be :
Revenue.. .. „ M £2,472,266
Expenditure .. .. _ 2,436,286
Surplus .. .. .. £35,980
Considering the unusual crop of probate and succession
duties, the general and unusual prosperity of the State,
and the neglect to keep the public works in repair (as
lately sworn to by the Engineer-in-Ohief), this isn’t much
to be proud of. Through the Broken Hill trouble S.A.’s
new financial year makes a worse start than usual; so
the outlook is not too promising. The deficit for 1902
was £222,316, so it would take near seven such surpluses
as the present one to make good that one year’s shortage.
Queensland owns the largest deficit of the
group, taking population into account
Revenue. Expenditure. Deficit.
1902 .. .. £3,535,062 £3,967,001 £431,939
1903 .. .. 3,526,465 3,717,806 191,341
Decrease .. £8,597 £249,195 £240,598
Again the situation has to some extent been saved by
Australian Ministers Kingston and Turner, who, to the
disgust of some part of Brisbane, declined to allow the
Customs to be swindled as formerly, and were, therefore,
able to return to the State £905,000, instead of £809,000
which Treasurer Turner had estimated, mainly on the
basis of previous State experiences. The income tax,
largely a poll tax, added £141,894 of entirely new revenue;
land revenue gave £38,000 more ; but in almost every
other line the 1902 figures were not reached.
For a State already possessing the heaviest debt per head
of any country in the world the expenditure of loan
money was still extravagant— £1,022,404 against
£1,161,688 in 1902. The railways, upon which have
been spent about £45 per head of the people, had a
deficit of £384,165, equal to about 13s. 9d. per head.
The bill for interest on the whole public debt was
£1,509,182 ; for every £1 spent on education Queensland
sent £4 15s. 6d. to J. Bull by way of interest; and,
while the interest bill keeps growing, the education
bill, naturally enough, shrinks. More retrenchment
(that is to say, more cutting down of the money spent on
education and so on) is now proposed. Queensland can’t
cut down the interest bill if it wanted to ; and its ruling
gang hasn’t pluck enough to ask the other absentees that
bleed it to contribute fairly to the much too small reve-
nue. So it proposes further to add to its troubles by
borrowing some more; it has been “ watching the market
for some months, and thinks that the time is now almost
opportune.” Poor Queensland 1

Rain-fakir M‘Car thy having failed to relieve it,
droughty Broken Hill now puts its trust in the Owe’Sul-
livan ; and, one way and another, it is hoped that enough
water will be got to keep the mines ana works running
on two shifts before long. To obtain this result half-a-
dozen sources of supply will be tapped. The Broken Hill
Prop, has built itself a large temporary condensing plant,
and will pump mineralised water from every available
abandoned shaft in the neighborhood. This supply is not
large, for even mineralised water is scarce in a desert,
but it will help. The Water Co. is now also draining the
silt and the underlying bed of sand in the reser-
voir. More dependence is, however, placed on
the arrangement which has been made between
the N.S.W. and S.A. Governments, under which
800,000 gallons of water will be trucked up from S.A.
every week, the States dividing the cost. This water will
be brought from the railway dams along the line. The
State was faced with the alternative of feeding 15,000 or
20,000 people, or practically subsidising the mining in-
dustry to a rather less amount in order to keep them at
work, and it wisely decided that the latter was the better
business. Whether the scheme will work out all right
depends a good deal on the ability of the B.H. Prop, to
condense the mine water on a large scale ; if it cannot do
that, then the 800,000 gallons will not go far. Of those
men who cannot be given places on the mines as many as
possible will be employed on the new waterworks at
Umberumberka, which have already been commenced, in
anticipation of Parliamentary sanction. Now that it has
been clearly shown that the existing means of supply are
inadequate that sanction will presumably be forthcoming.
* * * *
Crowl Creek mine, Shuttleton (N.S.W.), is
developing in the deep ground beyond the most sanguine
expectations of its owners, and there can now hardly be
a question as tft its soon becoming one of the leading
copper mines of the State. Furnaces are in course of
erection, and should be in blast in about two months.
At last the public is beginning to nibble at the shares ;
there are numerous buyers and few sellers. It is a case
of “give a dog a bad name” which has caused the
speculative people of Melbourne to hold aloof from the
stock so long. The men who were fortunate enough to
purchase the mine for a mere song compared with its
actual value had a consistent run of ill-luck in N.
Queensland and other ventures, and thus, for a time,
they lost their power to lead the public.
• • • •
Advices from London indicate that the Great
Cobar (N.S.W.) deal has been practically completed. The
capital of the new co. is to be £2,000,000, as recommended
by Kendall, the expert who examined the properties, or,
as he puts it, an equivalent to four years’ purchase on his
estimates and suggested systems of working. Instead of
being paid for in instalments as originally arranged, it is
understood that the purchase money will be paid in a lump
sum in August. It is stated that about £250,000 of the
capital has been underwritten in N.S.W., which is some-
what a surprising circumstance in a community where
the penchant is to speculate in small, worthless cats.
“ Sessil ”: A year ago the Vegetable Greek
(N.S.W.) tin field was in a bad state; the lack of water
prevented washing. Therefore the local lip was droopy,
and there was lamentation in the streets of Emmaville.
Now the place is experiencing a strictly modified boom.
The Consols is being pumped dry—naturally with the
idea of floating it again. Some men are winning fair tin
at the Nine Mile. M'Dowal and party’s star claim at
Silent Grove is working smoothly. The lode pinched,
but a shot uncovered it in full body again. The five men
working have sold £2OOO worth of tin this year and
have much at grass. It is a contact lode between slate
and granite and was prospected in the first place by an
old miner, who took a large quantity of bismuth from it
and regarded it solely as a bismuth show. Romer and
Olive have a very promising lode close by. At the
“Gulf” some selectors from the flat country struck fairly
good tin on land that had been walked over by a genera-
tion of prospectors. It is said that, after the find was
made, tin was found lying on the grass around it. Parker
and Sons have struck a patch of very rich wash dirt and .
are doing remarkably well. At Torrington prospectors
are making fair wages. Carr’s pertinacious little lode at
the “ Gulf” still yields the nice little income that it has
produced for a generation. There are fairly reliable
reports of a good find by a Government-aid man in the
rough country near Kangaroo Flat. If correct this will
lead to the development of a large tract. Asserted by
some that the Emmaville field is played out. Recent
developments suggest that it has not been properly
prospected
C. Towers (N.Q.) looks very healthy. With the
exception of the Brilliant Central and Day Dawn Gold
Mines, divs. are likely to be maintained, and several
shows, including Queen Central, North Queen, New
Queen and Phoebe, look like reaching the dividend stage
before very long. Marshall’s Queen, now all the dead
work is completed, should contribute appreciably to the
div. total, and Brilliant Extended is, with an increased
output, a certain addition. Craven’s Caledonia, owing to
poorness of crushing, has suspended stone raising and
goes on with sinking straight. Day Dawn gold mine for
some reason has sacked 40 men off stone and is pushing
on with underlie to test deeper ground. Plant’s Deep
Lead, on the western deep country, has floated, Brilliant
Central directors having invested their reserves in 70,000
of the Deep Lead scrip. Wallis’ Day Dawn at the S.W.
corner of the field looks promising. The host of pups of
the Ruby P.C. are feeling their way downwards
steadily. On the north the Deep Levels is plugging per-
sistently after a reef of some sort or other. Around the
Queen everything is being urged along in the new shows
on the strength of the Queen Central striking the Queen
Cross Reef at any moment. Two further-out boom-floats,
the East Victoria and Queen and the Wonder and Surprise
have sensibly amalgamated, and are vigorously explor-
ing. Clark’s Worcester is in the dumps, practically only
tributors’ stone being raised. Victory, under pressure
from English holders, is likely to be reconstructed—or
possibly sold as a going concern.
The surprise of the month on Charters Towers
(N.Q.)has been the sudden resurrection of the Imperial,
an outside show. Present co., in four years, spent
£ll,OOO and gold won, and reached an advertisement
calling shareholders to consider liquidation. Scrip stood
at Id., the lower levels were abandoned, and a last
crushing of 60 tons from the No. 2 west level was going
through. Nobody expected anything ; but when the
directors went to the mill for the clean up, instead of
a bar of 500 z., one of 2530 z. awaited them. The news
caused a rush of call-payers, anxious to keep their scrip,
said rush and proceeds of gold leaving the co. hand-
somely in credit. Scrip now, after touching 25., stands
at seller, Is. 6d.; and liquidation meeting is postponed
for one month.

Be the hifalutin of Caldwell, who recently filled
up several Australian papers with the yarn that the
Rhodesia Exploration and Development Co. was putting
a few hundred millions intoCloncurry (N.Q.), it is printed
in London that the co.’s name was used without autho-
rity. All that has happened, it is explained, is that
another lot of African brigands have put up £lO,OOO and
hold an option over the properties, upon which they are
having a report made.
* * * *
Westralia Mt. Morgans is pushing on develop-
ments, the main shaft being down over 600 ft. Five levels
are being driven, and large reserves have been laid bare.
Two lodes are being developed, the main, or east, lode
which passes through the shaft at 550 ft., and the new, or
west, lode. Most work is being done on the former. At
the 200 ft. level the east lode runs up to 40ft. wide, while
at the 300 ft. it bulges out to 95ft. and averages 60ft. for a
distance of 240 ft. In places the ore is very rich, running
up to several ounces to the ton. Co. is earning a profit
on 15dwt. ore of £6OOO a month, so can easily afford to
continue quarterly divs. of a share, or £15,000. Of
its 500,000 shares it has now issued 480,000, 22,000 having
been recently placed at 30s. apiece to cover cost of addi-
tions and alterations to plant. The fortunes of this co.
T *i.^ ee S buil ? n P rom very small beginning's. Origin-
ally two Tremain mills were installed, which were even-
tually displaced by a 20-head mill bought from the
defunct wild cat, Golden Cliffs. Last October this mill
was scrapped ’’ in favor of 60 head of heavy stamps,
one of the best equipments on the fields.

South Kalgurli (W.A.) is to be given another
to me , w ith an available cash capital of
IQ nnn Frank . Gardner has agreed to take up the
18,000 reserve shares at 225. 6d. on condition that a new
AA^ ormed ' vith a nom inal capital of £200,000, or
£60,000 more than the old co. As each shareholder in
tne old co. will, in addition to being allotted share for
snare in the new co., be entitled to apply for one new
share at the isshe price of 225. 6d. for every three held,
Frank.will be entitled to 6000 of the new scrip at 225. 6d.
Mine is to be run by Ralph Nichols, manager of the
Perseverance, and if there is any hope for it (and he
should know) this astute ’Murkan and his clever under-
study, Flynn, should soon show it. The present treat-
ment-plant will be dismantled, and an up-to-date process
substituted. In this property what is known as Morty’s,
or Perseverance, lode has been utterly neglected, as it
proved poor at the junction of the oxidised and sulphide
zones. As this is the best lode in the Perseverance, and
has been proved of good width and grade right up to the
South’s boundary at several levels, it is pretty safe to
Eredict that good ore continues, and has not been cut off
y an imaginary boundary line. The co. should soon
come on the div. list once it re-enters the ranks of the
producers, though some development will first have to be
undertaken.
**. • *
Queen Margaret (W.A.) is making rapid strides
with its new property, the South Gippsland. At the
285 ft. level the south drive is in about 270 ft., all in pay
ore. As this is a great deal further than the original
holders drove in the shallow levels, the backs for a good
part of this distance go right up to the surface. At the
intermediate level nearly 150 ft. of driving has been done,
while at 185 ft. the level is in 120 ft. The reef runs from
6in. to 3ft., and averages about 2ft. The last clean-up
reads 4500 z. from 240 tons. All the ore so far treated
comes exclusively from developments, no stoping having
been done. The 10-head mill has only run half-time, or
12 hours, as the kibble used in the shaft has been unable
to haul more dirt. Cages will shortly be used, and the
mill will then run full time. The output should be nearly
IOOOoz. monthly.

with their characteristic foresight, always place security

creased as compared with the previous year.”*

The merits of the Wilfley Ore Concentrator are.
now fully recognised throughout the Commonwealth,
and its use is extending every day. The New No. 4.
pattern is doing excellent work at the many mines on
which it has been installed. Recent additions to mining
plants comprise : four tables to the Great Fingall, six to
the Oroya Brown Hill, four to the Ivanhoe Company, four
to the Sons of Gwalia, three to the Queen’s Cross Coy.,
Charters Towers, three to the Long Tunnel Extended,
one to the South Moon, Bendigo, and many other mines
too numerous to mention. Upwards of 600 of the Wilfley
Tables are now in use in Australia, giving excellent results
on gold, silver, lead, tin, and copper mines. Full par-
ticulars and a useful pamphlet on concentration can be
obtained from the Sole Manufacturers in Australia, the
Austral Otis Engineering Coy., South Melbourne.*

Mr. Mephan Ferguson, of Melbourne, reports
that there has been a great improvement in the general
outlook of the engineering business since the new year
Started, and that his pipe making works in Melbourne
and Perth are working full-handed. Amongst the orders
now being executed at Footscray works is one for 7 miles
10 chains of 18in. diameter light steel spiral-riveted pipes
for the Victorian water supply ; and at Perth works a
contract is in progress for over 6 miles of lOJin. diameter
spiral-riveted pipes, flanged joints, for the Greenbushes
Tin-Mining Syndicate. About two miles of these pipes
are to stand a test pressure of 8001 b. to the square inch.*

Society
Sydney Evening Noose of 7th July had
ten columns filled with details of the execution of
Grand and Jones, with scraps of old murders,
executions, etc., thrown in. In getting the reader
ready for the “drop” scene, it inserted the
following: “From this beam two great black
ropes were hanging. For a couple of days past
prisoners had been engaged twisting and greasing
these horrible ropes to make them soft and
pliable.” If the News is correct, it is about time
the authorities interfered. If the News is wrong,
then it should apologise to the prisoners. A man
may steal or forge a signature, but to grease
“ two great black ropes” Ugh!
THE REAL DIFFICULTY.
GOSHEN: “ Let's see! First, ye’re goin’ to leave
der pisness! Second, ye're joins der Pible
Crishtians! Third, yd re tinks ’am, sandvich
is goot! Fourth, ye’re goin’ ter marry a
Crishtian! And” (in a yell) “ vat’s ye goin'
to do vis yer no-a-se?”
To put an end to the heart-burning
absence of a military guard of honor at S.A.
Governor’s reception, why not on future occa-
sions of the kind raise a guard of 500 unem-
ployed armed with pickaxes ? They could be
got in these hard times ■ for Is. each, and if
his Ex. was attacked by insurgents or dogs or
Primitive Methodists they would be a much more
serviceable guard than the average military outfit.
Also a regiment of 500 unemployed hitting the
country’s enemies with pickaxes would be a noble
sight.
* * * *
While typhoid laid out its victims whole-
sale at Tamworth (N.S.W.), and while scores of
poor patients were without either decent accom-
modation or nursing, the local hospital committee
continued to keep a large part of the building
empty; and the Government medical officer de-
plores that the institution did not rise to the
emergency. But when did a N.S.W. country
hospital rise to an emergency ? How can it rise ?
It always has to live from hand to mouth. The
poorer the people in a district the greater the
need: of a hospital, and yet the less accommoda-
tion it can offer, because it depends upon local
contributions. If the contributions are small the
Government subsidy is small. So the poorer the
people and the more Government help they need
the less they get. An absurd system, surety.
Whiddon, M.L.A., desires to know if Pre-
mier See’s attention has been called to the way the
streets of Sydney are blocked by loiterers. This
is probably sarcasm ; if Mr. See’s attention needs
to be called, then his attention must be deaf. The
most notable peculiarities of Sydney are the
multitudes of apparently respectable citizens who
stand talking in the middle of the pavement,
or simply smoking, loafing, and gazing at space,
regardless of the trouble they cause; and the
persistence with which almost everybody—even
William. M‘Millan occasionally—walks on the
wrong side of the footpath. The Bulletin, as
the only hope of reform, is organising a society of
large aggressive men, who will be bound, under
heavy penalties, to walk on the right side of the
footpath regardless of consequences. They will
be courteous to everybody who also walks on the
right side, and they will concuss fiercely the
person who walks on the wrong one. They will
be pledged by oath to bang hard into people who
block the way by loafing. If anybody loafing or
walking on the wrong side of the pavement goes
down in the concussion, the society will tread on
him and go on without asking whether he is hurt
or not. A thousand large men could reform
Sydney on these lines.
• • * *
Pastor Fitchett has contributed to Melb.
Abgus a long article on “Wesley’s Place in His-
tory.’ Like “ Bleeds that Won the Empire,’’ it
is not an original production; the holy man shows
great industry in collating other people’s opinions.
That he has little of his own to say is shown by
the omission of the obvious comment that the
Church of England made a great mistake in treat-
ing Wesley as a schismatic and driving him out
of that body.. The Church of Rome, wiser in its
generation, did not treat Loyola as a schismatic 5
it incorporated him as a new force and gathered
the fruits of his enthusiasm.
* * * *
At “a conference on public morals” in
Sydney, the other day, of all who preached or
prosed, Rev. W. G. Taylor was the only one who
dared to indicate the real cause of the social
evil. Whilst the wage for women employed in
shops and factories is, on an average, a good deal
below what suffices for a decent existence, a big
percentage of women will inevitably be forced
into an indecent existence.
Municipal reformers in Australia should
seek to establish a compulsory sanitary record of
all dwellings. By examining a public re-
gister, then, anyone could ascertain the
“health history” of a house—whether
it had ever harbored any infectious dis-
ease ; if so, what disinfecting precautions
were adopted, and so on. Writer has
known on the Blue Mountains (N.S.W.)
a family to come on a holiday trip to a
furnished cottage from which, the pre-
vious day, the corpse of a consumptive
was carried !

“ D-H.” : I got a new servant last
week, and asked her yesterday how she liked
my place. “Very much, sir, because I’m
near me young man.” “ Oh,” said I, “you
are engaged then?” “No, sir, I’m only
ffoin’ ivith ’ini.” Ye gods, I’m puzzled !
Answer me someone. Has a girl any hold
on, or is she engaged to, a man who goes with
her, and what is the meaning of “goes”;
and, further, what are the intentions of-,
young man who “ goes ” with a young woman ?
Bent’s latest addition to Victoria’s comic
opera railway service is the appointment of a
casual blackleg to the position of head spy and
scrutineer over all employees on a large section
of the railways. This loyalist, with no know-
ledge <?f railway work, has a free pass to travel
either in carriages, vans, or engines on his inqui-
sitorial work, and his peremptory orders to do
impossible things are calculated to cause a
revolution. His office empowers him to interfere
with officials in charge of stations, vans and
engines. A few days ago he practically reported
the whole staff on a full length of line. Again,
he reported a porter to headquarters for alleged
gross incivility to him. Unable to get the man’s
name he described him personally, particularly
mentioning his curly head. Stationmaster to
whose station the complaint was sent, wrote
across the report, “Not known here; staff all
straight-haired.”
* * * *
The Warren, the old Marrickville (Sydney)
property from which the Carmelite nuns were
expelled a couple of years ago, has been restored to
secularism and had its sacred character shifted off
the premises by the removal, the other day, of its
founder from the picturesque vault on the grounds.
Many would-be purchasers strongly disliked the
idea of having that tomb on the premises, so
“ Mother Mary ” was taken up and transferred to
the nuns’ new home.
* * * *
The Ballarat police ran in a tight old
fortune-teller the other day, but when charged
with intoxication she said “ cock-a-doodle-do ” so
persistently to the magistrate that she was sent to
the asylum instead of to gaol. Yet, though every-
thing definite except “cock-a-doodle-doo” had
apparently faded out of her mind, she seems to
have had quite enough intellect left to be a fortune-
teller. As long as the seer can crow at her cus-
tomers there are hopes in that line.
‘‘ Burglar Bill ” : The ordinary gaol slang
is inane andstupid. The words possess no merit
to warrant their usurpation of proper
English, but now and then a word
strikes one as carrying its own mean-
ing wrapped in the pronunciation.
“ Smoodger ” pronounced as written,
and signifying a gaol crawler, one
who squirms before the authori-
ties, is one such. Let anyone
hear the word for the first time,
and with ninety per cent, the
meaning is apparent. Its
opposite, “pebble,” gaol
slang for rebel, is not
nearly so comprehensive.
The “second timer” pos-
ing as a “first timer”
generally draws sus-
picion on himself by the
use of his slang. Heard
a man at a political
meeting the other day
say that the speaker
was a “smoodger.” I
tapped him smartly
on the off shoulder,
and he sprang two
feet from the ground
in fright. He had been
there !

An old age pensioner, dragged up before a
Sydney Bench for the maintenance of his four
children, complained that, for some reason un-
known to him, his pension had been stopped this
month and no explanation offered. The magistrate
ordered the children to a home, and directed the
aged father to find Is. fid. a week for each child,
which will just leave him 4s. per week for his own
support, even if his full pension resumes again.
If it doesn’t resume, his weekly income will ap-
parently be 6s. less than nothing.

“What is it , Bill? Tastes like sheep-dip!”
“ Well, did yer think it was (join’ to he rum hot?”
“ Yul.” : I have also a theory for raising
the birth-rate. Make babies illegal, any lady
having one to be punishable by fine
or, say, amonth’simprisonment under
extra-comfortable circumstances. The
amount of fearsome thrill the average
lady would get out of the situation
while she secretly evolved the deed
blissfully and sympathetically aided
and abetted by the household Mary
Jane, the washer-lady, Mrs. Green,
Mrs. Brown, and Mrs. White, of the
left, right, and front respectively,
would be terrific. When the crime
was fully matured at last, and police-
man X7OOO appeared at the door one
morning for the purpose of bringing
the malefactor to book, every window
in the locality would frame a red,
excited face and curl-papers breath-
lessly striving to witness the denoue-
ment. The policeman would be cour-
teous and considerate but firm, and
the prisoner would be'removed in a
cab. The thrilling excitement into
which the said prisoner would be
thrown for weeks after would make
her feel, just like the persecuted
Countess in Home Jumps. She would
be imprisoned in a mansion with the
best food and medical attendance,
and if she was taken away handcuffed
the handcuffs would consist of two
gold bangles connected by a chain,
but the feeling of martyrdom and
persecution would be there—just like
the heroine in Home Jumps. For
the sake of keeping up the feeling of mar-
tyrdom, she should be ■ taken away hand-
cuffed. Fancy the large, comfortable grievance
of the lady who had been 12 times arrested
by a brutal Government, and had pined for 12
months in a dungeon something like Government
House or Sargood’s mansion.

TO KISS HER HAIR.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
To kiss her hair which, uncontrolled
Against my cheek lies, fold on fold,
I plead with all a lover’s art.
Her lips, consenting, softly part,
And mine are pressed to locks of gold.
" Hove can’t be bought ” was said of old ;
But yet—though not for cash—was sold
To me the right, at Cupid’s mart,
To kiss her hair.
No maid can match her ; this I hold.
Her figure’s dainty—such a mould
No artist's chisel could impart.
Then who can blame, who has a heart,
That I continue, times untold,
To kiss her hair ?

The dim white ghost of Benjamin
Goddard, the initiator of “Kyabram” reform,
made a fleeting reappearance in Melbourne Age
last week, to denounce Labor leader Watson for
advocating the Federal High Court, and to ask,
“as a worker having done my 20 hours out of the
24,” whether Watson can be regarded after that
as a “friend of the workers and the unemployed
who are starving in the streets for work at 6s. Gd.
a day.” But the world is so full of these dreadful
old inhabitants who say they used to work two
weeks every day, and then throw a bullock in the
air for exercise when they knocked off, that The
Bulletin grows sceptical, and would like par-
ticulars. Especially it would ask Ben Goddard
these five questions: (1) Is he quite sure he
worked 20 hours out of 24, and that he isn’t
simply the oldest inhabitant who believes he
remembers having seen Cromwell shaking hands
with Julius Oaesar when he was a boy? (2) How
long or how often did he work these hours ? Did
he do it more than once ? (3) Who saw him doing
it? (4) Did he do it when working for an
employer or for himself? (5) And if he really
did it, does he consider that the consequent lack
of time for mental improvement and the turning
of himself into a mere beast of burden account in
any way for his present rather dull condition ?

A rather typical scrap from report of the
Gormanston (Tas.) Board of Health meeting :
Mr. St. Leger drew the attention of the board to the
outbreak of smallpox at Launceston, and suggested that
some steps should be taken.
The Chairman agreed with the suggestion, and re-
marked that some steps should be taken.
Mr. Kean inquired as to what could be done.
The Chairman replied that, in view of the fact that one
case at Launceston had been 18 days out of the hospital
without a true diagnosis of the disease, something should
certainly be done by the board.
It was decided, in the event of a local outbreak of
smallpox, that steps be taken ; but what nature of steps
did not transpire.
’Tis quite a common belief in Australia that to
pass a resolution that “steps should be taken” —
vague, undefined steps to nowhere in particular—
settles the most difficult of problems. Taking
steps to nowhere and signing mortgage bonds are
the two greatest capacities of the Australian
municipal statesmen.
There were two of them sitting on a
a seat in Hyde Park, Sydney. The woman
sat away from the man,
and professed that she
didn’t want to be kissed
when she really did.
Every time he approached
her side she told him not
to, and slid away along
the seat. She was so
engrossed with her graces
and affectations that she
forgot every seat has
an end two of
them, in fact, one
at each end. He
made another
forward move,
and she slid
right off the
seat with a
yelp, and sat
down heavily
o n Ishmael,
the homeless
dog, whojwas
sleeping un-
seeninthelong
grass. The dog
gave an aston-
ished howl and
a bound like a
young lion. The
lover laughed,
and a couple on
the next seat gig-
gled. The laugh,
the giggle and
the dog caused her
to give him a plain
and unvarnished cor-
ner of her mind, evi-
dently till then un-
known to him. When she
had quite finished, which was not for some time,
he remarked coldly that perhaps for the future
they had better be strangers. She agreed. Then
Ishmael, the dog, sneaked back to his lair with a
hazy notion that, low as he’d sunk, he’d once
more been the friend of man.

Charity ball organisers have to dodge
Friday evenings now. Sydney Newsboys’ Society
having fixed on a Friday for a charity ball, the
Hebrew members of the committee resigned,
pointing out that their Sabbath begins at 6 p.m.
on Friday. Date was then changed. Moses,
Isaac, and Abraham are, as a rule, just as charit-
able as they are acquisitive, ana no charity
enterprise can afford to run against their feelings.

Harum ” on

Most fellows
have a fancy
barmaid. My
fancy is a
pretty little
lady barber,
who has to
pat every
common
johnny in
creation un-
der the chin
and soap him
with both
tongue and
brush at same
t i m e. 11
makes me
jealous. I
visit her
three times a
week, gener-
ally at slack
times, and, as
she scrapes my
face, she tells me
hertroublesand some-
times allows a big tear
to drop on my nose. She
works from 8.30 to 6 week-days and till 10 on Satur-
days, and gets 15s. per week. There are two other
women in the shop—the proprietress and a slave, like my
little woman, who does the same and gets the same, viz.,
15s. per week. My girl reckons the hawk-eyed proprie-
tress is a devil to get on with, and clears £lO a week.
She fines the girls 6d. every time they cut a man. My
girl loses on an average 4s. a week at this. “ Unless you
soon earn enough to marry me, I’ll be cutting someone’s
throat,” she said yesterday. Poor little beggar, she’s
miserable. Took her out last Sunday afternoon. Passed
two johnnies ; heard one of them say, “ There’s the little
tart from the barber’s shop ! ” Felt mafl, and would have
jumped on him—only it was Sunday. I’m only a
struggling journalist, and as soon as I am able to dig my
hand deeper into the pocket where The Bulletin keeps
its money, my “ lady barber fancy ” and I will be wedded.
All Bulletin contributors will receive invitations. Oh,
happy day 1

Extract from interview with Miss Rose
Scott in Melbourne Arena-Sun :
Imagine the state of a society when, to aid a Home for
Prostitutes, society women will get up a ball, yes, and
work like slaves to sell tickets and make it a success, and
then dance at it —they, the cream of womanhood, dancing
—the excuse, charity for their degraded sisters. It seems
to me such women would dance on their mothers’ graves
Well, did any mother object to her grave being
danced upon? Still, apart from ma’s grave,
the affair is bad, no doubt. The cream of
womanhood —the thick substance on the top—
actually dancing personally leaping up and
down, and throwing its flesh and clothes in the
air, so to speak, and whirling round and round on
its own feet, and jumping its body and soul off the
ground, with a view to the reformation of its
fellow-creatures, is a humorous idea. When a
person bounds" upwards for the sake of a fallen
sister, and comes down with a whack for the sake
of another, it approaches comedy. This reckless
and cheerless bouncing of the cream ought to be
suppressed.

Old Dr. Ross, the most remarkable man
in the N.S.W. Assembly, has Been asking
Education Minister Perry if he will cause female
State school teachers to leave off wearing gaudy
jewellery and outrageous back hair and red and
green clothes, and compel them to put on a nice,
neat, quiet uniform, and dress themselves plainly
and dowdily a‘s becomes their station. The usually
harmless Perry replied with a satirical “Yes-
No,” but it is understood that his refusal to inter-
fere with the ladies’ clothes and jewellery and
back-hair, and to bring down on himself the
wrath of 2244 teachers, was quite definite.
Burglars have lately been so bad in
Adelaide that it has been seriously proposed
to hold a day of prayer for deliverance
from breaking, entering, and the removal
of valuable property. Adelaide always
prays on an emergency, but in this case it would
be better and more profitable to engage 100 extra
policemen, and pay the force better.
* * * *
The keen westerly-wind season in Sydney
has revived the complaints of the Sydney electric
tram drivers about their severe experiences on
the exposed front of the motor. The cutting
wind and accompanying dust soon affect the eye-
sight. Specs, or goggles are sternly forbidden.
There seems no valid objection to having a glass
front to the trams, as urged by the men.

Parson Dill-Macky broke out last Sunday
in a vigorous Boyne Water sermon, and strongly
denounced the Catholics for their neglect of that
commandment which says, “Thou shalt not
make unto thee any graven image.” After
looking at the very bad graven image of Parkes
which a mostly Protestant Ministry inflicted on
Sydney, The Bulletin wishes the Ddl crowd’s
practice was a little closer up to its precept.
A blast from Launceston :
Launceston (Tas.) so far as present scribe’s experience
goes, is the most conceited town on earth. Launceston
has a fairly decent sewerage system, and the town gener-
ally is very creditably drained. Unfortunately, the decent
sewerage and creditable drainage stop miles too soon.
The town lies far up the Tamar, a very dreary and squalid
tidal river, with mud flats in it. On to these mud flats,
right in and abreast of the town, the sewers discharge ;
with the result that, at all times and seasons, the Tamar
is, with one or two possible exceptions, the dirtiest, ugliest
river in the Commonwealth. From this river strange
stenches are wafted across the town. However well-
drained and sewered, Launceston must be, in the
nature of things, ripe and ready for seizure by any
epidemic that happens along. But the Launces-
tonians call that river a “ magnificent harbor,” a
“ gift of gracious Providence,” and what not, and
write yearnful poems about it. Also, if you suggest that
the river might be improved, the water thinned down
some, and the mud-flats helped to be as innocuous as
the river-mud can be, the Launcestonians will rise as one
lunatic and curse your beastly evil mind. Just at present
Launceston doesn’t mind the small-pox so much, because
it reckons that it’s a superior sort of small-pox, and
decorative. But in the face of the far too easy pre-
cautionary measures, adopted by a Government which
bids fair sooner or later to die of its own funk, Launces-
ton squeals like a woman. At the bare suggestion that
the pest-stricken town on the dirty river should be sort of
isolated, the townsfolk scratched like cats. The doctors cf
Launceston hospital diagnosed the early cases of the pest
as scarlet fever ; and one doctor outside the hospital
industriously treated one small-pox patient for appendi-
citis. But when anyone suggests that these doctors may
possibly be open to criticism, Launceston lets out a
screech about the folly of alarmists and the malignity of
“Southern prejudice.” Launceston’s superb conceit and
unutterable self-love are really interesting. ,<•
* * * *
The break up of the Victorian railway ser-
vice goes on with increased velocity. Reduced
wages, longer hours, uncertainty of the future,
constant spying by Bent’s informers, the heart-
breaking job of working with the miserably incom-
petent blacklegs and the misery of working with
engines that are in the last stages of rust
and old age, are producing their effects. The best
men, who feel reasonably sure of getting work
elsewhere, are leaving by scores. Next trouble is
that Bent’s blackleg firemen are to go up for exami-
nation as engine-drivers this month. Among the
old drivers every one has had about 15 years' rail-
way experience—they all had years of experience
before being entrusted with a driver’s job. The
blackleg, who scrapes through by favor, will be
allowed to run a train to perdition on the strength
of about 15 weeks’ experience at shovelling coal.

A brand new reading of the law of steal-
ing. At Fremantle (W.A.) recently, five men
were charged with being stowaways and travelling
from Largs Bay in the Tarcoola without paying
fare. In the local police court, they were each
fined £3, so the Court plainly regarded their act
as a crime. They had but 45., however, and cn
an alternative term of imprisonment being presst d
for, the P.M. declined to sentence them to ar y
term whatever unless the captain of the ship
guaranteed to pay for their maintenance during
incarceration, his reason being that the punish-
ment was solely in the interests of the steamei s
owners, and that they could not have, made a
diligent search of the vessel before leaving. Cn
the captain forcibly declining to guarantee five
prisoners’ maintenance at any price, the men
were allowed to leave the court, with a warning
from thie bench that they would be expected to
pay the fine later on. The case now stands thus :
Five men stole private property (a ride on a
steamship), but because the owner didn’t prevent
them stealing his property, they are not to be
punished unless at the owner’s expense. Yet
the law was quite willing and is still willing
to collect and keep for its own use the £l5 in
fines. It refuses to recognise the offence except
when there is money in it. '
PROFESSIONAL ITEM .
“ An ’ ef per ma uants me, tell ’er I’m ’ere, n
the surgery.”
A new Bishop has just been imported
from Britain by Maoriland, and he has been
promising to “put some life into our slow-going
community.” T'other Sunday he started by
declaring that “none but the meanest hound
would try and upset another person’s simple
faith.” That’s a very good start for the Bishop,
and it probably will “put some life” into the
community if, the next time he advocates a mission
to upset the simple faith of the Kanaka or the
Mohammedan or the Buddhist or the follower of
Confucius, somebody arises and calls him a mean
hound.
MY LADY HELP.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
She can be both demure and coy
With eyes that flash and lids that droop
To tell a lie I would not stoop,
I’m told she flirteth with my “ boy 1 ”
My lady help.
One tells me so whose word I’d take
Against the word of ten like her ;
And so I’ll watch them both with care.
And if I catch her then I’ll make
My lady yelp.
M.U
KATI KATI.
DREAMS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
You ask me would I like my dreams made true ?
Yes, when I’m dreaming, as I often do,
Of anyone I like—for instance, you.
If some power then would make all dreams come true
How glad I’d be.
But, even then, I fear it would not do
For he or she —at least, I mean for you
Might at the time perhaps be dreaming too—
And not of me.
ALBAR.
SATIETY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A rose without a thorn
You sent me for a token ;
A rose without a thorn,
Fresh as the breath of morn,
And sweet as love unspoken.
A rose without a thorn
You sent me for a token.
But ah, too sweet the rose,
Too rare for my undoing;
Too sweet, too rare the rose,
Sure, every lover knows
That thorn-pricks speed a wooing.
But ah, too sweet the rose,
Too rare for my undoing.
