A WOMAN S LETTER.
Sydney, July 14,1903.
My Dkar Arini,—
Wife of State Premier See has been very ill
with bronchitis for the laßt three weeks, but is
now recovering. One of the things that surprises
men is how women can face, or rather breast, the
theatres these winter nights in decollete gowns
after being furred and coated all day. These
nights, when their men-kind wear overcoats in
dress-circle and stalls, nothing but the possession
of a splendid diamond necklace ought to excuse
the risk of pneumonia or pneuralgia (I don’t see
why it shouldn’t be spelled that way). Which re-
minds me of the current story about Mrs. Pat
Campbell. The actress was doing a season in Mon-
treal (Canada) lately, and posters showing her in a
decollete gown—nothing out of the ordinary—
were put up throughout the city. Moralists
came in the night and carefully plastered strips
of paper over the offending neck and shoulders.
Said that the Department of Morality ordered the
patching. Hm ! One would like to have Mrs. Pat
Campbell’s manager’s affidavit that his was not
the paper and paste-pot.
Onoe I had a dog—a G-ordon setter; owned him
from puppyhood for five years. He had the brain
of an Edmund Barton and the manners of an
aide-de-camp. After he was stolen, and during
my period of mourning, I chanced to look over my
“Newspaper Cuttings” books and found I had
written thirty-two guineas’ worth of sketches and
stories about or around that dog. I was
reminded of him the other afternoon whilst
waiting in a dressmaker’s ante-room, where there
were heaps of society papers and daily papers
containing “Ladies’ Letters ” from most States of
the Commonwealth. Some of the papers were
rather old, others quite young. In the
former the “ dithpled Madden girls ” and
their juvenile-looking mother were exploited
issue after issue in the women’s column.
In the later papers Janet Lady Clarke
was exploited for all she was worth, which is
considerable —her dress, her manner, her charities,
her sympathies, her family, her hair, her typhoid.
The Clarke and Madden families must have
proved very payable to their dry-blowers. There’s
a great knack in prospecting certain families or
certain society women. Sydney women either
don’t lend themselves for exploitation, or our
women chroniclers don’t' knack in that direction.
Though we haven’t here a baronet’s family two'
generations old, there is some material. A few
grains of golden sand could be made out of
Mrs. Burdekin and her egg-shell complexioned
daughters ; Mrs. Philip Morton’s clothes ; Miss
Irene Marks’ bewildering smile and phalanx of
admirers; Mrs. Bernard Wise’s wit; and Miss
Windeyer’s “views” are all as good or better
than anything of their kind Melbourne can show;
and Miss Margaret Harris’ jewels are not
chronicled ,as they deserve.. For the rest, Lady
Munro is in Sydney sometimes.
As long as the cables continue to bleat about
“the successful appearance” of the Australian
singer, so long will last the delusion that Smoke-
ward lies the way to fame and fortune. Scarcely
a week passes but we read that some girl
from Australia with merely a drawing-room
voice has made a successful appearance at
the Bechstein Hall or St. Genesis Chambers.
Then, after that—silence. But Miss Eva Mylott
is, it seems, destined to continue her successful
appearances. Great authorities have pronounced
her voice to have a future. Yet some of Eva’s
warmest Sydney admirers used to vow that Eva’s
platform appearance would bring her a rich hus-
band rather than fame and fortune as a singer. A
fine, brass-bound hossifer, cither military or naval,
was, in the admirers’ opinion, Eva’s predestined
mate. She has been singing at the students’ con-
cert of the Marchesi school, and the Paris corres-
pondent of one of the English papers comments
upon her “thoroughly correct intonation of
Italian.”
I hear that Cardinal Moran claims to have in
his possession the identical prayer-book which
belonged to Mary Queen of Scots, and which she
carried with her to her execution. It is bound in
vellum and has a worn silver clasp; but whether
it is really a better prayer-book than those that
have not been to any execution I don’t happen to
know. But if ever I become a famous character,
and am beheaded for trying to restore the Stuart
family to the Throne, and for waving my parasol
in the front rank of the insurrection, I am going
to carry an extra-special, strongly-bound volume
on my way to the block. It takes two or three
centuries for the book to become really valuable,
and a flimsy one would fall to pieces just as the
price was going up. How do you rise in insur-
rection, by the way, and start restoring dethroned
royal families ?
A curious religious development in Australia is
the number of Christian-born women who become
“converts” to Judaism. All these marry a
Hebrew husband shortly after or before conver-
sion, so the. notion is palpably matrimonial.
Excluding them, the Jewish faith very rarely
secures any proselytes. The Christian woman
will sacrifice her ham only for the reward of a
husband. A rabbi in another State told me that
“we look very coldly upon these ’versions. There
are plenty of Jewish girls for the men of our race
to marry, so we do what we may to discourage
Christian women coming into the competition,
even though they do ’vert to our faith.’*
Yesterday I saw a man cross from Mosman
Ferry at Circular Quay (Sydney) to the Pitt-
street tram, and he read a magazine the whole
time. He took no thought for his life; he never:
looked either to the right or the left, before him,,
behind him, nor between his feet. He just
grinned contentedly, with his eyes glued on his.
magazine, and wandered blindly among charging-
trams, racing vehicles, rollicking cabs, and lorries,
driven at a trot. No, he’s not dead; none of:
them happened to strike him. But had I been,
able to afford the inevitable fine I would have,
prodded that mam something extra tremendous..
He is the sort of idiot who causes tram accidents,
and harrows up the feelings of tram officials, and.
makes complete strangers waste their valuablei
time asking the crowd what has happened.
Went to a gas-cooking demonstration on am
afternoon last week. The demonstrating place
was well filled with wives of men who were evi-
dently in work. A few confessedly very poor-
women were present and a large number of chil-
dren. As the show was duller than being in
church, and lasted close upon three hours, the
demeanor of the youngsters surprised me. It was.
full of bright expectation, and they looked resent-
fully at every fresh arrival. Reason: the results
of the demonstration—scones, rock-cake, buns,
Swiss roll and puff-pastry tarts —were distributed!
amongst the audience at the close of the matinee.
Two very young women were the “cooks.” One
wore glasses and had a lot of silky curly hair-
hanging over her eyes. It does brush my mental
fur the wrong way to see a cookie’s curls mingle in
the batter of the Swiss roll. As the show was given
to prove the excellence of a certain make of gas-
stove there was no explanation of the methods of
preparation. One merely saw the raw material as;
it was being put into the oven. When it came
out —golden, or bright brown, puffed to three
times its original size—and previous to being given
away, its relative merits were judged by three,
women from the audience. For the two girls,
were rival amateur cookies.
One of the most interesting side shows of the.
exposition was the chatter of the women. It ran.
on the dishes their husbands liked and death-
bed scenes. One gave a minute account of the
death of a friend. “ She began to die at half-past,
nine on Saturday night, and she finished at half-
past four on Friday morning. She was dying a
whole week, never stopped an instant, th’ rattle
goin’ the whole time an’ her head rollin’ from
side to side THAT awful. Every now an’ then
she’d come to and turn me out, but bless,
you ! I’d always come back again.” A friend
who had just finished a cheerfully-given account
of a news-boy’s iniuries by a tram, turned to a
quiet woman and asked if she thought it possible
to make a Swiss roll for six with less than nine
eggs. “ Mrs. ’Obson ’said she did it with four, but,
as I was just sayin’ to Mrs.Binks, I’d rather she’d
eat it than me.” The quiet woman temporised
by saying, “Well, what with eggs at 2s. 9d. or 3s.
a dozen, but ” —hurriedly—“ she hadn’t a stove
like that, where you put in the roll or puff paste
with all the jets turned off, and they just cook
with the heat that’s in the stove.” Mrs. Binks
admitted that she hadn’t that kind of stove either.
She Couldn’t pay “ eleven-ten "for a stove:
A small boy attended the children’s fancy dress
ball, in aid of Civil Ambulance, dressed as a
butcher and carrying a wooden tray of meat. He
walked up to Professor Anderson Stuart, and,
pointing to a sausage, said, “This is you, and
this,” indicating a bit of roasting beef, “is Sir
Harry Rawson.” The meat was so labelled, and
was evidently intended for (fancy dress) delivery.
Talking of balls: A damsel was taking notes at a
recent academic frisk when an academician
balanced up to her corner and asked, “ You’ll put
in the fellows’ degrees, I suppose ? ” “ Certainly,”
was the reply, “and their addresses and telephone
numbers also.” But he didn’t see the implied
sarcasm—only showed his nice teeth in satisfaction
and went away.
Sydney’s Bob Cab Smith married off one of his
daughters while in England. Rumor says that
when the Archbishop gets all his daughters mar-
ried he will place his hat and gaiters at the feet of
a well-dowered Australian lady. In case this is
the first he has heard of it, he is hereby assured
that he might do a lot worse.
The exodus of singers continues. Julia Davis
went last week; Bertha Faning (Mrs. M‘Knight)
is packing her grip. When news came of Sylvia

domiciled in Sydney asked if she was an abori-
ginal. From Australasian’s London correspon-
dent I learn that she is a Miss Downing, of Mel-

Phil May is seriously ill at his home at St.
John’s Wood, says latest mail letter. Constant
visitors who try to make him believe that there is
no such thing as pain are sculptor Mackennal and
painter Longstaff.
At the first night of “ Mice and Men,” Nellie
Stewart was handed a violet boa—that is, three
yards, at least, of a boa made of violets, with
chiffon ties. It was something unique in tha way
of floral trophies, and the audience hoped she
would, wear it in the ensuing act. But perhaps
she thought it would not fit her 1786 frock.
Captain Wilson, the new aide to Excellency
Rawson, was on public view for the first time in
Sydney on Monday night, when the Rawsons went
to see “Mice and Men.” He is a tall, fair,
curly-haired young man, and was wonderfully
conspicuous in a scarlet uniform. Most of the
young women in the audience turned languishing
opera-glasses on him.
Lindfield golf-links, on the North Shore line,
have attracted many golfers to that suburb, and
competitions of some importance are decided
there. A “mixed foursome” on Saturday last
fell to Mrs. H. Braddon (of the Tasmanian Brad-
dons) and Mr. Cook.
Marriage at night is somewhat of a growing
institution. Miss Amy Reynolds, one of two
sisters who used to warble with some success in
Sydney, and who went Londonwards for further
trill training, married last week in the Congrega-
tional church a bridegroom named Banks, whom
she met abroad. The “ Voice ” breathed at night
and the “ Obey ” was omitted from the marriage-
service. If this omission be the practice of the
Congregational Church, that creed ought to get a
lot of women ’verts. I am told that the Reynolds-
Banks bridesmaids looked queens of Sheba—real
birds of Paradise. They wore pink chiffon with
court plumes in their hair.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent s—
“ Chief characteristic of the Domino Ball was
its rigid respectability ! Each smart matron had
weighed in the balance and approved of the
masked members of her set, thus doing away with
any risk of a she-somebody twirling round with a
he-nobody, or vice versa. Then, in the round
■dances, the sheep knew the goats by the favors
•worn, so the whole thing was ‘absolutely safe,
■doncher know ? ’ All the cliques cliqued as usual,
;SO goodness knows what object they attained
by hiding all form and comeliness behind face-
screens and body-bags ! Masked Melbourne, try-
ing to ‘ let itself go,’ is suggestive of a character in
somebody’s obscure novel. He had brought up bis
three prigs on the strictest Gradgrind principles,
but one Christmas a rich relative turned up and
aaid he liked to see the young people enjoying
themselves. So the father, in a fierce whisper,
told his three prigs to let their animal spirits
loose, and they couldn’t, for they had none.
“ One masquerader, to my knowledge, did
try to work up the correct carnival spirit but made
the mistake of mixing her drinks. That is to say,
she introduced swirling, whirling, tarantelle Lan-
cer methods in a ‘set,’ otherwise peopled by staid
jog-trotters, with the result that a fluttered subur-
banite, in domino of glazed calico and features
secluded behind lamp-shade of blush-chiffon, was
spun off her legs and piled up in a heap.
“The animal masks were hideous and one
couldn’t admire the new style face-valances of
lace, gauze, &c. The small, old-fashioned black
masks proved the most becoming, while the ghast-
liest exhibits on view were the weird wax casts of
wearer’s features. Nearly as frightening were the
transparent gauze horrors, matching owner’s
domino in color. Imagine women infatuated
enough to peer through green or purple muslin,
■or to face the music handicapped by a false nose
•of pink wax!
“ Dames Sydenham Clarke, Hutton, and Gillott
watched the mummery from the platform, squired
by their respective husbands, plus Vice-Admiral
Fanshawe. The Dowager joined them later in
scrumptious all-white, her raven locks looped on
her neck-nape with girlish Cadogan-plait effect.
Many of the smartest smiled on the show from
the balconies, among them the radiant Mrs.
Isaacs, her white silk appliqued with pale blue
and clustered with roses of faint pink. With the
dancers Miss Elaine Molesworth was seen to be
young and pretty in spite of her disguise—rather
an effective domino of flowing white silk bordered
with pale blue, and inoffensive black mask which,
after the unmasking, she wore slung coquettishly
over her shoulder like a race glass.
“Leading Melbourne firms are not going to
rush the idea of an advertisement ball again. It
doesn’t pay in any sense of the word. For the
recent mask hop, big rag firms were worried all
day long by shoals of fetching girls, each willing
to bear a becoming hoarding on her back for
the sake of the presentation outfit. One
firm allowed generous guineas as expenses
for domino and mask. When finished, and the
4 ad ’ labels affixed fore and aft, the firm ex-
pressed satisfaction. But it had reckoned with-
out the woman. On the night of the hop she
withdrew most of the labels and placed the others
where the balcony eye pierced not, so that firm
is protesting emphatically, and there’s trouble in
the camp. In another instance, where a business
house considered its dazzling representative not
sufficiently on show (she was in hiding most of
the night with a brother masker), it asks the fair
one to foot the domino bill.
“Victorian Artists’ Show grows tamer. The
trouble is diagnosed by some as ‘ provincialism,’3
which, misleading its victims by the applause of
its own little back room, lulls them into content
with their own mediocrity.. Others aver that the
exhibition suffers, not so much from the stationary
attitude of the established artists, as from the
weak strivings of beginners. Striving beginners
deserve encouragement, of course, but not to the
point of admitting their praiseworthy little at-
tempts into a show of alleged ‘ pictures.’
“A show minus Longstaff's work always con-
fesses to a want. Longstaffian-pictured people
have a flash of fire in them, recalling Genesis with
its ‘ And He breathed into him the breath of life,
and man became a living soul.’ If Longstaff’s
people are fire and'clay, M’Cubbin’s, maybe, are
honey—and oatmeal. Latter’s ‘ Motherhood’ in
present show depicts a wholesome, beautiful,
placid woman in short-waisted red gown, sitting
with w r ell-cared-for children and a docile dog
among flowers. Some denominational ladies’
college should hang it in its main hall to show
parents and daughters what good girls may come
to if they remember cold baths, porridge and
catechism in the days of their youth.
“A canvas that awakens much feminine ire in
this year’s Exhibition is one wickedly misnamed
‘Travels of a Royal Personage.’ Every loyal
She turns that way to feast the orbs on a stately
pageant of Princes, and sees—a bizarre scene of
flaunting dancers, with legs poised whizzingly,
around a Royal Don Juan, while other males of
his ilk leer at the dancers.
“There were two luminaries at the ‘Stoning-
ton ’ reception on Thursday night—the Vice-
Admiral and the Austrian Count. Both sported
medals, but the Monsignor’s robes made a more
imposing sight than the brass and braid of the
Navy. Three hundred is a crush at ‘Stonington,’
and no peacock tail got the chance of a full
spread. The concentrated buzz of the 300 voices
was also trying to the nerves and the performing
warblers. Aide Browning, getting the wink from
the music-loving hosts to stem the current of
chatter, battled through the crowd and implored
silence. Lady Silverlocks struck a bright note
in lustrous black and great garnet illuminations.
Mrs. Everard Browne lit up her expensive black-
and-white garb with a wonderful array of dia-
monds. The principal singer of the night was
the Conservatorium mezzo-soprano, Miss Annie
Davidson, a smart Hebrew-featured girl for whom
Marshall Hall predicts a future halo. Miss
Davidson is the daughter of the Chief Inspector
of Public Works.
“During the recent railway strike the families
of the engineers were not the only ones to
tremble. On the night of one hot debate, the
wife of one of the political tyrants was carried
fainting from the gallery. But one other strenu-
ous dame backed her spouse all through the debate,
and cheered the uneasy members of the Ministry
with her Gospel of Stern Discipline. When a
woman is strong and dominant she is merciless.
The sense of power spoils her femininity in most
instances.
‘ ‘ The two most attractive flutterers of the pre-
sent are Commissioner Tait’s wife and a fascina-
ting French girl, Mdlle. de Rougemont—a rather
significant name in Australian ears. Mrs.
Tait is fair, pleasant, and a fine frocker. Her
twang is piquantly light. The French charmer
is a guest of Mrs. Dunbar-Hooper, and flourishes
lots of style. A favorite garb with her is one
running smoothly and closely over the figure, but-
toned up the back, and making for hour-glass pro-
portions. Women who slop over and bag in
Russian style look odd beside this laced-in 1901
figure. It must be admitted that the old, frank
line of bust and the gradual slope of the waist is a
pleasanter sight than the negress-like hang-over
of flesh with an invisible meridian.
“The knot-tying of Major Clifton Brown, of
the 12th Royal Lancers, and Justice Hodges’ fine
daughter is set down for to-day (14th). The
Majah is light-haired, pink-cheeked, and very
English. He has been"staying at Justice Hodges’
house, and has been feted by Toorak, which thinks
him ‘ awfully fine form.’ The engagement came
about last year, when the two attractive Hodges
sisters were on tour with their parents. It’s be-
coming quite popular for Melbourne girls to get
snatched up when travelling.
“Miss Hilda Cumming played hostess at the
Rupertswood spree on Friday, when a goodly
crew of tailor-made sports rallied. The cornely
beauty was rigged seasonably in warm, mealy-
grey, and wore her own rose complexion,
though most others favored a detachable one.
The Iceberg Premier was billed for a speech, and
failed at it signally. The idea of that prunes-and-
prism person, with his sharp-edged lips and
tight-buttoned coat, being cast for the rollicking
breakfast speech to a pack of hot-headed hunts-
men, was preposterous. M.H.R. Cruickshauk
fitted his talk more to things turfy, and cut his
speech to the required measure, getting off one or
two houndy jokes neatly.
“ The Governor-General was present on Steeple-
chase day at Flemington. Governor Clarke talked
with Chief Tennyson and then mounted No. 2, box
with the Huttons—the two dames of this party,
innocently supplying a striking contrast in their
thatches of silver and gold. Miss Vera Clarke
wore a confection of tender green, and thp head
mount continued the tone with intervals of shrimp
pink. The dress had first seen the light in a
Bridal procession. The sudden sunshine induced
other flutterers to give their bridesmaids’ raiment
a day out, and one was in this way reminded of
all the season’s knot-tyings. Mrs. Tait, in tow of
the Forrests, wore a plain smoke-blue garb and a
tricky hat of cochineal flowers—k very bright
affair.
“The Women Writers’ Club (Melb.) received
Miss Rose Scott with trumpets, also shawms,
last week. Mrs. Cross (Ada Cambridge) has re-
ceived numerous congratulations on the recent
appearance of her first grandchild, whose advent
has been eagerly desired —and despaired of—for
seven years. The child (a boy) has brought great
joy into the Cross family, and the gentle little
authoress flushed and smiled over the topic as if
he were her own. Tea and scandal beguiled the
afternoon cheerfully. A detachment of club-
women and suffragists turned up a couple of days
later at the Sydney express and gave Miss Scott'-''
a good send-off.
“ There was a large, eager tea-drinking crowd
to view a show of china-painting last Monday,
next door to the Wattle Club. The ‘Cliveden ’
mistress alighted on the scene in black and
bereavement violet, muttered , the necessary
grace to set the show moving, and rustled out
starchily. The dessert and dinner services on
which the best daubing was done made a good
how.
“ Two frilly sisters, not long back from a Con-
tinental gad, tell their chums that Paris has broken
out violently in a white feather mania, and they
support the information by wearing the whitest,
the longest, the most wonderful feathers ever
seen here. At a recent race meeting, they showed
forth in immense hats, with racing track brims,
on which giant ostrich plumes sprawled inordi-
nately. Yours affectionately, Akenehi.
The Catholic Press, professedly the organ of
the R.C. Church in N.S.W., with several church
dignitaries among its directors, strips itself naked
and howls thus in the highways:—
No. one likes to advocate the triangles and the cat, but
the times are desperate. We have tried kindness, and it
has failed. Everything that humanitarianism has de-
signed for the reclamation of the habitually violent
criminal has been used without effect.
Certainly everything has been used, and tire
resources of civilisation are exhausted ! N.S.W.
has tried keeping the police force at the lowest
possible ebb and giving it so many non-police
duties to perform that it has no time to keep an
eye on the malefactor. It has tried having no
“ Move on ” law, and letting the push congregate •
in the streets as much as it pleases. It has tried
giving the magistrate full permission to let the
gory pushite off with 10s. fine, and in most cases
he would probably keep on doing so even if the
“cat” were legalised. It has tried all. these
things and others like them. Now, how would it
be to get more police ; to let them stick solely to
police work, so that there will always be a tall,
angry “cop ’ bearing down on the push wherever
it is; to fix the minimum penalty for push assaults
at two years ; and to get a church which can exert
some moral influence on the people instead of
a poor, impotent, ignorant, battered failure
which, after toiling for 1900 years, can only sug-
gest flogging the congregation as a means of
reform ?

PERSONAL ITEMS.
The Sultan of Johore bears a more or less
striking resemblance to bookmaker Sammy Allen.
The oldest bowler in Victoria is John
Watson, of Kyneton. He attains his century on
July 26.
Miss Eadith Walker has been again elected
vice-president of Sydney Rowing Club. Miss
Eadith, when a young girl, pulled a plucky oar
on the Parramatta River.
It is one of the conventional fictions of
journalism that a Pope’s career is “brilliant.”
The late Pope is said to have had a “long and
brilliant” time within the walls of the Vatican.
A grand-daughter of the Hebraic-visaged
“ General ” Booth has become a Harmy hofficer.
They’re “keeping it in the family,” and no mis-
take. Three generations feathering their nests.
Poor old Paul Kruger, now nearing his
78th year, after wintering in the South of France,
has gone back to Holland to live. His eyesight
has almost gone; otherwise, it is said, he is fairly
well.
William Robinson, a former member of
the Victorian Parliament, recently died at the age
of 82 in the Melbourne Home for Aged and, Infirm
persons. The railway pass issued to M’s.L.A.
was found among his possessions.
Victorian Judge a’Beckett was at a dinner-
party, and, in the absence of her husband, the
hostess asked his Honor to say grace. The Judge
promptly sentenced the victuals to death with
the words—“ May the Lord have mercy on this
food.”
Captain Dunsford, Lloyd’s surveyor at
Lyttelton (M.L.), who died lately at the age of
85, started life with the East India Company, and
many years later settled down as the first gold
buyer at Ballarat diggings. In ’54 he opened the
Bank of N.S.W. there, but subsequently took to
the briny again, and commanded many big sailing
vessels before taking his last berth.
Deceased : Frederick Brown, Chairman of
Committees in Victoria’s House of 48 Rich Land-
lords. He had been a resident of Beechworth
since 1853, and a member of the House of Rich
Landlords for 19, years. Shiels, when he ran a
short-lived Cabinet in the bank-breaking days,
made Brown an unpaid Minister with nothing to
do, and Brown did it for seven months. That
was his whole record of Ministerial greatness.
THE MINISTER FOR SMALL AFFAIRS.
L.B.M.: “ Can you spare a moment, Mr. O'Sullivan, I want to see you about the regeneration
of Australia.”
THE OWE’S.: “ No, my boy. Run away, I haven't time. Fm too busy over this most im-
portant question of a penny an hour dirt-money.
Civil servants; no matter how big their
salaries, seldom lay up much riches in this world.
Critchett Walker, who handled over £llOO a year
for many years, died worth under £5000; and he
drew over £4OOO in a Tattersall’s sweep a couple
of years ago.
After gradually working his way up the
ranks since 1876, Upton, of the S.A. detectives,
was appointed head of that department at the end
of June. ■ Within four days he was dead. An
unusually short reign in a civilised country where
policemen, as a rule, die a natural death.
Henry Spencer, the long-haired old man
who used to sun himself so regularly in Wynyard-
square, Sydney, has died at 93. He was a man of
intellectual turn, but more contemplative than
practical. He corresponded with front-rank
writers, Herbert of the same name being free and
frequent in the postbag. A son of his is Auditor-
General of Westralia. •
The Commonwealth has in its service a
man who claims to be a son of the late Prince
Consort. He says that he is the senior uncle of
the little person who was out here a year or two
ago. He put up with the wickedness of royalty
until he was 16 ; then it got too strong for him,
so he escaped over the garden wall of the palace
and came to Australia.
Barrister St. Ledger, who is a prime
mover in the scheme to collect a £lO,OOO fund to
fight the white Australians at next Queensland
Federal elections, is a man of parts. Originally
a school teacher, he got into the legal profession
some years back and stood as an unsuccessful
Laborite in a Queensland State election. Then
he backslided to Philp and Co. and stood as an
unsuccessful Tory at Charters Towers. As a
Labor man he came third out of four candidates;
as a Tory lie came last out of four candidates.
S.A. Govt. Geologist Brown, the man
who broke the Arltunga boom, was born in Nova
Scotia, but for about 40 years has had connection
with geographical work all over Australia. He
has an extensive knowledge of the Australian
bush Was originally in the employ of Victoria
but left and went to Westralia and thence to
Canada. Returned to Australia in 1881 and -after
18 months in the N.S.W. Geographical Survey
was appointed G.G. for S.A. and has now held
that position for more than 20 years.
The question of mental in Lienee upon
man’s bodily condition seems to defy medical
analysis and put theory to confusion. Henry
Jones and Digby Grand were put into two con-
demned cells to await their hanging for one on me.
Jones fattened considerably whilst Grand lost
flesh.
Died at North Sydney recently, the widow
of B. O. Holtermann, who found the immense
mass of gold at Hawkins Hill in 187 b it weighed,
before being broken up, 6301 b., and was wmth
,£l2 000. Holtermann’s partner, Beyers, sold out
his interest in the claim for £70,000. Holtermanh
and Beyers both entered Parliament, but left no
mark.
The rain-maker, Dr. M'Carthy, does not
travel in style. The Adelaide interviewer who
went up to Aid gate to meet the Melbourne ex-
press missed him when he searched the boudoir
cars, but on another attempt, at a wayside station,
he discovered the modern Aquarius huddled up in
the corner of a second-class carriage. Ihat was
in,the day of his greatness. If he sank with his
failure he probably left by seizing the rear buffer
and running behind.
Thomas Roberts, who died at Fitzroy
(Vic.) on July 6, aged 91 years, was a member of
Ross’s Arctic expedition. In Maoriland, he
fought prominently in the early wars. At one time
he was master of the ship President, which he sold
in the early fifties to the Government for conver-
sion into a prison hulk in Hobson’s Bay. Many
early citizens, some of whom probably still live
and wash themselves in strict seclusion, were
flogged on that old vessel.
Maoriland Attorney-Generalship, which
has been vacant since Patrick Buckley dropped
out in 1895, has been filled by the appointment or
Colonel Pitt to that office. Pitt, who commande 1
the Maoriland contingent at the late Widow oi
Windsor’s diamond jubilee, went to Maoriland
from Tasmania many years ago, and saw service
in the Maori war. It was to him that le Whiti
yielded at Parihaka. Pitt was lowered to the
Upper House in 1899.
T M. Slattery, M.L.C., will probably
stand for a seat in N.S.W. Assembly next elec-
tions. “ Tom ” acted splendidly m the Assembly
long ago. 44 How it is that such a person as Mr.
Slattery can possess any influence in any com-
munity I am never able to understand, said
Henry Parkes. But 44 Tom ’ had just then fried
the posing Henry in a burst of vituperative
oratory, and Parkes was never able to under-
stand how any of his opponents possessed any
influence.
Veteran Major Charles Westrup, buried
lately in M.L., took a conspicuous part in the
Maori war, and came out with flying colors in all
his battles. He became a fairly rich man in after
years, but lost the lot through unwise specula-
tions. Then he asked the Seddon Government for
a billet, and got a temporary clerkship in “the
Largest Wooden Building in the World.” When
the Big Gun applied the pruning knife six
months ago he kicked Westrup out, and the old
man went home to bed and died. But he was
accorded a grand military funeral !

Late Mount Morganaire Hall will be
regretted by those Rockhamptonites who tasted
of his bounty in the autumn of 1880. Hall “did
in ” about £7OOO for a week’s carnival of racing,
biking, rowing, and mixed drinks. After 23
years the oldest inhabitant of that thirsty town
recalls the few days when there Was a red tinge
from the rising of the sun to the going down
thereof—and the nights, when it was common to
find some total strangers sleeping comfortably on
the front verandah whilst some more would be
reposing (filled with emotion and light refresh-
ments) in the bath.
One of the best known characters in
Perth (W. A.) is a lady orator whom Providence
has inspired to pursue a vendetta against
the legal profession. The lady some years
since lost some property in a law-suit,
hence the trouble. She is a Sairey
Gamp during the week, and every
Sunday lectures on the local
equivalent for the Yarra bank on
“ Legal Robbery in W. A.” Some-
one sent the lady a ticket for the
ooening of the new Perth Supreme
Court lately, invitation to which
was issued in the name of the
Government, and a chill smote
the Devil’s Brigade, assembled in
wig and gown to welcome the
Governor, on seeing the enemy
occupying a position of vantage
amongst the guests. The lady, how-
ever, to their great relief, contented
herself with calling for three cheers for the
Governor.
Passed out at Sale, Gippsland, the Rev.
W. S. Logan, a pioneer cleric of the old type
A native of Orkney, he emigrated about half-a
century back, and commenced to save souls in the
bush, a work hindered by the exceeding roughness
of the said bush, and the exceeding unwillingness
to be saved of the said souls. Whether the saving
process effected much or not, his name is remem-
bered and respected all over the back country of
Victoria as that of a parson who never shrank
from wind or weather, hard travelling, or a rough
tongue—or, some say, from “putting up his
hands.” His wife, in every respect his mate, died
a few months back, after which the old man turned
his face to the wall and quietly slipped off.
During his last days his memory for later events
quite left him, but he delivered over again the
first address he gave as a young man—an address
d 1 veivd to about 30 bushmer.
Rowland Rees, an ex-Minister of the
Crown, who sat for 20 years in the S. A. Assembly
and who was accounted one of its most eloquent
talkers, was taken to the Parkside Asylum a few
days ago. Just previously John Richards,
another ex-legislator, was sent into durance as a
dipsomaniac. Richards sat for the mining dis-
trict of Wallaroo, and was the first Labor mem-
ber sent into the local Parliament. He was then
a teetotaller, and a leading light in religious
matters.
Through the retirement of W. C. Walker
from the M.L. Ministry, Seddon becomes Minister
of Education and of Immigration. The King has,
during his 10 years’ reign, been either all the
time or some of the time Premier, Minister for
Public Works, Minister of Mines, Minister of
Defence, Minister of Native Affairs, Minister of
Labor, Commissioner of Trade and Customs,
Postmaster-General, Electric Telegraph Commis-
sioner, Treasurer, Minister of Immigration, and
Minister of Education. So far he hasn’t been
Minister of Justice or Attorney-General, Minister
of Lands or of Native Affairs, or Colonial Secre-
tary ; but he has done some of the work of all
these offices.
Edward Greville, an old Parliamentary
party, died last week, aged 82 years. For 10
years he represented Braid wood in N.S.W.
Assembly, and, as usual, when he ceased to be
much good there, he was appointed to the Leg.
Council. He dabbled in printer’s ink as well as
legislation. He founded the Year Book, and had
an interest in country directories. Also, tin re
was a time when Greville and Co. and Gordon
and Gotch had under a very large thumb most of
the minor country press of the country. In the
mining boom many fortunes were lost in Greville’s
Rooms, George-st., then the headquarters of the
mining fraternity. The G.P.O. stands on the
site now.
“ Maitland ” : I remember the “ silenced
cleric ” mentioned by “Euroa”(B., July 2). A
little, thin, clean-shorn man, starving visibly, but
his talk of South Africa took in no one who knew
the country. The then licensee of the Elizabeth-
street hotel which he frequented was a Dutch-
man—it was during war-time that the silenced
one used to glide in and out —and his reverence’s
S’African (book) talk and temporary pro-Boer
sympathies often got him a prolonged attack on
the counter commissariat with a long sleever
gratis. Too many glasses originally led to his
being silenced in England and exported, and it
was for appropriating glasses (opera) that he got
two months’ gaol.
Sydney University has produced its first
Doctor of Laws for 11 years, (not counting the
mock degree ladled out on George of York), in the
person of Geo. W. Waddell, nephew of the
N.S.W. Treasurer. This privilege of label-
ling himself Dr., won’t necessarily help the
new LL.D. much. The degrees of LL.B.
and LL.D. were almost unknown when the pre-
set front-rankers of N.S.W. Bar were reading.
Just as the leading men in the medical profession
were quite content with a minimum college
diploma, so used the man of law to do as little Uni-
versity work as possible. Indeed Want, Salo-
mons, and Reid have no ’Varsity degree at all.
Lately, however, University degrees, both legal
and medical, have come into favor.

Writes “Buzz”:
Now that the drought has fairly broken in Queensland,
we may expect to hear of some sensational transactions
in stock. Most of the Q. meat-works closed down months
ago for want of cattle, but the O.Q. Meat Export Co. at
Lake’s Creek has managed to keep going all through. A
few months ago they started canning and preserving fish
and poultry, with most excellent results, and as the co.
paid a fair price for all fish delivered at its wharf, it
gave fresh life and impetus to the fishing industry. Kep-
pel Bay and the whole of the coast round about Yeppoon
and Emu Park teems with fish—it only wanted a sure
and regular market to set things running, and directly
Lake Creek offered this, several young fellows started
fishing stations on the coast, running their fish up to the
creek per train daily. At one place, a few miles from
Yeppoon, an enterprising one time employee at the
Creek started a canning factory on a small scale, and
having got a very fair market among the Rockhampton,
Mount Morgan, &c., &e., storekeepers, was doing capital
business when last heard from. Less than three years
ago the Central Q. Meat Preserving and Export Works
changed hands —were bought by an English syndicate for
£BO,OOO, one-third cash. The English syndicate, which
got a bargain, has since then entirely changed the man-
agement of the place, which is now run by American
bosses. About two years ago the Lake’s Creek
cattle buyer secured 2000 head from Blackall, to be
held by owner till the drought broke, as the roads
were impassable. Strange to say, these cattle, or
most of them, have survived on scrub, bottle-
tree, or something else ; and now that rain has come, and
the roads will soon be passable, these cattle, while repre-
senting a matter of many thousands to their owners,
will save Lake’s Creek meat-works the humiliation of
having to close down and wait for cattle. By the time
the 2000 are through, others, bought months back, will
be fat enough to travel.
* * * *
“ Kiwi”: Maoriland’s once-abundant bird-
life is perishing fast, partly through the ruthless
waste of our fine forests, but chiefly because of
the introduction of stoats, weasels and ferrets, to
make war upon the rabbits, which are nevertheless
still plentiful, while the country is being overrun
with their “ natural enemies.” Hence our birds
are disappearing rapidly. Ducks of all kinds, the
kiwi, the weka, the kakapa are being swept out of
existence. Nearly all our birds nestle on the
ground, and some of the most interesting species
are wingless. They are, therefore, a ready prey
to enemies against which they have no instinct to
warn them, Worse still, the acclimatised pests
seem to thrive; in fact, the ferret becomes large
and ferocious beyond recognition. I saw one shot
recently which weighed 41bs. It was believed at
first that our climate would keep these creatures
in check, but unhappily it has no such effect, and
—alas for our birds !

Dear Bulletin.— ln a somewhat pessi-
mistic article re the spread of the tropical earth-
eating disease, Geraldton (N.Q.) State medical
officer M ‘Donald asserts that, in adults, the com-
plaint carries with it a strong yearning for
alcohol. Can the earth-eating trouble be
responsible for the mostly rummy and beerv
condition of tropical Queenslanders? It is not
unusual to find a whole township, including the
goat and the stray dog, “under the influence.”
Sobriety in general is rare enough up North to be
worth including in Fitzgerald Bros.’ side-show.
Apropos —the drunk in the home of the granadillo
and themurderous Solomon Islander, when charged
with “bein’ spacheless, yorr Washup, an’ dis-
liorrderly, ” can now plead ankylostomiasis. He
is bound to get off if he insists on his plea being
written down. There is hardly a J.P. in the
North who can spell that word. — Dtckolt.

“E.R.M.”: Travelling near Warragul
(Vie.), a short time backfl put up at a bush pub.
and was told the following, which has become
quite a legend up there. Stopping at that pub.
for the night, in the early seventies, were the pas-
sengers by the mail coach, among whom was an
exceedingly aggressive Yank. He held forth for
two hours or more in language superbly garnished
with rich curses, upon niggers, whom he “reckoned
to be the goldarnest critturs that ever disgraced
the earth.” In the morning the coach continued
its journey with, to the Yank’s disgust, a nigger
passenger. Soon after, a diversion was caused by
the sticking up of the coach by bushrangers. Ail
passengers were relieved of their valuables, and,
to prevent the alarm being given too soon, were
tied back to back and left some way from the
road. The American’s partner in this “ set ” was
the nigger, and he was bound to that ebony
heathen for 16 hours. When found, the things
he had said had shrivelled up the vegetation for
16 yards either way, and there is still a bare spot
thereabouts to mark the centre of the proceedings,
though the outer parts of the circle have partially
recovered.

The health officer of Poowong-and-Jeetho
Shire (Vic.) lately threw a heavy consignment of
insulting language at his employers. After re-
porting on an outbreak of typhoid at Jumounna
—which township does without drains and buries
its night-soil in a paddock above the level of the
houses—he proceeded: “The callousness of the
council for the health of the townspeople in this
shire is appalling. . . Can no sense of duty stir
the local board of health to put our drains in
order, or are we to await the advent of a scourge

to decimate the community before it stirs the
members’ lethargy? Typhoid fever is a dirt
disease; I desire, therefore, as the local health
mincer and as a citizen, to impress on the council
the immediate necessity for action, even if it has
to beg, borrow or steal the money.” And the
shire magnates weren’t annoyed at being insulted
thus openly ; they merely decided that it wasn’t
advisable to increase the rates on any account.

• * * *
A tale of the country beyond the BarcoO :
Kidley Williams had occupied Burbanks station for 30
years. In the eighties he thought he was a rich man.
They were branding 3000 -calves a year. In the early
nineties a change set in, and things grew worse and worse,
until in 1902 they branded two calves ! But just then
the rains came, and the grass sprang as it had not done
for years. He listened to the siren’s song, and hesitated.
It might all come right, after all. Then he reflected that
it was only annual grass and light herbage that had
sprung ; the old drought-resisting plants were all gone.
He counted the cost of re-stocking ; he counted the risk;
then, plucking up courage, he packed his portman-
teau, sent on what few head of stock were left, and
abandoned Burbanks, improvements and all.

“ Riv.” : Harry went from the river bend
where we were camped to the Homestead for
rations, but came back empty-handed. “I only
saw the storekeeper,” he said; “an’ I couldn’t
get nothin’ from him, the cow! But I shook him
up. I ses: ‘I suppose yer laborin’ under the
seclusion that we’ve got plenty tucker, are yer—
yer blot on the face o’ Providence? Further-
more,’ sez I, ‘if you call yerself a gentleman—yer
nothin’ but a blanky perforator of the truth ! ’
My word !” finished Harry, enthusiastically, “he
did look. Talk about a surprise ! Why he was
fairly putrified to the spot! ”
“ F.C.” : Had some experience of
one of Bent’s blackleg drivers lately ;
don’t want any more. Left Gembroolt
(40 miles from town) at 5 p.m., arrived
Melbourne 10 p.m. Changed first at Fern
Tree Gully. Here Bent’s angel took
charge. He meandered gradually along
till past Ringwood, where the Mitcham
hill has to be tackled. He had two
shots at this, and finally surmounted it.
After Mitcham came the down grade,
and here he shone. He overshot the
next station by about a train length.
Dwelt there for five minutes, then slowly
backed his machine to the platform.
He went flying past the next station
altogether, but managed to pull up a
mile further on. Then the guard’s voice
was heard inquiring if there was “Any-
one for Blackburn?” One small voice
piped out “ Yes,” but we in the smoker
urgently desired its owner to hold his
peace or we’d be there all night. Amid
a chorus of yells to Bent to come and
give his beauty a hand, to get off and
shove, and other complimentary re-
marks, we moved off as gradually as
before, arriving at Box Hill to find the
connecting Melbourne train gone long
ago. We were full up of Bent and his
“loyalists,” but what the half-dozen
passengers left behind at Blackburn
station thought of Tommy’s pet isn’t
known and wouldn’t be printable if it
were.
* * * *
“ B-.A.P.” : Astonishing the care-
lessness with which some stumpers and
wood-splitters deal with dynamite. Re-
cently saw two stumpers working, each
with a plug of dynamite inside his shirt;
the idea being that the warmth of the
body would soften it ready for use. One
man was chopping and the other fooling
round with a lever. In another man’s
camp, one night, I found him pensively
pulverising dynamite with the butt end of a
wedge. He had some more lying close to the
fire, being warmed. When remonstrated with,
be stated that there was no danger, and added
that he had often driven a plug into a hole too
small for it, with a maul! He was reaching for
an axe to illustrate bis remarks when I left hur-
riedly. Now, what does start dynamite, besides
the usual percussion cap ?
A LAMENT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
(“ On and after July Ist no more weather forecasts for
Australia are to be issued by Mr. Clement Wragge from,
Brisbane.”— Official Announcement.)
There is sorrow at the heart-strings of the man who sails
the sea,
And the farmer and the squatter are as sad as sad cau
be ;
Our daily life seems duller, and a cloud has dimmed the
sun,
For the Weather Prophet passeth, and the Seer’s work is
done !
Let us touch the harp-strings gently, let us play a muted!
chord ;
Let us shun the good old flowing bowl, and flee the
festive board ;
Let us mourn , awhile together, town and country, farm
and ship ;
Like a foal that’s lost its parent, let us hang our lower
lip.
Let us turn our faces earthward—that once sought the
fulgent stars,
We liiay gaze no more at Clement’s barometric isobars 1
For we burn with indignation at the cruel, stern decree—
“ iou shall hear no more from Brisbane what the
weather is to be /”
Do they know—these dour dictators—what their dark
deed really means
To the miner, and the milkman, and the party growing
greens?
To the cabbages of Brighton, and the spuds of Warrnam-
bool ?
To the anxious bargain-hunter, and the festive Sunday-
school ?
Ay, and more, to strong men walking—how the dickens
will they tell
If it’s going to be fine or is about to rain like hell?
And what about new weather (when the old lot’s bald and
grey)
The stuff they used to turn out fresh at Brisbane every
day?
Must we linger while the dun years spin their weary warp
and weft,
Living on moth-eaten weather that Wragge dropped here
when he left?
On aged scraps of climate, musty, frayed and second-
hand,
When we want fresh climate daily, just like any other
land ?
Shall we live on stale tinned cyclones, just to save the
Prophet’s screw ?
Patch the seats of worn-out droughts, and try to think
them fresh and new?
Or rouse the heathen’s scoff by sending to him in his lair,
Humbly asking .if he has a half-soled isotherm to spare?
Shall the alien see at midnight a Thing all torn and rent,
Prospecting in his ash-keg, or o’er his gutter bent,
Seeking disused isobars, or, struggling with his dog,
shall he
See it stealing old “depressions?” Shall he know that
Thing is we?
Shall we cadge for misfit monsoons from the stranger cold
and hard ?
Prig humidity that’s drying on the clothes-line in his
yard ?
O Australia, be a nation strong—a folk to manhood grown
And decide to pay your Prophet and have weather of
your own 1
O. C. OABOT.
CALLIN’.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
0, the Road’s a-callin’, mate ;
Time to up and get.
Feel yer blood run quick and straight,
Oampin’ early, risin’ late,
Goin’ slow, you bet.
Hear the roosters crowin’ loud
In the early morn,
Over ranges purple-browed—
Sun just burstin’ through a cloud,
And ye know yer born.
Jackass sittin’ on a limb
Laughin’ fit to bust;
What the bunyip tickles him
You don’t know, but just join in
O’er a breakfast crust.
Lizards sleepin’ by the way,
Parrots skimmin’ by;
Scent of rain-damp earth, and hay,
Silences of twilight grey,
River, plain, and sky.
Blasted bustle of the town
Failin’ well behind.
Sprintin’ thieves with heads bent down
Racin’ for a stray half-crown—
Out of sight—and mind 1
0, the Road’s a-callin’, mate ;
Callin’ you and me,
Sons of hers, so pack up straight,
Open-armed she lies await.
Out we travel, free !

“Kati Kati”: Be tooth - drawing
experience. When a boy, took gas once
Dentist advertised process as absolutely
painless, and assured me I would never
feel the extraction. I never did. Fat in
his chair, breathed the gas, and quickly
forgot all about teeth and toothaches in a
dream that I was in hell and suffering all
the torments of the lost. I had just said
to myself, “This is likely to last, so I must
just make the best of it,” when I came back
to the dentist’s, and found the tooth had
been extracted without my noticing it at
all. Have had two teeth out since then
I jerked them out on my own. It is not
worth while to go to hell to have one s
teeth extracted without pain. An old
farmer told me that, when a boy, his father
used to extract the children’s first teeth
by fastening a string round the trouble-
some customer and giving it a half-hitch
around the chimney bar short enough to
bring the child unpleasantly near the fire.
In half a minute he would begin to scorch,
and rush back on his haunches, leaving the
tooth where it was tied. But I don’t
know about the invariable truthfulness of
old farmers.

* * * *
“6x8”: I want to know, whether rain-
manufacturer Dr. M ‘Carthy is any better at
stopping rain than at making it? Can he bring
down dry weather? In many parts of tropical
Queensland a rain-stopper is the person mostly
in demand just now. If anything, floods are
worse than droughts. A drought sets one moving
to wetter climes, but a flood imprisons you and
condemns you to live on the flesh of the gruesome
gohanna that swam to your island or gum-tree
in search of safety.
* * * *
For a droughty land, Australia has an
amazing number of rain makers. As soon as
Broken Hill started to make terms with M‘Carthy,
they all rushed that dry and distracted city.
Most of them relied simply on prayer; the obvious
advantage of that process was that it didn’t
require any plant, and there wasn’t any need to
spend money on a railway-ticket. One offered to
pray until rain came at Is. an hour, This one, a
female, didn’t specify, however, whether she
worked on the eight-hour principle, or whether
she would keep going the whole 24 hours, and the
Council put the offer aside as too indefinite. A
few wanted the cash wired. “ I have a wonderful
power given to me to help my people,” one wrote.
“ I can see the force that is being used to bring
rain is of little value. Telegraph sufficient for
my expenses to the Hon. It. E. O’Connor, and I
will pray for you.” The municipality did not
know just how large a professional pray-er’s
expenses were, but reckoned that if he hired a
K.C. as an agent they must be fairly high, and so
did not risk it. As soon as McCarthy got to
business there was another downpour of letteis.
They were mostly in women’s writing, and nearly
all of them solemnly warned Broken Hill that it
was offering a deadly insult to high Heaven—that
it was relying upon a base usurper, and that if it
kept dry for seven years it was its own fault.

“Jimmie Pot”: Why do folk so often
assert that the old Cornstalk town of Windsor is
a weary, sleepy, drowsy place ? In a recent issue
of the local Gazette there appear cautions against
interference with children, against making mis-
chief between me and mother, against keeping
property that does not belong to one; also
Challenges to various contests for huge amounts—
Jenkins offering to jump Jones for £5, and things
like that. Perhaps Windsor is having a Renais-
sance. It was lively and snappy in the olden
golden days.
“ Warree ” We hear much ab
times about Sydney registry office methods,
but nothing is ever said about country
ditto. The country R.O. mostly combines
with that business the advantages of a
home for the. out-of-work domestic, pre-
sided over by an old female harpy; charges
about 15s. a week. One unclean room
serves for ah the “lodgers.” The registry
fee and the board money are always made
a first charge on the wages, and heaven
help the unfortunate creature who seeks
to evade payment.

An audacious wool-robbery was nipped
in the bud near Gummin (N.S.W.) in 1900.
Incited by the wool-boom, some brigands started
shearing—simultaneously with the station —in
some scrub near the boundary. The sheep were
taken from the paddocks, shorn, and put back
with the sheep shorn by the station. But the
operators neglected to brand the jumbucks, and a
smart stockman soon noted the number of “ clean-
skins.” A search was made, and the camp found
with 100 fleeces in it. No catch was made.
Ready on Ist. August. The Bulletin’s new
book, “ Such is Life !” by Tom Collins. 4s. 6d.
Whoever would be “ Cock o’ the walk ”
Must open his mouth and crow loud,
For whispers and mutters are plainly not talk,
And very soon lost in a crowd.

address must appear on MS. Ko liability is under-

State) accompany.

The Victorian Artists’ Show.
The Y.A.S. is holding another annual winter
demonstration at the galleries in Albert-street.
Melbourne, and to look around those galleries
with a bilious eye, after reading the local press
notices of this year’s display, is to wonder whether
the writers take their ideas from the artists who
provide it. Leaving 100 unambitious water-colors
out of the question, there are about 140 oil-
paintings, mostly such as no true artist would
submit to the public for approval. Why disguise
the fact that the desire to make a fair-sized show
tempts every Australian art society to flatter the
pretensions of hopeless daubers and dabblers .
Perhaps the worst of the pictures are less un-
worthy of admission than the worst in previous
years, but after giving hearty thanks to the
committee for the things it didn’t hang, there
is little left to be grateful for. Take the
“ star” productions. Nerli’s portrait of Rev.
Dr. Marshall is a large irritating article, priced at
,£200105., the ten shillings corresponding with the
odd three farthings on a draper s ticket. Ihis
apostolic advertisement is a reminder of the Dr.
Bevan portrait which Nerli exhibited last year.
The Rev. Dr. was shown in his habit as he lives
when expounding the earthly blessings of 1 ree-
trade to his disciples. Rev. Marshall appears in
full pulpit garb ; his black and white whiskers
are turned somewhat heavenwards and he wears
the smug smile of a good shepherd returning
thanks for rain. Catalogued as a humorous design
for a stained-glass window figure, the study in
black and white would be well enough. As the
second in a series of glorified parson portraits it
calls for protest. Other parsons, doubtless, are
waiting 1 their turn, and a picture of Bishop
Clarke saying his prayers may come next.
* * • *
Three or fout other pictures by Nerli have been
hailed as masterpieces. The praise has been laid
■on too thick. “Gwendoline” (222) is a dolly-faced
child in white satin frock and pink shoes. A
profusion of pink and white roses adds to the
painful effect of little Gwen’s “ get-up ”; she has
an unconvincing arm and hand, and she utterly
lacks the charm which Longstaff imparted to
his portrait of Marjorie Williamson. “My Lady
in Green ” (169) depicts a thin, bony lady in a
pea-green evening dress. Her bare bust and
arms seem to cry aloud for concealment, and the
■essential unloveliness of the picture is accentuated
by an affectation of “strength.” “Bacchante
(165), the bizarre presentment of a whirling
Eastern woman—also by Nerli—is welcome
for the sake of its rich decorative scheme ot
■color, but concerning the giddy females figure
nothing seems certain, except that it is inhuman,
and the neck is painted out of all sympathy with
the shoulders. A clever artist is entitled to be an
impressionist in a work of this sort, but
at least avoid impressions of impossibilities.
“Motherhood,” by Fred M’Gubbin (11), is a
family group —mamma in red gown, seated on a
log; small daughter, two boys and the dawg,
against a forest background. The coloring is not
a triumph ; the lady and the two little ones beside
her might have been posed by a photographer, but
they are pretty subjects for a good, honest picture.
With a second picture—a girl in white—MLubbin
scores somewhat.
* * * *
“Equestrian Portrait,” by Hugh Ramsay (25),
is a vast stretch of canvas. A rather wooden
young man leans one arm upon a formidable dark
bay horse in the immediate foreground, whilst the
animal nibbles grass. An aggressively brown
study. A flat brown hill or wall of paint is
placed behind the horse. Of course, there are
some good points about this extensive production,
or it wouldn’t be covering such a large area of a.rt
gallery. The hands of the young man are realistic,
likewise the horse, when considered on its huge
“ own ” whereas the heavily outlined face of the
man is lifeless. The work of a promising novice
■on an heroic scale. Walter Withers, sane and
■careful as usual, contributes two capital Australian
landscapes, with plain truth in them, and John
Forde Patterson, also in his familiar style, gets
-very near to poetic excellence with ‘ Ihe
Lagoon ” (39), a pale moonlight picture that anyone
can admire at once and go on admiring—if alreac.y
accustomed to J.F.P.’s mannerisms. Schelten.a
and Arthur' Boyd repeat themselves pleasantly
and the more passable of the Melbourne lady
artists are represented by respectable works in
some cases. But in the large majority of the
pictures something, or everything, is distinctly
-wrong—drawing, coloring, composition, or what
not. The painters are not supplying a Want.
They have no more claim to approval than has a
bad actor who worries along in the theatrical pro-
fession, for the vanity that influences either sort
of pretender is one and the same. The public
has no time to discover evidence of petty techni-
cal proficiency in pictures which don t impress
it forcibly, or vaguely, or delight it at all. or
the artists and their circle of friends there is
some entertainment in believing that geese are
swans, especially when one poses in the character
, of a swan.

(OPPOSITE REDFERN
RAILWAY STATION.)


“ Jaybell ” on Queensland old age allowances:
Railway and Works Minister Leahy now disburses the
charitable endowments of his much-reconstructed State ;
and is wearing his finger-nails down to the quick in a
mighty effort to fatten his charges on half rations.
Every possible move is being put forward to take back
the grudged weekly ss. from the aged poor—conceded
some two or three years ago without special
Parliamentary sanction. In a number of instances
the pensions are thrown with violence on
his hands by old folks unable longer to
tolerate his “police visitation” annoyance. The per-
sonal remarks of some of the hoofy callers would land
them forcibly in a horizontal position on the pavement
if addressed to younger generations. One perambulating
mass of intelligence wound up his strictures on a refined,
but unhappily poor, female acquaintance of mine,
recently, by informing her that, “Av yez have aven a
twinty-second cousin in th’ counthry, he’ll be med sup-
port ye ; th’ Guvvermint can’t be doin’ it all th’ toime for
yez, ye know.” Later, when the same genius learned
that the distressed gentlewoman had a son-in-law, earn-
ing enough to wallow in the luxury of a new suit every
four years, he grew passionate and slammed the gate,
and proceeded to interview the peeping neighbors con-
cerning the family history of his victim. The next time
I call round her wav, no doubt the poor lady will be able
to show me skin-flint Leahy’s demand for a refund of
the whole £3 10s,—the blessing of the Philp Government,
showered on her down to date.

28 Renwick-street, Leichhardt,
Sydney, November Bth, 1902.
Dear Sirs: My eldest daughter
suffered very much while residing in
Central Queensland from general weakness.
I procured Scott’s Emulsion for her, and in a
few weeks she became quite strong and hearty
by its use. Mv younger daughter, Ruby,
seven years of age, suffered from bronchitis,
but, thanks to Scott’s Emulsion, she has quite
overcome the complaint and is equally well
and strong.
Yours faithfully,
(Signed) Edwin Collins.
Messrs. Scott & Bowne, Ltd.,
.483 Kent-street, Sydney.
HAS IT EVER OCCURRED TO
YOU? That all disease is at owner’s
risk ? Has is also occurred to you that there
is a way to avoid a disease—if that disease be
of a wasting character, weakness or bron-
chitis—a sure way, not a doubtful way?
That way is by taking Scott’s Eniu’sion.
Has not one who has seen the cure performed
just proved it to you ? It is not necessary to
tell you about bronchitis, nor is it necessary
to tell you about the weakness that some-
times attacks children. The whole thing that
you want to know is contained in the infor-
mation : What is the remedy ?

The Slippery Absentee.
The incurable weakness of Parliaments in not
saying what they mean has cost the S. A. Govern-
ment a considerable reduction of revenue, and
dealt a nasty blow to the advanced principles of
taxation. S. A., being then a progressive place, im-
posed a land-tax of in the £, in 1884. In 1894
it doubled the tax on all land over the value of
£5OOO in possession of one individual or cor-
poration, thus making the S.A. Company and
other big landholders take a share of the
country’s burdens; and, at the same time,
it increased the tax of every absentee owner
by 20 per cent. Thus the small absent
capitalist paid Id. plus one-fifth, and the big one
(and nearly all absentee owners are big ones) paid
a penny plus one-fifth of that. So the matter
stood till last session, when S.A. tried to do
something towards meeting its liabilities by
adding Jd. in the £ all round to the tax. Thus
the small resident person pays §d. in the
and fairness would seem to demand that the Fat
one should pay (as before) double, and, if an
absentee, one-fifth extra on his total. Neither
point, however, was expressed in last year’s Act,
and the former one has never been pressed, so that
Fatness carries only its extra |d. burden. Thus
whilst in 1894 the rates used to be -|d. on the
small man and Id. on the big one, they are
now fd. and l£d. So the big estate no longer
pays 100 per cent, extra, but only 67 per cent.
The absentee penalty, however, being a percentage
on the total payable, would seem, by all the prin-
ciples of common sense, to be automatically ad-
justible so as to rise or fall with the taxation
borne by the resident. But Parliament didn’t
say so, and the whole Supreme Court of S.A. has
just decided that it must go by what was said. Thus
the large absentee owner has to pay for this year
l|d. plus one-fifth of a penny in the £, whereas it
was certainly intended that he should pay l|d.
plus one-fifth of l|d. In the test case on which
the point was decided £35 of revenue and the
costs went in one pop. Probably the S.A. Legis-
lature will put the matter right during the present
session, but the absentee landlord gets distinctly
ahead —for one year, at all events.
Westralian Unionism.
“ J.E.W. ” whites in a doleful strain from the
West:—
“ R.M.’s” optimism (Bulletin, June 6) regarding the
Westralian Labor party’s prospects for the Federal
elections is hardly justified by facts. Unionism as a
political force has made no advance in the Golden State
since 1901; rather it h:.s gone backward. The miners,
who account for most of the Labor vote, have split into
two unions bitterly hostile to one another, and the
A.M.A., the younger of the twain (immeasurably the
stronger on the Golden Mile), is avowedly non-political in
character. Moreover, half the miners at Hannan’s are
not organised at all. These, with the rest of the
non-unionists, form the majority of the State’s
working population. They reckon they have had
no cause to admire the way the labor movement ha* been
run. The blatant individuals at the helm have been
engaged for the last 18 months in quarrelling among
themselves and setting the unions they misrepresent by
the ears till many ardent Laborites have become utterly
disgusted. The State Labor party, too, has been largely
a failure. Excepting Mulga Taylor, they ratted on the
railway men during the big strike of two years back, and
they have since been solid supporters of the finicking,
dilettante, mostly conservative Walter James. Now
for “R. M.’s ” boasts categorically. Mahon I
pass as being safe for Ooolgardie, but Fowler is a
long way from being the certainty “ R.M.” prophesies.
Perth is not a Labor seat, and Fowler triumphed by the
aid of the Foreign-trade majority and the weakness of
his opponent. He has not been an overwhelming success
in the Reps., and a moderately strongman—o. J. Moran,
for instance—would beat him handsomely. At Kalgoorlie
the Labor crowd will want a good man and complete har-
mony in its ranks in order to beat Kirwan, and it doesn’t
look as if it will have either. Certainly the calibre of the
people mentioned so far as probable candidates is such as
only to excite mirth. For the Senate the chances of
the Labor people’s returning even ope member don’t
appear too bright. De Largie, as a member of one of
the big contending unions, is looked at askance by a
good percentage of unionists, and his thoughtless asper-
sions on the crippled Moorhead are remembered against
him. He is not unlikely to be beaten in the Selection
ballot. The other probable Labor candidates are as hope-
less a crowd as Kirwan’s prospective opponents, and it
would appear that the Westralian Labor party exhausted
itself in Hugh Mahon, Pearce and Dalgleisii. The
mediocrity of the others is wearisome to contemplate. A
man like Watson or W. M. Hughes would be a godsend
to the movement, but there is no celestial visitant in
sight yet.
. .
Acting-Chief Justice Stephen, whose healthy
down on perjurers is commendable, has made a
back-door attack on a supposed member of the
tribe. A vvitness, the husband of a plaintiff,
made irreconcilable statements on oath about a
bill-of-sale. The Judge could have committed
him to stand his trial for perjury. That course,
however, entails trouble on the Judge, and calls
for the judgment of a jury, which often disagrees
with that of the Judge. The Acting-Chief Jus-
tice, therefore, without taking trouble or risking
a jury’s opinion, applied his authority as Presi-
dent of the Court and sent the presumed prevari-
cator to prison for three months for contempt.
The situation is not satisfactory. If, in the
Judge’s opinion, the witness committed perjury,
he ought to have been committed to stand his
trial for perjury, and the jury would have had its
say on the question. By calling supposed prevari-
cation contempt, and summarily dealing with it
as such, the legal procedure in respect to perjury
is set aside. Perjury is rampant, no doubt. But
every contradiction in evidence is not perjury.
Intermittent memory is a common complaint.
That is why a jury is needed to determine what
perjury in a particular case is: To allow a Judge
to call contradictions contempt, and under that
term to punish, solely at his own will, what he
regards, and says he regards, as perjury is a
serious innovation.
+
John Darling, S.A. Opposition-leader and more-
or-less real Premier, has an idea that, as Australian
State debts fall due, Britain should guarantee
the renewal loans, Australia agreeing to give
Britain in return a preferential tariff of 5 per
cent, besides £500,000 a year extra as a naval
contribution. This is the sort of especially
noxious idea that requires to be stamped flat.
Australia has already got a habit of leaning up
against anything it can get at, and to hire Britain
as a new financial leaning-post would about
destroy any little scrap of self-reliance that
remains. Further, it would give Britain a new
hold on us ; the Government that hacks our P/N
has a right to put the bailiff in, suspend our Con-
stitution, and collect our taxes, if it has to pay the
P/N for us. Further, if Britain guarantees our
debts as they fall due for renewal, and Australia
pays £500,000 extra subsidy in consideration
thereof, that means that, for many years, we will
be paying about 5 per cent, and from that down
to 2 per cent, for the use of Britain’s name, which
is a very high rate. It would be much cheaper to
start right here and now, and put the £500,000 a
year to a sinking-fund for the repayment of the
debt. All the financial dodges and side-steps on
earth aren’t equal to the simple device of just
paying the dashed old debt.
Maoriland’s multifarious anti - gambling
leagues have spasmodic fits of energy ; they do a
howl against the tote about once a year, and then
relapse into somnolence again. At the annual
meeting of the Christchurch branch (which has a
credit balance of 10s. 5d.) “Tommy’’ Taylor
lamented that the Anti-Tote Bill was always talked
out in Parliament, but he intended that there
should be a division next session, although he
declined to say how. As a logician, Taylor col-
lects the loaf. He said that a speculation in
wheat was not gambling, because the profit was
the result of forethought; but he denounced gold-
dredging specs., which also require forethought, in
scathing terms. Any chance spec, in which the
speaker is interested isn’t gambling, apparently.

Sydney Telegraph did its friends con-
nected with the “great natural producing indus-
tries” a bad turn when it declared that the
railways were charging much more for carrying
papers, which still go at one-fourth ordinary
parcel rate, than for carrying milk. Not only, it
seems, is milk carried at a ridiculously low and
utterly unprofitable rate, but “empties” are
returned for nothing; and there is to be a row
over it in Parliament. It is simply another case
of the “natural” industries, whose barrackers
are always prating self-reliance, leaning up against
the State, and dipping a hand into the State’s
pocket for a huge subsidy. And the worst of it
is, the man who milks the cow doesn’t get the
subsidy. Even the man who owns the cow doesn’t
benefit largely. They get the skimmed milk ; the
rich landlord takes the cream.
HUMAN POISONS
Continuously being made in each of us! Their fatal nature l
Why they are made I The diseases they cause I How to
render them harmless 1 Their antidote 1
Life is maintained by a process of waste and renewal. It is as essential to life that the tissues of
the body should continuously die as it is that they should be continuously renewed. To the un-
initiated this may sound strange, but any doctor or scientific man will tell you that the tissues of the
body are in a constant state of decay, and that the decayed or dead matter must be removed, or it will
accumulate and cause disease or death.
THE KIDNEYS AS CLEANSERS.
It is the function of the kidneys to remove, in the form of uric acid and urea, a large proportion
of the waste matter caused by the decay of the tissues. It is the failure of the kidneys to efficiently
remove uric acid and urea which causes people to suffer from Rheumatism, Grout, Neuralgia,
Lumbago, Backache, Sciatica, Anaemia, Gravel, Stone and Bladder Troubles. The
most common indication that the kidneys are not doing their work properly is
PAIN IN THE BACK.
A much surer test as to whether the kidneys are in thorough working order is an examination of the
urine. If some urine passed the first thing in the morning is placed in a bottle or covered glass, and
is allowed to stand for twenty-four hours, and it is then cloudy, or there is a brick-dust like sediment in
it, or if particles float about in it, then you may be sure that the action of the kidneys is impeded by
weakness or disease, and no time must be lost in restoring them to effective vigour, or uric acid
1 oisoning in some of its many forms will be the result. When you suffer from Rheumatism, Gout,
Neuralgia, Lumbago, Backache, Sciatica, Anaemia, Gravel, Stone or Catarrh of the Bladder, you are
suffering from
URIC ACID POISONING.
But there is no reason why you should continue to suffer from any of these complaints. It is oniy
necessary to restore the kidneys to health, and all uric acid and other urinary poisons will be duly
expelled from the system in a natural manner, Since the discovery was made (about thirty years ago)
of the remarkable power possessed by
Warner’s Safe Cure
to cure all disorders of the kidneys and to restore to vigorous health those vital organs, any person
who now suffers from any form of disease caused by failure of the kidneys to efficiently perform their
office, suffers needlessly, because a course of Warner’s Safe Cure will speedily restore him or her to full
blooded health. Warner’s Safe Cure is an antidote to all poisons formed in the human body, owing
to incapable action of the kidneys or liver.
Mr. Murray L. Allen, of 4 Marsden Terrace, Clifford-street, Goulburn, writing on the last day of
the old yea.r, says : —“I have had a bad attack of inflammation of the kidneys and calculus (stone),
both of which yielded to the power of Warner’s Safe Cure.”
Remember that pains in the back, painful passing of mine, inflammation of the bladder, torpid
liver, cloudy urine, pains in the back of the head and neck, rheumatic pains, swellings of the body,
all are Nature’s means of indicating to you that your kidneys are not able to do their work properly ;
and also remember that Warner’s Safe Cure will speedily and permanently restore your lost health.
Warner’s Safe Pills are a gentle cathartic, and assist in accelerating the cure.
A treatise containing full particulars of Warner’s Safe Cure and the diseases it cures will be sent
post free on application to the Australasian Branch, H. H. Warner and Co. Ltd., Melbourne.

formation that out of 444 customs prosecutions in
the Commonwealth up to date, 210 of the swindles
have been in N.S.W.
In the N.S.W. Legislative Council, on
division, Mr. T. M. Slattery’s amendment on the
Address-in-Reply was supported by one member,
Mr. T. M. Slattery. Questionable if “Tom was
doing splendidly ” that time.
Melbourne Age now speaks dolefully of
the Irvine-Bent-Shiels Govt, as a worn-out and
paralysed organisation which has exhausted its
usefulness, and is too tired to call Parliament
together and get on with its work.
Up North, Philp and co. are working up
a £lO,OOO “Defence Fund” against the coming
Federal elections. What Fhilp and co. want to
defend they don’t say definitely ; but presumably
it’s the Kanaka and the customs swindle.
There was a saving of £lOOO in the cost of
Hansard in one year in S. A. as the result of the
cutting down of the membership of the two
Houses. And if the Council had been abolished
the saving would have been much greater.
London Daily News on the Irvine Strike
Bill:
Its terms remind one rather of some of the more
ferocious efforts of Continental reactionaries in our own
part of the world than of the usual attitude of Australian
authorities toward such questions.
N.S.W. Treasurer Waddell reckons that
the Sydney Freetrade daily newspapers have so
far had £50,000 in subsidies from the State—in
addition to a high protective tariff against Vic-
torian, Queensland, S.A., and other outside
papers.
George Reid’s latest Yes-No is that he is
opposed to the Naval Subsidy agreement, but is
going to vote for it. Also, Reid wants to know,
with great bitterness, why Judges of the Federal
High Court are not to enjoy pensions as State
Judges do, his own party, whose policy he is sup-
posed to direct, having voted against the pensions
in his absence, but presumably with his approval.
AN INHOSPITABLE COAST.
Why they are leaving! Some say because of the legislation for shorter hours and more pay.
We think it is because the leading features of the country are forbidding.
In the case of the Vondel, the Dutch vessel
that Premier Jenkins Iscariot lost his head over,
Chamberlain felicitously expressed a fact. “ Aus-
tralia is a federation, not a federal league,” wrote
Joseph, in snubbing the effort of Jenkins to assert
“State rights.”
The secretary of Sydney Trades and Labor
Council complains to the D.T. of the absence in
the local Arbitration Act of power to appoint, as
temporary members of the court, experts in special
trades. But clause 23 of the N.S.W. Act does
give that power.
Broken Hill is such a good place to get
out of just now that, as soon as the Arbitra-
tion Court had finished taking the evidence of the
important witnesses, it set out for Adelaide and a
bath, postponing counsels’ speeches until it could
get back to Sydney.
The Mullah Abdullah of Somaliland sent
an insolent message demanding to know what the
British wanted in his country .—Cablegram. And
quite a lot of people, when they wake and find a
burglar in the house, insolently ask him what he
wants. This world is full of insolent questions.
A man working on Wilcock’s Gladstone-
Rockhampton railway contract, giving evidence
in Rockhampton police-court, the other day, said :
“ I get 7d. a yard for cutting earth to build up
the centre of the railway, and the best men can
only shift six yards a day.” The sub-inspector of
police corroborated this statement. He said he
had made inquiries and had ascertained that men
employed on the railway works were only getting
3s. and 3s. 6d. a day !
Commonwealth Ministers are said to be
hatching a scheme for shifting the Federal capital
from Melbourne before the end of 1904. Knowing
that Parliament will refuse to vote large expendi-
ture, a proposal will be made to erect temporary
wooden buildings at an expense of less than
£20,000.

Tnis was done, of course, at the request of a depu-
tation of Townsville Fat Men. But Philp and
co. profess to believe in egual rights—irrespective
of color. Why then this injurious differentiation,
as Spruce Myth would say ?

Assembly are unanimous in their opposition to
