Sydney Labor Council has received a
straight challenge to declare its fiscal attitude,
the wire-netting workers having asked its assist-
ance to secure a 10 per cent, duty on wire-netting.
Foreign competition in the trade has reduced the
number of local employees by one-half, and their
wages from £3 to 30s. a week. The Bulletin
trusts the Council will decide sensibly. There
cannot be a fair-wage policy without Protection.
At Toowoomba (Q.) Quarter Sessions, re-
cently, two men were charged with robbery and
violence. The undefended prisoner was duly
found guilty, while his alleged mate in the crime,
who had legal advice and defence, was duly
acquitted. Cases of that description are very
frequently met with, and it goes to show more
than ever that a Crown that attacks an unarmed
prisoner with the best legal. weapons is a very
cowardly and objectionable Crown. Let it be
asked for the 946th time : When is the Law going
to equalise matters by appointing a public de-
fender as well as a public prosecutor ?
One of the jokes of the Owe’Sullivan
management of the State-owned Fitzroy Dock
(Sydney) is the habit of charging accident pay
against the job, however small, that the employee
was engaged upon, when the accident took place.
As a result, in one case, somebody was served
with a bill for £3 for repairs to a boat, and £l6
16s. accident pay to a man who fell down while
the job was in progress. The struggles to collect
the £l6 16s. were quite unavailing.
New motions, of which notice has been
given in the Australian Parliament: By Mr.
M'Millan, to amend the Customs Act of 1901 and
the Immigration Restriction Act (details not
given, but probably intended to make things
easier for the swindling importer and the colored
fellow subject). B.v Mr. Fowler, that the
Commonwealth should take over exclusive rights
of bank-note issue. By Mr. Chapman, that the
Commonwealth should take over the State rail-
ways and tramways.
A most unkind thing has been said by the
N.S.W. Public Works Committee to the See-
Owe’Sullivan Government. It has deliberately
dug up the old, villainous swindle whereby the
Federal customs duties on materials imported for the
construction of public works (works that are paid
for out of loans) are charged to loans, and then the
proceeds of these duties, when refunded by the
Australian Government, are put into ordinary
revenue. It calls on the State Government to
abandon this profitable swindle —this just when
the Government is much more anxious to find
new swindles than to give up old ones.

About an old
Bill:
Robert Stout, now
Maoriland’s Chief
Justice, probably has
a faint memory of a
Bill which bore a
family likeness to
Irvine’s infamous
travesty. It was
some twenty years
ago, and the M.L.
Legislative Council
was told that de-
vastating strikes
were going to hap-
pen. Promptly the “Threats and Molestations Bill"
was brought into that Chamber of Death; in an
off-hand way it was explained that the measure
was designed to advance the principles and prac-
tice of law-and-order, and it slithered through with-
out debate—the Council was a very dead-corpse
House in those days. Stout seems to have been caught
when he wasn’t looking ; anyway, he took charge of
“ Threats and Molestations ” in the House of Reps., as a
matter of form, and the Thing was introduced. But a
large and comparatively young man named Seddon, from
the West Coast, bawled at it so vigorously that for the
first time the Thing really got attention, and one or two
older members said it wasn’t what they had supposed.
By this time Stout had read it, and thought it over, and
he ended its career by moving that it be thudded under
the table. And it was.
Queensland income tax is administered
curiously. Wonderful how many wealthy people
contrived to show that they worked their proper-
ties at a loss, or at less than a profit of £lOO, in
1902. There is talk of grazing farmers worth
£lO,OOO or more, getting off with the payment of
10s. One of Queensland’s few wealthy men, a
man who derives not one penny of income from
personal exertion, but who allows his children
£lOOO a year each for pocket-money, is freely
mentioned as having escaped with a 10s. tax.
Gormly, M.L.A., has a motion to lay
before N.S.W. Assembly that all the money
obtained now by selling State lands (at £1 an
acre) should be used to buy back (at perhaps £2O
an acre) large estates which were formerly given
away, or else sold at 10s. or £1 an acre. But until
there is a graduated land-tax which smites big
estates so heavily as to make them impossible,
this resumption business leads to very little, It
is a struggle to bail out a leaky boat with a
funnel, and no one has even thought of corking
up one end of the funnel.
Arthur Rae writes
In your issue of Thursday (2/7/’O3) you state that I am
the Labor nominee for the Senate elections next Decem-
ber. Such is not the case. The last Labor League
Conference decided (hurriedly) to run three candidates
for the three Senate vacancies, leaving the P.L.L.
Executive to find the money and organise the fight. For
various reasons it was subsequently decided to run only
one Labor candidate, and the three men who had been
nominated (of whom I was one) withdrew their nomin-
ations. Fresh nominations for one candidate instead of
three have since been invited, but, as the nominations
have not yet closed, no one can predict whom the choice
will fall upon.
N S.W. Rep. Ewing on Kyabramapoo-
trism :
We should pay special attention to Kyabram, because
it is clear that she holds the master-key to proper
administration, which is competence combined with
economy, though unfortunately she holds it in a drunken
grasp. But, although her speech ma3’ be accentuated
with hiccoughs, which speak of a recent debauch, and
her breath redolent of beer, we should hear wnat she has
to say. Kyabram is entitled to speak on the subject of
political debauchery and repudiation, because she has
herself repudiated, and knows the horrors of the situa-
tion. She has sinned, but she is determined that no one
else shall do so.
One of Eden George’s bitterest complaints
against Premier See was that lie had been promised
a set of the N.S.W. Historical. Records, value£4o,
and had not received them. Premier See explained
that he had in the bookbinders’ hands a set, value
£2 12s. 6d., which he would forward to Mr.
George when bound. How Mr. George could
appraise the value of the Records at £4O Mr. See
didn’t know. Meanwhile, the public is pretty
tired of the ex-Government supporter who says
the Government promised him a pair of boots or
a gridiron, or something, and deceived him on the
matter.
The Victorian railway men are supposed
to suffer a percentage reduction on all wages over
£125 a year. But the Government deducts the per-
centage from all wages over £lO Bs. 4d. a month.
The month a railway man makes £lO Bs. 4d. he has
the percentage docked. The next month he may
be put off duty part of the time and only make
£5 —but the amount docked previous month isn’t
returned. Consequence is that though the deduc-
tion legally applies to wages of £125 a year it
illegally applies to wages much below that figure.

“I do not think it possible,” reports the
Victorian Inspector-General of Works, “to
eliminate Government stroke on Government day-
works.” Perhaps the system does want altering.
This Inspector-General is paid by the day (or the
year—by time, anyhow), and so are all his officers;
and it seems that they don’t get satisfactory
results. Suppose, therefore, the Government tries
the contract system so far as the bosses are con-
cerned ? Here’s a job, estimated to cost £5OOO.
Suppose the Government called for tenders for
“bossing” it? It’s always said to be the work-
man who can’t be trusted to work by the day.
Why isn’t it just as possible that the fauJJ; lies
with the boss ?
In Westralia, in accordance with* the
principles of high finance, one public depart-
ment always pays another for services rendered.
It, balances in the end, going from one Govern-
ment pocket to another and back again, but it has
its risks. ' The other day it was discovered that
Railway Commissioner George, finding water
plentiful on the goldfields, had been buying soma
millions of gallons, from private sources, at 45.,
because his department thus saved Is. per thou-
sand on the ss. it would have had to pay
for water from the State’s great Coolgardie
Water Supply, which has cost the country nearly
three millions. Minister Rason, on finding
it out, sort of forgave George.for his “strict busi-
ness principles,” in buying in the cheapest market,
but, as every cent paid to outsiders is dead
loss to the Treasury, George was ordered not to do-
it again.

August 19,1901.
Mr. S. A. PALMER,
•v . Agent for Webber’s VITADATIO.
Dear Sir, —I wish to add my testimony to the value of
VITADATIO. For over five years I was a great sufferer
from Liver Complaint, Indigestion, and General Weak-
ness ; I was unable to follow my usual occupation, and
for three years had been under several medical men ; I
have also taken courses of almost all the well-known patent
medicines, without any beneficial results. Whilst living
at Maryborough, about two years ago, I heard of the
wonders being performed through the agency of VITA-
DATIO, and, after making enquiries, I decided to give it
a trial, which I did, with the result that at the present
time I am fully restored to health, and feel it my duty to
let others know what VITADATIO has done for me.
I will be glad to answer any enquiries about my case
personally or by letter which must have stamped
addressed envelope enclosed.
I remain yours gratefully,
WILLIAM STEWART DRUMMOND.

Late of Maryborough.

THE
Australian Art Association
desires to notify Subscribers and the Public
generally that the OFFICIAL painting “The
Opening ot the Commonwealth Parliament,”
by TOM ROBERTS, which is to be presented
to the Nation, is being completed by the artist
at the Imperial Institute, London, where final
sittings are being obtained from the Prince
and Princess of Wales and Suite.
The, following extracts from correspondence
are published for general information :
From The Australian Art Association to the Rt.
Hon. Sir Edmund Barton, Prime Minister:
“ I enclose a pamphlet issued in connection
with the sale of reproductions of a black and
white drawing by Mr. Charles Nuttal, which it is
alleged has the official recognition of the mem-
bers of your Government. Will you be so good as
to inform me if the production referred to HAS
the official approval of your Ministers.”
Reply from the Prime Minister:
“Mr. 0. Nuttal’s picture of the ‘ Opening of
the Federal Parliament’ is NOT under the official
patronage of members of the Federal Government.”
NOTE— Hr. TOfl ROBERTS’ Picture will not
be finished until SEPTEMBER next. Reproduc-
tions will be obtainable EARLY NEXT YEAR.

“ 0.” on Queensland politics :
There is some speculation in Queensland political circles,
as to what will happen to the Independent Opposition
party during the approaching Parliamentary session.
Now that 0. M. Jenkinson is back in the he will
probably make some effort to resuscitate the old “ third
party, which he led after the retirement of the late
\V. H. Groom. Anyhow, the Independent Opposition
consists of seven pledged members—Jenkinson, Blair,
Summerville, Fogarty, Cooper, Hodge, and Plunkett—a
factor of some importance in a House of distracted
Ministerialists and growling Laborites. The tendency
seems to lead to a combination of Labor, Independent
and malcontent Ministerialists. Labor-leader Browne,
ex-Labor-leader Kidson, Jenkinson and several ex-Minis-
terialists have expressed opinions favorable to such an
alliance, and if the Labor men will take the others in,
the chance is that Philp’s sun will go down in mud.
The idea of the State as something to
cadge from has just bubbled up again in Perth,
where the A.N.A. has applied for the free grant
of a valuable block of land, in order to erect a hall
in which to do its spouting and its mutual
greasing. Lands Minister Hopkins didn’t have a
block of land worth £lOOO or so to give away just
then, but he promised to “see what he could do
for the A.N.A., and communicate with it at a
later stage. ” And as he has also promised to do
what he can for some people who want to hold a
national show, and for the Dog and Poultry
Society, and the Civil Service Association, and a
fey friendly societies, it will probably be worth
some local member’s while to watch what Hopkins
does do in this matter of giving away large slices
of.public estate. In no eastern capital has the
State hung on to as much land as it already
requires for its own uses ; with the result that the
rent bills are already something enormous. And
Perth will go the same way if it doesn’t look out.

SPORTING NOTIONS.
The Grand Nat. Steeplechase drew probably
the biggest winter crowd that Flemington has ever seen,
although Saturday was so cold that the horses hoofs
were clogged with ice. The day proved exceptionally
cold for punters, too. Grey horse Grafnell was thought
of by the crowd in general as a “ green ” four-year-old
that, previous to winning the Maiden Steeplechase a
week earlier, had jumped badly, and sometimes refused
to jump at all. Of all the horses in the race the grand
son of Grafton was the most expected to fall, and whilst
friends of the stable were backing him down to 12 to 1,
the unenlightened public put their money on a dozen
others—and lost it. Grafnell jumped faultlessly, won
easily, and put up a time record —6.3lJ—in doing it. He
is the third grey who has scored the big trick over the
big sticks, and the third winner of the Maiden to bring
off the double event.
Seeing that Grafnell was an undeniable race-
horse among ’chasers it is more than likely that even Sir
Harry with a “ stand up ” couldn’t have beaten the young
’un. Both of them 4-year-olds, they were weighted within
21bs. of one another, and the grey was the more clever
jumper, anyhow. Still, Scobie gave the favorite credit
for being able to get round the course, and it now remains
to be guessed whether the foolishness of putting a dazed
jockey upon the “ good thing ” was, or wasn’t, responsible
for i s utter failure. An hour before the field went out
for the Steeplechase jockey Sutherland had been carried
down the course in the ambulance waggon, stunned by a
fall from Irate in the Doutta Galla Hurdle Race. It
seemed out of the question that Sutherland could ride
Sir Harry for all he was worth in the next race, and the
word went round that Paddy Regan was to have the
mount. Nevertheless, Sutherland did ride Sir Harry.
His shaken nerves were said to be all right again, but the
result seemed to belie the supposition. Sir Harry, with the
resurrected jockey in the saddle, shaped unpromisingly
from the start, and at the fourth jump they came to grief.
Then the public, for the most part, pinned the remnants
of its hopes to Freedom, Error and Anchor. Anchor
never made a pretence of being able to get near tbe front,
and Error was clearly pumped half a mile from home,
but hardv little Freedom, the victor of 1901, humped his
burden in the van with the audacity of a light-weight.
Good old Freedom! Until they got right into the
straight the chestnut was holding his own with Grafnell.
The other grey horse, Burghos, had dashed up to the
pair and stayed with them for half a minute, then he
dropped back whilst Grafnell went ahead. Freedom was
beaten and the multitude lapsed into melancholy.
Scobie’s good things for the G.N. Steeplechase
are very untrustworthy. Last year, he had the first
favorite in Royal Mail, but the Mail fell, whereas his half-
trained stable companion, Kiota, surprised the trainer by
jumping safely. This year, Scobie reckoned Sir Harry an
expert fencer, but he repeated the Royal Mail disappoint-
ment.
* * * *
Though stable connections gathered a goodly
pile through Grafnell’s win of Grand National Steeple
(a prominent Sydney politician is said to have procured
the bulk of the coin), turf scribes, touts, racecourse
“guns” and the like wouldn’t have him as a probable
winner in such company. They all had his displays at
Caulfield and Williamstown in mind, for he shaped then
as if he had never seen a big fence in his life ; but in the
Maiden Steeple at Flemington Grafnell didn’t put a foot
wrong, and settled Wongleburnie, Killarney, and others
nicely. Said that Grafnell’s owner hesitated about
“ accepting” on Thursday last week, and wouldn’t have
done it had mt a prominent “book” prevailed upon
him. The horse’s success meant getting the bulk of noses
out of dire trouble, as everything thought to have a
chance was coupled in doubles with the winner of the
G.N. Hurdles apart from the money invested straight
out— particularly on the second nag, Freedom.
♦ » * *
Successful sire Grafton was much in evidence
Saturday. Three winners, Grafnell (G.N. Steeple) and
No Name and Famous (Jumpers’ Flat race and Flying
Stakes at Warwick Farm) are his get.
♦ * ♦ *
The 19 starters in Y.R.O. Grand National
Hurdles didn’t include one mare. Three were stallions
and the rest geldings.
* * * *
The advance of the ’Murkan jock. Yankee
rders have won the English Derby last three years :
Reiff on Volodyovski; Martin, on Ard Patrick; and Maher
on Rook Sand.
* * * *
Early morning watchers of the gallops at Rand-
wick last year, when hot favorite (Sir Harry) for last
Saturday’s G.N. Steeple was, being trained by his then
owner, Tom Lamond, dubbed the nag the “ ’bus-horse,”
because of his lumbering gait and clumsy appearance.
Lamond bought Sir Harry from Mr. H. 0. White for 40gs.
Present owner would want a heap more than that to part
with him.
* * * *
Speaks much for the quality of the compara-
tively small amount of lepping material N.S.W. owns,
when Sydney-trained nags win two successive Grand
National Steeples. Victoria has 20 ’chasers to the Corn-
stalk State’s 11. Twelve of the home cracks lined up,
while two came from N.S.W., and one each from Tassy
and S.A.
* * * *
V.R.C. Grand National meeting prize money
totalled £6625. Principal winning owners were T. Blunt
(Grafnell) £1652, J. N. M’Arthur (Marmont) £1575, J.
Scobie £737, and Rupert Clarke £5ll.
• * * •
The Miller stable scored its first win at the
meeting in the final event—the Footscray Steeplechase.
This'was the Miller consolation stakes, "and it introduced
an element of humor into a meeting remarkably free from
blood-curdling mishaps. The African (heavily backed)
and Emms ran for Albert Miller. When The African
waltzed along in front, his stable companion, in the rear,
came to grief, but the mare was immediately on her
legs again, and she proceeded to steeplechase on
her own. At first her intentions were strictly
honorable. The riderless mare jumped obstacle
after obstacle, gaining ground steadily, without get-
ting very near to her friend The African. Then
Emms made up her mind to win the race anyhow. She
ran round the jumps, sneaked to the fore, and led The
African to the winning-post—a good exercise gallop. The
black horse won by a head from Sweet Alice, who, by the
way, showed a vast improvement on her form of the pre-
vious Wednesday.
• • • •
How they do drop. Sensational winner (Ampier)
of A.J.O. Epsom Handicap, a y«ar or two since, was sold
in Melbourne a few days ago for £5O. The nag was
backed for a heap of money down to a short price and
was supposed to have gone wrong a day or two before
the race and his backers tried their best to unload.
Failing, they were compelled to hang on to their money
and win, in one or two instances, small fortunes.|
* • * •
If the mid-winter successes of a racing stable
were a reliable guide to its “ form ” in the coming Spring,
one could safely predict a great time for the Cup horses
that Scobie has in hand. Leaving the jumpers out of
consideration, the running of Fairy States at the
Flemington Grand National meeting goes to prove the
excellence of Scobie’s lot, seeing that he didn’t think the
gelding worth entering along with Emir, F.J.A., Free
States, Hauturier, Malster, Miltiades, Pliable, Sinnang,
United States, and Sweet Nell, who are all of them
engaged for both the big events. But the Fairy States
case is not the first instance of Scobie having trained an
angel unawares. He had no high opinion of Clean Sweep
a month before it took the Melbourne Cup, and it may
be that in not entering Fairy States for the principal
Spring handicaps he threw away his best chance of
winning the double. He never had the gelding fit till
quite lately. Last Spring and Summer Fairy States
passed for a third-rater with a turn of speed. In the
light of last week’s performances it stands revealed as
a good stayer. It might have got into both Cups at a
light weight.
* * * *
The V.R. 0. handicapper unconsciously ‘ ‘ tipped ”
the mare Carolina for next Caulfield Cup when he gave
her 7.7 for the Melbourne Cup, or 131 b. more than the
rival handicapper has given her for the Caulfield event.
One map was afraid that the aged mare, who has run.
only twice (unplaced) in five years, might be another
Revenue, whereas the other man reckoned her a hopeless
case. Carolina, by Trenton-Caroline, is in Leslie Mac-
donald’s stable, where the seldom seen Revenue and
Kinglike munch the corn of idleness.
* « * *
Rt racehorse nomenclature. Perhaps the hardest
name for the bookie to grapple with when calling the
odds belongs to a hurdle gee-gee in South Australia with
the awful name of Wakawhawhai. [Knew a horse called
Popocatepetl once.— Ed.]
* * • •
Dan O’Brien has great hopes of pocketing the
New Zealand Cup this year with Heroism, a N.S.W. bred
nag by Grafton out of a Carbine mare. So far, however,
the moke has done nothing out of the ordinary.
* * * •
M.L. ’chasers this season are below the calibre
of those seen out for some years past. Few of the 28
engaged in the C.J.C. G.N. Steeple are anything ap-
proaching first flighters. The G.N. Hurdles (35 entries),
however, includes some really good cattle.
* * * *
During the demolition of the fateful “Cathe-
dral ” wall at Flemington (Vic.), recently, workmen dis-
covered a snake in the fence, which was another and per-
haps the last kill that will be chronicled at that particular
spot.
* * * *
J. J. Miller’s Sporting Pamphlet for 1903 comes
as a reminder that the weights for the Cups are out, and
the names of the future winners with their performances
to date are included in the little pink-covered book afore-
said. Why the book should be called a Pamphlet is a
mystery which only Miller can explain, but by any name
it would be as valuable —or ruinous—to inquiring punters.
Also it is fully charged with sporting records, some of
which are rather amazing. For instance, an American
horse, named Heatherbloom, is credited with a high
jump of Bft. lin. at the Chicago Show last year. A speed
record for 10 furlongs was scored last year by an Australian
horse in England. Our old friend Australian Star covered
the distance in 2min. 2 l-ssec. on the Alexandra Park
course.
• • * *
“ J.Q.” : Charters Towers J ockey Club’s annual
meeting was a considerable success, although Ikey Mo.
was not even allowed to look over the fence. Though
the prize money was less, the tote tax greater, entries
and admission receipts less than last year, the club made
a larger profit, the amount being £9OO, as against
£669. Attendance-profit would probably have been
greater but that a cycle meeting clashed with the second
day’s racing. Still £3400 more went through the
machine than last year : biggest dividend was £8 9s. for
10s., and the average odds for 15 races of moderate and
small fields panned out 6 to 1 on winners.
* * * •
Crack nag Purser’s defeats at Warwick Farm,
Saturday, as well as at Hawkesbury a week before, and in
Melbourne two or three times recently (always carrying a
good load of coin), suggests that trainer Bill Kelso lacks
his dead dad’s astuteness, otherwise he’d give the horse
a spell (which is, apparently, badly wanted), and bring
the great miler out to sweep the boards at the weights
now being allotted.
* • * . *.
One time Kogarah (Sydney) owned Lieutenant,
now 12 years old, followed up recent successes by winning
Newmarket H’cap (5 fur.) at Ascot (V.) last week and
looks good for a few more successes yet—he got over the
ground in fast time of 1.5}.
• * * *
Imported champion pacer Almont, atop of bis
mile record set up two or three weeks ago, threw two
miles behind, at Ascot (Vic.) t’other day, in 4.23},
3 3-ssec. better than M.L. Ribbonwood’s figures for the
continent. Monday last, on same track, he tackled
world’s 3m. best (6.55}, owned by ’Murkan mare Nightin-
gale and accomplished 10 years ago), and, never breaking
once, reduced it by 5} seconds.
•*• * *
Lepper Comboyne’s quest for V.R.C. Maiden
’Chase during past five years is worth considering—he
has been after it four times. In 1899 he was of seven
starters, and among the four that fell. Next year saw
him an absentee, but in 1901 he lined up and again came
a cropper. Last year he got over all the leps and finished
fourth, hjit latest occasion saw the fence that brought him
down first time of trying for the coin prove disastrous
once more. The fact is unique. Crack M. Mooney, who
brought Grafnell home Saturday last, had the mount in
both first and last efforts.
* * • »
Westralia has gone stark staring mad over
horse-racing, and financial results of last month show
that the Boulder Club’s exchequer has been swelled by
about £l5OO ; and at Kalgoorlie, thanks to good manage-
ment and excellent weather, the local club raked in over
£3OOO as a result of a two-days’ meet. Still, sport on
goldfields was always of a fluctuating kind—either a
wild cat or a bonanza.
H. W. STEVENSON,
The Famous English Billiard Champion.
Photo, by C. J. Kirk, Surrey, England.
Up-country mare Faithful, by Metal from
Devotion, fairly spread-eag-led a field of 14 in Pace Welter
(1 mile) at Warwick Farm Saturday last. She is now
better than a possible Epsom Handicap winner. Prior to
reaching Randwick she gathered Scone Opening Handicap
(7.8), Stewards’ Mile (9.8), New England Opening Handi-
cap (lOst.) and Boxing Day Handicap (9 fur.) with 9.12.
Faithful only has 7.9 in the Epsom and 7.5 in the
Metropolitan.
• • • *
Methods of N.S.W. Trotting Club stewards are
peculiar. Meeting before last, deeming trotter Joe’s run-
ning suspicious, they “cautioned" connections of the
nag. At last meeting Joe won, whereupon the stewards
“ carpeted ” all concerned—horse included—and disquali-
fied the lot on a charge of inconsistent running. Joe was
in a peculiar position that day, for had he not won at all
the same punishment probably awaited him. Situation
is absurd. Offenders were acquitted when they should
have been “ dealt with,” and “ dealt with ” when quite
innocent.
* * * *
The utter uselessness of A.J.O. stipendiary
stewards was glaringly manifested at Warwick Farm
Saturday. Flying Stakes saw Lord Cardigan run up to
Famous in the straight and look sure to beat him had
Famous not bumped and forced t’other towards the rails.
The further they travelled the more was Lord Cardigan
crowded out until the two passed the post, up against the
judge’s box, locked together. Verdict was half a head
win for Famous. Owner of second nag protested and all
onlookers thought he would certainly score, as rules
provide that if one horse jostles another so as to prevent
him winning, whether intentionally or otherwise, the
offender shall be disqualified. Stewards, however, met
and dismissed the objection. Surely they’ve dug their
grave this time.
* * * *
Queensland Turf Club’s report reveals a short-
age of £1092, mainly attributable to increased exactions
of the Government which meant a drain of £l4ll over
and above that of the previous 12 months. Full tax on a
turnover of £83,148 for last year was £3949 Bs. 9d., as
against £2538 Os. 9d. on a turnover of £101,521105. in
1902. Prize money distributed for 20 days’ racing was
£14,460, while year before £15,179 went for 21 days’
racing.

Two stake-winners at Belmore (Sydney) Cours-
ing meet, Saturday—lan Hamilton and Puppy Wrangler
—were thought so little of before opening of present
season that then owner gave former away, and latter was
sold cheap—a gift almost. lan Hamilton suffered from
some trouble which was thought incurable ; he is litter-
brother to crack Sydney hound Great Heart, runner-up
in Australian Waterloo Cup, t’other day.
* * * *
Crack English billiardist, H. W. Stevenson, in
Sydney present week, isn’t quite the dashing operator
heralded, and certainly hasn’t the go nor anything like
the presence of Roberts, who looked and acted the cham-
pion, and, though Stevenson is a remarkable player and
excels his comparatively huge countryman in many re-
spects, he doesn’t impress to the same'extent. Strangely
enough opening day of match against Memmott saw
Stevenson’s biggest breaks for afternoon and evening
sessions 197 each, and Memmott excelled himself—so
consistently did he score—making 117 once. Second day
brought perhaps one of the finest displays Sydney has
seen. At one stage Memmott (getting 1800 in 5000) had
gathered within three of what Stevenson accumulated,
but then latter let himself out, and, after making a run
of 123, fairly staggered onlookers with 416 in 26min.—the
result of nursery and drop cannons and hazards accom-
plished in masterful fashion. Next came 101, and the
afternoon’s play ended. At night the Englishman
carried an unfinished break to 97, and followed with 100,
120, 90 and 347, the first 200 of last-mentioned compila-
tion was put together in 12min. Memmott’s best efforts
were 99 and 78. Proceedings closed with Memmott 444
short. Stevenson’s night score, 833, was accomplished in
the exceptionally fast time of lh. 4min., and he averaged
69.5 for breaks. Final occurred Monday night. Steven-
son was consistent always, but Memmott fell off mate-
rially. Afternoon saw first-named bring an unfinished
break to 136, and, later, put 92 together, followed by 210
occupying 14min. Other runs were 109 and 71. Mem-
mott’s best effort was 52, and he made only 241 to the
visitor’s 835. At night, Stevenson didn’t trouble much ;
he had the game well in hand. His highest contribu-
tions were 144,142, 110 (continuation from 71 in after-
noon), 79 and 77. Memmott sprung a surprise by piling
up more than his opponent; he scored breaks of 157,152,
80, 73, 66, &e., playing an all-round game. Ultimately,
Stevenson won by 373.

The Sydney Rowing Club, perhaps the most
conservative of the clubs in the N.S.W. Rowing Associa-
tion, has decided, by the narrowest of majorities in
general meeting, to instruct its delegates to support
the proposal for the inclusion of manual oarsmen in the
amateur definition. A proper representation on equal
terms with the ever-conquering Victorian Eight should
soon be an accomplished fact.

“ Scrip ” : Met an elderly one-time athlete who
said he knew Bob Fitzsimmoiis’ people in the “ Old
Dart ” before Bob was born. Strongman was, singularly
enough, the maiden name of the pug.’s mother, and her
brother was champion wrestler of Truro, Cornwall. Fitz-
simmons pere was a policeman in the same town for over
25 years.
* * * *
“F.”: Sandowisra (B. 2/7/’O3) is all right—
with a competent instructor to put you on the right
track. Tried it on my own for a while; some benefit,
not , much. Went to a Sydney instructor; very great
benefit, an all-round increase of muscle, a much better
appetite and a much-needed addition to weight. The
system depends on the exercising in turn of special
muscles by special movements in special positions, and it
is almost impossible to learn these from any chart.
Combined N.S.W. Country, last week, demon-
strated that rural Rugby is particularly good class or that
Metropolitan players are short ot high-grade form, in
which case they’ll surely go under to the Maorilanders.
Up-country forwards fairly ran over the city pack, never
giving them a hope in the scrums, and all through ex-
hibited marked superiority. Visitors had too much
thew, were too husky and too well-conditioned, also they
were too doggedly determined and sustained in their
work, for the dandy players of the city.
• * * *
M.L. football team, en route Sydneywards,
went under at Wellington, Saturday, to 15 drawn from
that city, Manawatu and Wairarapa, by 14 to 5. Said
that game was a “ mere scramble played in drenching
rain and a sea of mud”; nevertheless result detracts
from high standing given the combination by M.L.
authorities and football soribes generally. Still ’tis
cabled that M.L. Rugby Union secretary insists the
team is the strongest all round that has ever left that
State. Hawke’s Bay crack, Carlson, is coming; he scales
14st., is a good wrestler and a more then ordinarily cap-
able exponent of field games, and looks the strong
athlete from neck to heel.
* * * *
Fifty-third Queensland-N.S.W. football match
(Saturday) attracted 10,000 onlookers to Sydney Agricul-
tural Society’s Grounds despite rainy weather. At
outset the ground was a sheet of water which soon
became slushy mud. Display wasn’t much better than
mediocre, for slipping and floundering rendered tactics
impossible and they were consequently not often
attempted. The ball got very greasy early and became
greasier the longer it was in play. Preponderance of
brawn was with N.S.W. whose reps, averaged in the
neighborhood of 13st. while visitors scaled all round about
2st. less. Operations of Queenslanders took the eye
as smarter, cleaner, and more reliable than that of the
opposing 15. Ruck play saw visitors all there and not
anything inferior; they also frequently bested the home
side for the ball. Queensland lacked the aid of Spragg,
Dixon and Gralton, who were left behind ; had that trio
been available things might have ended t’other
way about, for the Northerners stayed better and, during
concluding quarter, gave the Cornstalks a very sorry
time. N.S.W. led through greater part of the game,
though backs frequently mulled badly and forwards fell
far short of expectations. Match terminated 11 to 6
against Queensland. Evans, captain of the defeated
team, played brilliantly. One-handed St. John took the
ball with wonderful dexterity and accuracy, and his
kicking and tackling rarely missed. Three-quarters
White, Wickham, Oxenham and Putter, and forwards H.
Judd and “ Paddy ” Carew were conspicuously good for
winners. Queensland or N.S.W. combinations of last
year would have fairly laid over Saturday’s victors.

Somebody lias taken the trouble to tot up
money given for bike races in Victoria during past season.
Sum approximates £6600 as against £9O less previous
year.
* * * *
Manner in which ancient negro Starlight made
an exhibition of Victorian crack middleweight Jim
Higgins in Melbourne recently, demonstrated how boxing
has declined. The lank-limbed black was never a
champion in his best days, but, humping the weight of half-
a-century,and with hardly any preparation, easily excelled
his youthful opponent, getting upper-cuts, swings, and
hooks home at will almost; he had Higgins bleeding
from the nose and lips early, and brought a stock of blows
into service that fairly overwhelmed and bewildered the
youngster. By-and-bye nature deserted the colored
man and then the pair sprawled often, for Higgins’
strength had petered out also, though he recovered
quickly. First one and then t’other was down, then
they w'ere on the floor together. Time for the twelfth
round saw Starlight too exhausted to go on, and Higgins
got all the money but none of the kudos.
• * * *
Arthur Cripps (holder) and “ Soldier ” Thomp-
son (ex-holder) of Australasian middleweight pug supre-
macy, had another rousing tussle for the title and the big
end of £2OO at Sydney National S.C. last week, and
Cripps won, but not near so easily as previously. The
fourth round was the “ Soldier’s ” from end to end ; he had
Cripps very mixed through losing his head as the result
of being sent to the boards thrice, once the effect of a
'rastle throw (the “garrote”), unintentional, appar-
ently, and consequently overlooked by the referee.
Cripps played “ lead ” for several rounds in a disrespect-
ful manner, and Thompson scored well in consequence.
Subsequently, Thompson was never in it, though all
through he displayed remarkable grit and stamina, and
took his thumping manfully. The first round once looked
as if ’twould be the last, for a clever baulk brought
Thompson over, and a right chop On the chin made him
sag at the knees and rock in his tracks ; then he clinched
and scuffled the 3mins. through. “Corners” saved him.
The 17th brought the end. Cripps landed right and left
on either side of the now very weak Thompson’s chin,
and then flung him from him, an inert mass, to the
boards. The crowd w'aited till Thompson recovered, and
cheered his spunk again and again ; he deserved to win,
but Cripps was too tough-hided, too strong, and some-
what too clever.

ought not to mean growing weak and feeble. It

A letter from Broken Hill, where the
water-famine is
Everybody said there was no need for immediate alarm.
Some said a month’s supply above the silt, others six
weeks’. The lowest estimate, the local paper’s, was a fort-
night. On the fourth morning went to the bath-room as
usual, got into the altogether, had five minutes’ Sandow,
turned the tap, waited, and said something; then, as
nothing but an answering gurgle came out of the shower,
had to resort to the good old-daj's’ system of dry-blowing.
And as we dry-blowed that morning we sadly summed
up what it all meant. We could hear the slow thud-
thud of the mills still, but we knew that inside
24 hours they must stop and our five or six
thousand men be idle. Yet even now, after several
days of it, we don’t realise what it all means, or may
mean. So far we live in hope of something turning up;
Broken Hill has infinite belief in its luck. The weather
has helped us, no doubt. Believe in M’Oarthy, the rain-
maker ? Well, none of us like to say we don’t. When we
find ourselves coming to the conclusion that it is rot, we
pull ourselves up with a jerk. It might, we tell our-
selves, be just on the point of raining—it might take only
just a very little kick to turn the scale in our favor, and
if Mac. can’t do much, he may at least be able to supply
that small kick. So we hope on, because for days past it
has looked, and still looks, as though it might rain if it
had ever so little help. Meantime the mines are doing a
little to keep the pot boiling. Not only Broken Hill, but
S.A., has been swept of tinsmiths to make condensing
plant for the Big Mine. The Trades Hall is being used as
a ration-issuing depot. The coupon system of strike days
has been revived. Necessaries of life are being bought on
a large scale ; the State issues a coupon, and the holder
can draw on these stores to the face value of the coupon.
Later on, no doubt, when the system is better developed,
a certain quantity of labor will be required in return for
the coupon ; that will suit Broken Hill better, for these
are not the sort of men who ought to be driven to accept
charity.

Victoria is an allegedly Christian State,
run by the Irvine-Bent-Shiels Government—a
monkey-on-a-stick combination, manipulated by
the Kyabramic crowd of agricultural sweaters,
who, in the name of economy, reduce the railways
to wreck; reduce the old age pension dole below
the barest sustenance level, and cut down the
hospital subsidy. And this is from a Melbourne
paper:
An old man named Thomas Orowley was found in a
pitable condition in a house in St. Hilda yesterday. The
police removed him to the Melbourne Hospital, where he
was found suffering from acute rheumatism. Questioned
as to his age, Crowley.said cheerfully, “I don’t know.
Put me down as 60 or 70 or so. That’ll be near enough for
me.” There were no beds available, and, considering the
patient’s state, there was no alternative but to take him to
the gaol hospital. Crowley was accordingly lifted back to
the cab, and was just about to start for the gaol, when a
breathless attendant ran out of a distant ward, signalling
violently. The cab was stopped, and the attendant
approached. “There’s room,” he announced, “ a man’s
just, died ! ” Crowley’s face lighted up. “ I’m in luck,” he
said, adding reflectively, “ but what about the other
man ? ”
An interesting cullud genelman, calling
himself Clance Smith, has fallen into the hands of
the Melbourne police, on a charge of uttering a
bogus cheque for £167. Clance Smith appears to
be a darkie person of bright enterprise but
neglected education. He lately came to Mel-
bourne from S’Africa accompanied by another
young man (white), who gave him a few pounds to
put into the cattle shipping business. The
ingenious Clance had drafted sundry circulars and
testimonials for himself and the new “firm,” one
document being intended
to notirfle the Stud Farmers of Australasia that Vetrinary
Surgeons Smith and Lowe were returning to the coloneys
off Australia as stud farmers South Africa, arriving at
Melbourne on ore about the end of June.
Also, the “ vetrinary cattle shipper” prepared a
testimonial from “the M.R.Y. Surgeons College,
Melbourne, to our brother M.R.V.S. Clance
Smith,” and a letter from an omnibus company
manager, who had
rftuch onner in reciomending Mr. Clance Smith, veterinary
surgeon, to any colonys, inasmuch as he was skilful, and
very keen in detacking disease among animals.
These can be regarded as humorous inventions,
but there is another document, which amounts to
something like blasphemy, signed “Colonel
Hutton, on behalf of officers and men.” It
expresses the keen regret of Hutton and his com-
rades in arms at parting with their well-beloved
vet. The cullured pussen who could thus bracket
Hutton and his men together, and take their
united names in vain, seems to deserve condign
punishment.

The latest news of the “ Flitterers,” the
Bendigo apostles of the alleged new religion, is
that they have disbanded in New Guinea, the
gospel having proved inadequate to the needs of
the time. “ Some of the missioners are returning
homewards, but no news is obtainable of the
whereabouts of the gentleman who was treasurer
as well as leader of the band.”
If the man on the Land wishes to remain
there in peace, he should employ W. N. Willis,
Land Agent, 21 Bligh-street, as his Agent. No
results, no payments.*
At Adelaide, last week, there died George
William de la Poer Beresford, in his 80th year.
He was appointed clerk of the S.A. Assembly at
the initiation of Responsible Government; and
held the office for 30 years. He was connected
with the Marquis of Waterford’s family. »That
family has been on several occasions represented
in Australia. Years ago, one member of it? used
to lean up against the Bull and Mouth Hotel,
Bourke-street, Melbourne. He was a remittance
man, so long as he remained in Australia. Very
wisely, he always leaned against the one old pub.,
lived and drank there, cashed his draft there, and
was looked after like a dissipated younger son,
whereas the remittance man of many pubs has
no standing, and is occasionally run-in. Another
member of the Waterford family was buried, years
ago, in the old Sandhills Cemetery, Sydney.

Who remembers R. Burdett-Smith,
C.M.G., “Cupid” Smith, as he was known!
Somebody, evidently, judging by the “In
Memoriam” notice which some faithful rela-
tives insert in the daily papers. Good “fat”
notices they are too, bringing joy to the Counter
Clerk’s soul and containing much important
information. The last one, for instance, date
June 2nd:
Burdett-Smith.—ln memoriam of the Honorable R
Burdett-Smith, 0.M.G., M.L.0., who died at his resid-
ence, 203 Macquarie-street, Sydney (in the very prime
of life), July 2nd, 1895. Universally esteemed, and
deeply lamented by troops of friends in England and the
colonies. Extract from letter of an ex-Governor of New
South Wales :—“His loss is a great one to the colony.
He was a true patriot, and his name will be handed down
to future generations in the colony as a pure-minded,
eloquent, and able statesman. London, April 23rd,
1897.”
But why is no mention made of that never-to-be-
forgotten day when “Cupid" marched so nobly
down King-street, Sydney, holding a 275. 6d.
umbrella over Alfred, Duke of Edinburgh ’

Auckland (M.L.) has got all sorts of ideas
into its head since Kipling called it “ Last, lone-
liest, loveliest, exquisite, apart. ” For a long time i t
has considered that the rest of Maoriland was con-
structed solely in order that it might have some-
thing to sit on the end of. Just now, when the
rest of the State is taking a more or less intelli-
gent interest in the doings of the world, Auckland
is watching the earth solemnly revolving around
a dock that it built at great expense. It is at
present trying to get a handful of the £20,000, that
is to be spent on patching up some of the rotten
bulwarks that the Motherland has left in these
seas, expended at its dock in order to rub some of
the desperate loneliness off it. Auckland is
like the absurd, person who bought a fine gold watch
and then invested in a suit of fine clothes in order
to let the watch feel at home. Then this man
went and stayed at a swell hotel, and bought a
motor-car so as not to have any incongruous spots
on the premises. Then he found that he would
have to let the motor-car on-hire in order to avoid
insolvency. Similarly Auckland first built a dock
that it didn’t want, in order to prove its supe-
riority, and then it went and spent a whole lot of
money on an; Admiralty House that no one will
live in, so that the Admiral who didn’t come
might stay there while his ship which didn’t
arrive was being repaired in? the dock that wasn’t
wanted. If hard times hadn’t loomed up roun
the corner Auckland was quite prepared to add a
few Pyramids and things, and cherish them as
ornaments. But hard times have loomed up with
a great loom, and at present Auckland is. concen-
trating its intellect on the necessity of getting
bent warships to come and get mended, and in
advertising for lodgers for its Admiralty House.
Unusually sensible remark in Sydney
Telegraph :
We are not going to scare over the alleged migration
of capital from any country. We have heard about it
too often. Never in the memory of the oldest in-
habitant has any rational legislative proposal been made
in this State without capital girding up its fat loins and
threatening to leave by the next boat.
It should do the Telegraph editor good to take
that home and sleep on it.

There is considerable barney up North,
among the people who love the Kanaka, over the
redistribution of Federal districts. With the
help of the Townsville and district vote they
thought they ought to be able to win Herbert
from Bamford, and Kennedy from M‘Donald.
But the Townsville anti-Labor vote will be coun-
terbalanced by the strong Labor vote at Charters
Towers, the two centres being now included in
Kennedy; while a State Labor electorate or two
have been tacked on to make up Herbert’s neces-
sary strength.

London Spobting Life states that the veteran
swimmer, Marquis Bibbero, intends attempting to swim
the English Channel next month (August). The cheerful
old person is only 80 years of age. Madame Isaacsen
who has already made one failure, also means to have
another try, and may possibly accompany him. Whether
it is proper for them to do this without a chaperon is a
moot point.

* * * *
The management of Maoriland Caledonian
Association, formed with a great flourish of trumpets a
few years back, seems to have fallen into a sad state of
disrepair. It was to control the games held by associa-
tions in both Islands, put professional running on a
proper basis, and all the rest of it; but though it really
apparently intended to set about smashing things at the
outset, it merely fell back in its arm-chair and peacefully
went to sleep. Now some of the strongest organisations
are clamoring for the registration of all pro’s, and
threaten secession if the inert management doesn’t infuse
a little more vitality into its proceedings and bring about
much-needed reforms.
* * * *
“ J.A.C.” : Be English Derby. It was first run
on May 4, 1780, when Diomed won, and was named after
the twelfth Earl of Derby. The hottest favorite to win
was Ladas, 1894, with 9to 2 on. Galtee More, 1897, had
4 to 1 laid on him. The longest-price winner was Jeddah,
1898, at 100 to 1. Hermit’s Derby, 1867, was run in a
snow-storm, when the odds were 66 to l. The largest
field was in 1862, when 34 started, Caractacus, another
outsider at 66 to 1, winning. It is remarkable that the
quickest time was done by Volodyovski.an inferior horse,
1901. He holds the record (2min. 40 4-ssec.). The King’s
horses, Persimmon and Diamond Jubilee, are next best,
with 2min. 42sec. each. The slowest Derby was in 1856,
when Ellington won. He took 3min. 4sec. to cover the
mile and a half. “Jim” Kobinson, whose first victory
was achieved in 1817, rode the greatest number of
winners, coming in first on six occasions.
* * * *
“ Le Chasse ” writes : Bulletin seems doubtful
as regards the late A. R. Robertson’s connection with
Newmarket Handicap winner Coronet. The fact is
Coronet was the property of A. R. Robertson and his twin
brother, D. M. Robertson. In addition to the substantial
whacks the brothers had out of their father’s es.ate
(about £40,000 each), they pocketed something like
£30,000 in bets over the Newmarket flutter, but money
ran through their hands outwards, like water through a
sieve ; they were fleeced right arid left and up and down.
When Coronet won the Newmarket the brothers started
an “ open house ” at Newmarket, and everyone who cared
to do so could swim in champagne, for a few hours, but
after that not a bottle could be found in the whole
district. So red-hot did the brothers serve up Coronet
for the Australian Cup that, had he won, the back of the
ring would have been broken. But certain children have
their dusky daddy’s luck, and it was the brothers who
walked home by the dirt road, while the gay and festive
“ Tommies ” rolled home in cabs, smoking shilling cigars
and blessing Moses for having come to the shnickin’ and
bringing good luck along with him. When the ring
breaks the bottom will fall, out of Sheol.

The son of Ham looms large on the pug horizon.
Negroes Sam Johnson and M’Vey are the only men in
sight likely to succeed Jeffries or Corbett (if the latter
beats the huge Los Angeles boilermaker next month for
world’s supremacy), and there are many who think either
black is certain to whip Corbett, and would go mighty
' close to accounting tor Jeffries. Joe Walcottand “ Young
Peter Jackson ” dispute world’s middle-weight honors
with Tommy Ryan, who draws the “ color line ” on them.
Another thick-lipped, ebon-hued one (Joe Gans) is un-
questionably the world’s fore-front light-weight, and
rated by some the greatest pug. of African extraction
that has ever lived.

“Z”: He was a racing man, and there was a streak
of good nature in his horsey phiz, that moved me to men-
tion the “ Higher Life ” to him in an offhand manner.
“Woteher drivin’ at, anyhow?” I asked him if he
thought “ running Dyes ” was fair to his fellow man ? He
finished his whisky leisurely ; I could see he was reflect-
ing. Then he made a sign to the beer-squirter, and,
turning to me, said, gravely and deliberately: “Dash
and blow my fellow man. If my neddie wants a bag of
oats will Mr. Blooming Fellerman buy ’em ? Not much !
So if he gets on to my horse and falls in the muck, that’s
his filthy funeral- I won’t be even a mourner.” Then I
gave it up. It was evidently quite useless to mention
the “ Higher Life ”to a racing man. Once a man owns
race horses he quickly descends to making money.
“ Adelaide ” : The Vic. lacrosse team, so badly
beaten here, stayed on for a match with Sturt, the
leading local club, and only won after a desperate battle
by 12 to 8. Four of the S. A. 12 were in the club team,
and made things very warm on the attack. In fact the
Yic. back men are only moderate, or else local forwards
are something wonderful, for in neither match could the
former prevent the latter from getting in right on
top-of the goalkeeper. Rudd “kept” very finely,
but he never saw most of the shots that passed
him—or a good many of those that he saved.
It seems likely to this writer that the win from N.S.W.
did the Victorian men a lot of harm. When you are
winning, anyway, you can show lots of science, and the
Melbourne men were so cracked up all the week by press
and others on their “ combination,” that when they got
here it was passing run mad. Certainly, their catching
was wonderful, but it is quite useless to pass to any man
unless he is free. If you can only launch the ball at a
group of two, one of whom is an opponent, surely the old
way of landing it 100 yards up the field is better ?

“Uppercut”: The correspondent who girds at
Sandow’s system is like the other man who denounces
athletics generally and imagines that strength varies
as the mass. Those who have closely followed
any sport know well that the man usually called
“fine looking” by frequenters of the block is
about as shapely as a bladder of lard when he
gets his clothes off, and just about as effective. The
crack athletes of Australia—aquatic, footballing or pugil-
istic—give no indication of their outlines in street attire ;
and what a surprise was Sandowhimself to the uninitiated 1
The value of exercise, whether in the form of athletics or
a physical development system, is properly appreciated
only by those who have gone through it; or, better still,
those who have had the misfortune to put their force
against those who have gone through it. Sandow’s own
particular system differs but little from a score of others,
and is probably no better. But Sandow made one great
discovery which previous physical culturists overlooked
—the psychological aspect of exercise. He was the first
to realise that improvement is proportionate, not to prac-
tice, but effort, in muscle as in everything else ; and by
impressing upon his disciples the necessity of keeping
their minds concentrated upon their muscles, he has been
able to turn out, with half the actual amount of exercise,
a number of youthful Herculeses able to tear to pieces
the old-time men who were wont to swing dumbbells
mechanically for an hour a day as they yarned about
their best girl to their room mate. Thorough believer
though I am in Sandow’s system, I fancy that "it will lead
to the deterioration of athletics. Muscle binding is the
inevitable result of such development. I would wager
that Sandow himself couldn’t turn off 100yds. in 15secs. ;
while, as for boxing, he might have a herald in front of
him to announce his blows.
Bacillus Londoniensis.
To hint in this 20th century that the effects of
travel may be harmful and not beneficial, is to
incur at once the reproach of an illiberal mind, of
a narrow view and of a parochial patriotism. Aet
many things which look disgustingly intolerant at
a first sight can be seen, in their true light, as of
great value after a closer examination. Perhaps
“travel,” in spite of its fetish position in the
minds of modern men, is not always a sure path
to knowledge and breadth of mind ; and perhaps
a partial prohibition of travel would not be to
nurse ignorance and foster petty prejudice ? There
have been great men who grew to noble estate
fixed within petty boundaries ; there have been
great rulers and reformers who owed nothing to
foreign intercourse. On the other hand, there
have been many instances where the travelling of
its rulers has led to a nation limping pitifully in
“tardy apish” fashion in awkward imitation of
some other land, and individuals have come back
from a grand tour with no better intellectual
broadening than a knowledge of the price of
chickens in Rome and the rates of ’bus fares in
London.
* * * *
The question is, so far as Australia is concerned,
practically as to the respective value of sturdiness
and of polish. The travelled Australian may gain
some polish. It is inevitable that in very many
cases he will lose some sturdiness. The man who
hasn’t a country of his own, only the beginnings
of a country, is not safe abroad. He has no great
national traditions of his own to stay him up, no
splendid history behind him, no very glittering
prospect before him. He is naturally tempted by
the glories of other lands, their riches and their
promises of great rewards, to forget the poor little
patch, he came from and to become un-Australian ;
that is, unless he is of the temper of a Cincin-
natus, and human steel of that sort is rare.
Study the average Australian politician before
and after his first visit to London, and the
effects of “travel” will be at once apparent.
Before, he is prone to speak of Australia
as the first consideration; afterwards—after
he has figured in a court suit, and grasped or
seen close to his grasp a title ; after his mind has
been infected with the dazzling possibilities of
the advancement to be won in richer fields—he is
all for “ the Empire.” That may be a good thing
for “ the Empire,” but it is a very bad thing for
Australia.
* * • •
The most vigorous period of Australia’s as yet
infant life was undoubtedly that time when “ the
Empire” was not in fashion, when British politi-
cians had not “discovered the colonies,” and
when Australian public men had to be Aus-
tralian because there was not much en-
couragement to be anything else. National
advancement only comes from an entire
self-devotion and a complete centralising of
a people’s energies on its own affairs. Indeed it
may be said that national advancement needs
what, from a cosmopolitan point of view, may be
called a “narrow” outlook. The eyes must be
in the boat. Energy must be centred in the fur-
row that is before the plough. The Australian
who is to do any good for Australia must be for
Australia, and Australia alone, all the time. It is
ridiculous for the Australian public man to
prate of a “broad view,” of “wide Imperial
responsibilities,” of the “great destiny of
the English speaking races,” whilst he neglects
Australia to seek the distinction of out-
side favor. Such an attitude is first cousin
to that of the man who neglects his own
family while attitudinising on platforms for
the advancement of humanity and the promo-
tion of the millenium. Australia cannot offer a
very glittering field to the public man as yet.
Its domestic politics attract but little attention
in the outside world, though they can promote
the happiness of 4,000,000 of people. It can hold
out no prospect of great riches to its servants,
who must accept as part of the price of faithful
service the consciousness of duty rightly done
and a country nobly served. Only to the truly
great man, whose mind is not wholly sordid,
whose intellect is sufficiently great to lift
him above the garish mummery of courts and
titles and barbaric gauds, does Australia give a
tempting enough prospect. There are such men,
whose love is for the work and not the reward ;
and they are wanted in Australia; and they need
no training in Court ceremonial, no familiarity
with the pomp of Empire, to fit them for their
work. For them “travel” is unnecessary, even
though not harmful. But Australia cannot at
first hope to breed many such men, cannot hope
to have them always available for its government.
It must store its wine in weaker vessels some-
times ; and it is these pots that are so liable to come
back cracked from London. Left in Australia,
subject to none of the temptations of the richer
world outside, they might follow the inspiriting
example of greater minds and follow the path of
Australian duty—narrow though it be. With a
knowledge of broader, easier paths, leading to
more glittering rewards, they are apt to desert
the ideal of Australia for the idol of “ the
Empire.”
* * * *
When Australia has done something for itself,
when it has redeemed its credit and tilled some
small proportion of its soil and set up its factories,
and grown to nationhood, it will be time for its
public men to consider themselves and their coun-
try in relation to the Empire. At present there
is too much work at home, and Australia cannot,
without treachery to itself, give any thought or
effort to other tasks. That is the view which
Australian public men should take. It may be a
dull, grey view ; there is no gleam in it of a
parrot’s feather, or a ribbon, or a bit of colored
glass, given with ridiculous solemnity by an
absurd ruling personage. But it has an intrinsic
nobility of its own, and a virtue which can afford
to incur the reproach of narrow-mindedness. To
it the bacillus of London is so often fatal that it
deserves to be a maxim of Australian public life
that public men should, as far as possible, be kept
away from Europe.
Queensland’s Agent-General, Tozeb, “has
warned the Federal Premier of the damage to
Australian credit that is certain to ensue ” from
the Government’s administration of the Immigra-
tion Restriction Act. Quite likely; and at last
Tozer does Australia a good turn by pointing
this out. But Tozeb goes wrong at this stage.
Administer the Act differently, he says—ignoring
the alternative course, which is to administer the
Act as hitherto, and put up with the consequences
so far as damaged credit is concerned. If a man
doesn’t want credit it doesn’t matter a straw to
him whether his credit is good or not; and so
with a country If, however, the country does
want credit, then it has to be careful to please the
man with the money-bags. And that is the al-
ternative that the Australian Liberal and Labor
party has to. face. It has the reliable word of
Tozer that either its credit or its independence
must suffer. Which does it value most—its honor
and the purity of the white race, or a loan ?

Hannan s Reward, Mt. Charlotte (W.A.), was
reconstructed 18 months ago with a 2s. assessment
alleged to be worth £15,000. It seems the assessment
for some unknown reason, only realised half this amount!
and that Manager Oorbould was not told till he had
pretty well spent the lot on much-needed alterations and
repairs. Oorbould then advised tributing the rich n arrow
leaders to keep the co. in funds, but was over-ruled and
resigned. The co.’s local attorney then appointed Henry
Nuttman to take charge, and, although co. has been pro-
ducing £lOOO of gold monthly, it has been costing some
£l2OO to produce it, and that game can’t go on for ever.
As before reported, half the men have been given notice,
and one shift only will be run. If it didn’t pay to run the
air-compressor and rock-drills two shifts, it certainly will
increase the loss to run one only. Tributors used to pay 15
to 20 per cent, royalty, according to the grade of ore won, so
that, had Corbould’s advice been followed, co. would have
been pocketing from £l5O to £2OO a month on the same
monthly yield, instead of losing that amount. This is a
case of little knowledge being a dangerous thing; Oor-
bould knew the ground thoroughly, and others didn’t.
Hence the muddle.

Peak Hill (W.A.) ia now producing about
3000 oz. monthly from lldwt. ore, and making a profit of
£4OOO to £5OOO. This should admit of a Is. dividend
every three or four months. The present manager, R.
Leslie Hall, deserves every credit for getting the mine
again on its feet. The co., which a little while ago was
£l7OO in debt, paid a Is. div. in April, and, as all profits
will now be distributed, the next div. may be expected
about August.

•'* * •
An old-time agitation has been renewed in Bal-
larat. This is a proposal for a comprehensive system of
drainage on the Sebastopol plateau. Several large and
influential meetings have been held and'every thing pro-
gressed smoothly until the question of expense was
mooted. Everyone proclaimed that the project was a
magnificent one, and that its accomplishment would lead
to a mighty revival of the mining industry, but when it
was suggested that the Ballarat people and the lease-
holders specially interested should contribute, they all
cut a very sorry figure. The only suggestion which met
with approval was that the cost should be borne by the
Government, which should be recouped by royalties on
gold won as the result of the drainage operations. Enter-
prise must be a dead letter with Ballarat men when they
deliberately put themselves in this humiliating position.
A new co., which bears the doubtful name of
the London and Globe Deep Leads Assets 00., has been
formed to take over the Loddon Valley (Vic.) properties
formerly controlled by Whitaker Wright. In addition to
the original blocks, it is stated that the co. has acquired
the “ Option ” blocks, which adjoin on the north side,
“ and the Madame Berry lead has been proved to run
through this property." From Madame Berry, Oreswick,
to Loddon Valley blocks, Moalort, is a far cry.

dend list. For past half-year there was an output of
6985 tons, netting the company £15,266, exclusive of all
smelting and treatment charges. Of this output 3731
tons gossan ore was treated at the Dapto smelters, and
3254 tons sulphide ore at Zeehan smelters, the gossan
averaging per ton lOdwt. of gold, 250 z. of silver, and 10
per cent, lead, and the sulphide 6dwt. of gold, 21oz. of
silver, and 10 per cent, of lead. Total output from mine
to date of last report was 19,477 tons of crude ore, giving
a net return of £49,617. The co. has in hand some
£ll,OOO or £12,000 cash, out of which the dividend is
being paid. All ore disposed of is sold as crude ore, there
being no concentration of any kind on the mine.

Every J. Bull concern which has yet tackled
N.T. ipining has made success practically impossible by
its riotous extravagance. It is little use offering a small
thing'to J.B. ;he answers that it isn’t worth while. To
get oyer that difficulty, nobody offers him anything
nowadays as a small thing; it is boomed to appear large,
a large price is demanded, and, if the co. is formed, the
arrangements are on a corresponding scale of largeness.
N.T. mines have, almost without exception, been handi-
capped beyond hope by this treatment. The N.T. Mining
► and Smelting Co. promises to go the same way. It has
chartered a steamer to bring out a cargo of rails, engines,
trucks, 60-ton smelter, bricks, &c., for two reverberatories,
blowers, 1000 tons coke, and a vast quantity of sundries/
A light railway line is to be laid between Yam Creek and
Mount Ellison, and generally the show is to be run on a
scale of magnificence never before attempted. They call
all this “ bringing foreign capital into the country to
develop it,” and it is the sort of thing that, itis alleged, it
would be a calamity to discourage. In the end, it is only
bringing into the country scrap-iron, rust and discredit.

‘ ‘ Salvation Hill Gold Mines ” Co., Gulgong
(N.S.W.) is in 18,000 shares at £1; working capital, £6OOO.
This mine was held by a local syndicate of 20. Then',
after sinking a lot of cash and a shaft to 210 ft., co.
was forced, owing to water and want of better hauling
appliance, to effect a London flotation, showing
its faith in the value of property by taking
one-third in shares and dispensing with the usual
cash consideration. A serviceable steam hauling
plant, was erected and work started about four
months , ago. The prospectus suggested sinking
the present shaft a further 200 ft. to prove the lode (a
main fissure formation carrying most of the gold in the
mineral) at that depth by driving and crosscutting; but,
owing to the present easy-going system of working, only
the youngest members of the original syndicate ever hope
so see that depth reached, provided, of course, that the
working capital stands the strain. The co. has an area of
about 45 acres, and local belief is that, with vigorous
management, mine might develop into a paying concern.
White Cliffs (N.S.W.) opal-field shows some of
the great things that private enterprise can do. An
English co. got hold of the central blocks of the field, and
has almost ever since pursued a policy of masterly
inactivity, doing absolutely no work itself, but levying
tribute from several hundreds of men who work the
ground. Naturally, nothing in the shape of deep pros-
pecting has been attempted ; and now, when the surface
gem has apparently been pretty well exhausted, the
State has had to “ part up ” enough to test the ground a
hundred feet or so deeper. A grant has just been passed
for this purpose. Very little stone is at present being
got on the field ; moreover, the demand for opal is re-
ported to have slackened seriously in Europe, so that
prices are not good.
At Poverty Point.
“Lily” Langtry has had a play written for her in
which there is a bedroom scene. Here Lily “disrobes"
gradually until she stands amidst a mass of dazzling
lingerie. But the Lily wears so many clothes in that act
that though she takes off enough to fit any ordinary
woman for bed she is still a clothed lady, and only her
still beautiful bust and arms are really visible.

“ J.A.0.” : They know a thing or two in China. There
the sons of actors—like the sons of public executioners—
are debarred from the mandarinate. Considering the
kind of justice a man learns on the stage, where large
estates are given away by policemen on a mere shred of
evidence, and the person who is accused by the villain is
liable to be hanged instantly, unless the funny man inter-
venes, this isn’t surprising.
Walkley, the well-known dramatic critic, writes thus
of an eminent actor known to Australians :—“ Mr.
Wilson Barrett’s Hamlet—the ideal Hamlet, it has been
said, of the British vestryman—a ten-pound householder
Hamlet.”
Sensibly decided by a German court that every person
paying for admission to a theatre has the right to a full
view of the stage, and that anyone given a seat which
hasn’t it can demand his money back. This throws on
managers an obligation to remove the matinee hat, pre-
sumably by force, if necessary. A man pays to see the
show—not the hat.
The silly, glorified walk of the London stockbrokers
(which performance threatens to be copied ad nauseam
in Australia) was utilised for biograph purposes at the
local Hippodrome. A series of pictures showing the bell-
toppered brethren toddling on their weary way was
exhibited twice daily at the Hip. with great success. All
the brokers paid their money to see themselves figuring
on the sheet, and as soon as a man spotted himself in the
picture he brought his womenkind along to admire, and
the ladies hailed his living picture with shrieks of excite-
ment.
“ Nar-Rooni ” writes: As one of the family, let me
correct a Poverty Point par (9/8/’O3). The “ portly Marie
Narelle, of Sydney,” is not a cousin of the attenuated
Kate Rooney at all—no relation whatever; in fact, it was
only within the last year or so that the two warblers
became acquainted. By birth our Sweet Marie is a Ryan
—Molly Ryan—and by marriage a Callaghan. But she
was born at Narellan (N.S.W.), hence the stage name,
which many mistake for French or Italian ; in reality, it
is Australian blackfellow. Miss Rooney was born at
Dubbo (N.S. W.). She is still studying with Santley, who
predicts much success for her on the concert platform.
Davidson and wife, leading actor and actress, having
gone into liquidation in the Divorce Court, the New York
American interviews both. Davidson says: “Actresses
are failures as wives. Give me a dish of boiled beef or
herbs where there is good housewifery. I never want
another wife with the artistic temperament. An actor
leaves the stage behind him, but an actress never does.
Her emotional scenes at home are the best in her
repertoire. Actresses are the least neat of women. They
are addicted to wrappers. They are extravagant. The
very temperament that makes a woman a success as
an actress makes a failure as a wife. She has none
of the rustic charming aloofness which is one
of the charms of another woman. When a man
protests against her doings it is neighbors, friends,
relatives, tears, acting, hysterics. She is too
strenuous for an ordinary man, who craves serenity.”
Now hear Mrs. Davidson. She says : “ Actors, as a rule,
are impossible as husbands. They are peacocks. They
are the most conceited men on earth. No one enjoys
living with a man who talks, thinks, dreams, and even
talks in his sleep, constantly of himself. He always wants
the centre of the stage. You will find the actor well
dressed, with a flower in his buttonhole, when he and his
family have had nothing to eat for two days. Mr. David-
son would buy three or four suits at a time, and wouldn’t
give me one gown. It is always a feast or a famine with
actors. They have all the sensitiveness and nerves and
whims of women, without a woman’s great tenderness of
heart. An actor has learned from his parts, if not from
his nature, the art of refined cruelty. Actors are fickle.
The exceptions are so rare that I don’t know any."

*Felo de Se,” which, we’ve been told,
Is meant for suicide,
Applies to Jones, who caught a cold,
Neglected it, and died ;
Carelessness won’t make old bones,
Nor health to us secure.
When you’ve a cold, don’t follow Jones—

Phthisis has robbed Bohemia of Davidson
([“Peter”) Simmonds, one of its most welcome
members, and Australia of oneof its most entertain-
ing writers. “Peter’’was originally responsible
for the Argus “ Passing Show ” column ; after-
wards Johnny Sandes joined him, and they
worked together till Simmonds left the Argus
and went to the Herald. Simmonds got his
earliest press training in the north of Victoria,
then took up city reporting and proved himself
one of the smartest of men at the game, first in
Sydney and then in Melbourne. He did also
some light magazine work almost as good as any-
thing done in these parts. He had hardly reached
middle age when he died, leaving a wider circle of
friends than most men can claim.

But is there now much better brew ?
Our vice goes cloak’d ; it wore no gown

What Women Should he Like.
“ Woman,” says a celebrated Spanish poet, “is
the greatest attractive force in the world. Her
step should be brisk, yet light; her touch gentle;
her eyes alternately bright and languishing; her
voice soft and low; her laugh merry and rippling;
a delightful creature, born to be happy, and to
confer happiness upon others.”
A pretty picture, forsooth ! Would that it
were true of every woman in the world. But un-
fortunately it isn’t; and all the flowery language
of all the poets of Spain will not make us believe
that more than ten out of every hundred women
attain this high standard. And that is saying
nothing in disparagement of the fair sex either.
Quite the contrary. It is only opposing a little
common sense to the fantastic notion of an
irresponsible rhymester. We think quite as
highly of women as the Spanish poet, and are
always prepared to champion them; but this is a
work-a-day world, and it is worse than useless to
shut one’s eyes to the hard facts of life, such as
domestic duties, sickness, and so forth. Here is
a brief account of one woman out of the many
who could not conform to the poet’s ideal.
‘‘For a number of years,” writes Mrs. Belle
Conlan, of 4, Parnell Place, Newcastle, N.S.W.,
22nd February, 1903, “I suffered during the
summer from acute indigestion and bilious attacks.
I was also subject to splitting headaches, which
would occasionally completely prostrate me. At
such times I would lie in a darkened room and
suffer in silence. I had no appetite, slept poorly,
and was low-spirited and nervous. Not until I
tried Mother Seigel’s Curative Syrup did I obtain
any relief. Patent medicines and tonics of various
kinds I had taken without avail. I bought a two-
and-sixpenny bottle of the Syrup, and had not
taken more than three doses of it when my con-
dition changed for the better. I continued to use
the medicine, and in a few weeks my health was
quite set up again. I can now eat well, sleep well,
and perform my domestic duties with ease—in
fact, all through the present trying summer I
have been quite free from indigestion, headaches,
and bilious attacks, thanks to Mother Seigel’s
Curative Syrup. I cannot praise it too highly for
what it has done for me, and shall always keep, a
bottle of it in the house.” Such is the testimony
of a woman who at one time was in danger of be-
coming a chronic invalid, but is now well, with
little likelihood of the return of her malady.

When Melbourne’s new “ Public Morals
League” recently waited on Vic. Minister “Jack”
Murray, to ask him to take measures to “prevent
newspapers reporting divorce proceedings,” it fell
in rather badly. Here is a portion of the verbatim.
report of the interview: —
Mr. Blyth, on behalf of the White Cross Society, pro-
tested against some reports which appeared in news-
papers. Sometimes they made horrible reading. Recently
a full report of a divorce case appeared in the news-
papers.
Mr. Murray : “ Did you read the reports?"
Mr. Blyth : “ Yes, I read them.”
Mr. Murray : “ Then I suppose that a great many
thousands of others read and enjoyed them.”
Mr. Blyth : “ The only reason I read them was .”
Mr. Murray : “ Ah, don’t give reasons.”
Solomon never gave wiser advice than that “don’t
give reasons.” The world’s holy moralists, when
they are detected coming out of a pub. with a
smell of whisky on their chin-hair, or otherwise
compromising themselves, always make the mis-
take of stopping to explain the perfect purity of
their motives. Then a crowd collects, cabs
stop, and dogs hurry up from round the corner.

“H.”: The most important discovery
ever made in the ruins of Asia was last year,
at Susa. A French expedition exhumed a huge
black stone, with an immensity of minute inscrip-
tions on it. They are the Laws of King Kham-
morabi, about 2600 years b.c. Professing to be
communicated by the Sun God, they largely ante-
date details of Moses’s Laws, in the Pentateuch.
INDICATION OF CROUP.
Hoarseness in a child that is subject to croup
is a sure indication of the approach of the disease.
If Chamberlain’s Cough Remedy is given as soon
as the child becomes hoarse, or even after the
croupy cough has appeared, it will prevent the
attack. It always cures, and cures quickly.

“J.F.D.” Listening to a Salvarmy circle
yesterday, I noted the rivalry in oratory and
bragging. Watched the man who was booked “to
follow on ” when the man who was speaking had
finished. His hands were clenched, and his whole
attitude seemed to say “ Wait till I get going,
you’ll hear something; his past life is small
potatoes to mine.” He jumped into the ring
with the force of a cyclone, and narrated more
crude and inartistic lies in three minutes than
Ananias could grind out in twelve months. And
the humor of ;it detailing his drunken orgies with
a huge pair of spectacles on ! A real beer drinker
near me assured me that he never saw a spec-
tacled man who could drink beer worth a hang.
Entomologist W. W. Froggart, in a report
to N.S.W. Minister for Agriculture, stresses the
urgent need for an up-to-date Codlin Moth Act.
According to Mr. Froggart fully two-thirds of the
apples grown in N.S.W. are damaged by the
moth, and, as a consequence £90,000 worth of
fruit, chiefly apples and pears, was purchased
last year from Tasmania, where, previous to the
passing of the Codlin Moth Act of 1888, the pest
was just as bad as it .is in N.S.W. The expert
recommends as a remedy systematic bandaging,
burning up all rubbish that might act as harbor
for the grubs, destruction of fallen apples, spraying
of trees with some arsenical compound, such as
Paris green, and astringent Act to compel all fruit-
growers to carry out these measures.
