Our House of Abdomens,
(By an infuriated Radical.)
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Within a Chamber sanctified
1 hey wallow in their pens,
Four dozen waistcoats gross and wide
Our House of Abdomens.
Above eaoh desert of white vest
A casual speck appears—
It is the head of Interest;
It is Greed’s eye that leers.
They speak with voices wheezing iow
Behind the walls of fat;
And, O, the sunsets pale their glow
Before each silken hat!
They sit like monsters out of some
Paleozoic time,
When on their pericardium
They crept amid the slime.
And so, to-day, these creatures creep,
Amid the slime they crawl,
And nose the legislative heap—
Our Lords Abdominal.
And some they take, and some they leave—
They are particular,
These porcine things that gasp and heave,
With mighty mouths ajar.
They do not stray to right or left,
But dwell there till they fall,
Entangled by Death’s fingers deft—
Our Lords Abdominal.
And then they hunt the styes of Space,
And send their vassals far,
To furnish for that vacant place
Fit waistcoat globular.
Then him they chose is furnished there
With feeding-trough of gold ;
He sniffs the viands, rich and rare,
Our cook-like Premiers mould.
He turns the heap all-satedly
With snout and cloven paws,
And we do stand bare-head to see
Them masticate our Laws.
The Bills prepared in many days
They rend in seconds few,
And O, ’tis sad to see us gaze
And see the monsters chew.
They are the Things we bow to while
They tread our Law-crops flat;
The blackened Yarra is our Nile,
Our Pharaoh’s name is Fat,
Within a Chamber sanctified
They wallow in their pens,
And O ! we show with fearful pride
Our House of Abdomens !
VIC.

H.M. : Have you ever noticed the
wide, faded, misfit raiment of R.C, boy-sliepherds?

A Melbourne sneak-thief struck an un-
usual snag or whirlwind t’other day. The
landlady of a Bourke-street pub. was surprised to
see a man rushing through her premises carrying
a large parcel. On stopping him, she "was
informed by the intruder that he had just stolen
a parcel from a cart and was trying to get away
with it. The landlady thereupon grappled with
him, and threw him on his back. On his rising,
she threw him again. Next round, he threw the
landlady, but she was soon up again, and got the
man on the floor and sat on him. She remained
on top for some time, but the under-manned
police force didn’t come, and the under dog
became desperate, crawled from beneath the
fedjand fled, leaving behind him a blown Mrs.
"public-house and a stolen parcel. Woman is
becoming a formidable social and political institu-
tion these days.

One of the funniest visitors to Melbourne
Income Tax Office was the presiding priestess at
perhaps the best-known of all the suburban
temples of Venus. She called to protest against
certain deductions being disallowed, and claimed
her schedule—made out appropriately on the
form marked “Personal exertion’’—should be
accepted. The department took exception to the
fact that she had deducted large sums expended
on wearing apparel, cosmetics, and perfumery
under the list “ Outlay entered into for the pur-
pose of obtaining money,” and she came to argue
her pomt. For a full hour the lady of pleasure
pointed out that were she running a greengrocery
instead of a resort of sin, she would cer-
tainly not waste her money on silk petticoats and
lace underwear, while plain cocoanut oil would
probably suffice for the adornment of her hair
instead of peroxide at 10s. 6d. a bottle. “ It’s a
damn|robbery, ” she asserted at length. “Do you
think I’d wear shoes like those if I hadn’t got to
and silk openwork stockings at 7s. 6d. if I was a
respectable woman?” The blushful official,
fearful of further examples of the sad lot of the
?«au er ’ s f ruc k his bell for a messenger.
bhow this lady into the next room,” he said
tactfully, “she wants to pay her tax.” “No, she
doesn’t, and she’s not going to,” cried the woman
scorned. “Do I understand you won’t allow
these deductions?” “No, madam, we will not,”
he replied weakly. “Then,” she announced,
catching up her skirts and exposing five distinct
petticoats in five distinct colors, “I’ll see Mr.
” (a very, very prominent politician), “and
you take my tip for it, he’ll give you the sack.”
So saying, she bounced off to write a letter to the
daily papers, showing how the income-tax stifles
the industries of the country and drives away
capital.

The Bushland and Her Child.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Bushland watched her poet son, and loved
him as he grew ;
She filled his mind with holy thoughts when all
his dreams were new.
Said she: “I have a priceless gift for songsters
who are young—
A fairy harp with roses decked, and golden
threadlet strung.”
She handed him her priceless gift—the gift that
thousands crave,
The harp that plays to mystic airs that live beyond
the grave;
He knelt before her splendid throne in joyfulness
and smiled,
And twain they spoke in whispers soft— the
Bushland and her child.
Said she : “Go forth, O youthful one, through all
the land and sing,
And whisper me the cadences a million breezes
bring,
The melodies they waft along, the dreamy airs
they sigh,
The croonings of the lazy wind that passes slowly
by.
“Goforth beyond the sordid few, and sweetly
sing to me
Of all the joys that lie beyond the pale of
drudgery,
The glories of the rising sun, his sinking softly
down
In gleaming seas of gold beyond the furthest
mountain’s crown.
“ Of scenes beneath a dreaming moon, the splen-
dors of the stars,
The restless roving mountain streams that drone
across the bars,
The wilderness of frond and fern where countless
creepers twine —
Of everlasting glories in dominions that are mine.
“Go forth and take this gift of mine, a fairy
harp, and strung
With threads of gold, and gaily decked, and let
your songs be sung.”
He took the Gift in thoughtless mood from her
who had beguiled
With promises and flattery a vain, unthinking
child.
And forth.he went, a wanderer, where youthful
fancies turn—
Through glories ’neath the moon and sun and all
the stars that burn,
In verdant vales, by mountain crags, and spark-
ling lakes and streams;
For Li'fe was young with rainbow tints, and rich
with splendid dreams.
Once more he stood before her ; but the fire that
burned of old
Within his youthful breast had died and left it
dark and cold.
“Oh ! mother mine,” he sadly cried, “this truth
has come to me :
That he who’d sing your dearest songs might die
in penury.
“You handed trie a useless gift that all creation
scorns ;
You decked my harp with roses, and —you set
my way with thorns ! ”
He heard a laugh—a cruel laugh—and saw a lip
that curled ;
Aside he flung his fairy harp, and faced the
great, grey world.
N.S.W.
GILROONEY.
The Wooing of Edward Riley:
A Story of Vandemonia.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Across the white-grass plain a creaking box-cart,
drawn by a shaggy, despondent-looking horse,
slowly approached the darkening forest. In the
cart, on a chest of stores, sat a hopeless-faced
woman.
The master and the man were digging a hole for
a gate-post. That is, the man, William Broome,
assigned servant, was digging, and the master,
Mr. Caleb Lefever, emigrant, lounged against the
already-erected post chewing black twist.
“ Ain’t it deep enough yet?”
“Naw.”
The late October evening was sweet with the
scent of sassafras bloom ; two young voices from
the milking yard mingled with the songs of the
bush birds; and from the rocks of the Tiers the
great shadows began to creep downwards towards
the plains. A pink-breasted robin fluttered to
the top of the gate-post and peered round the
master’s shoulder into his face with its innocent,
beady eyes. What it saw there must have dis-
gusted it, for it flew down and perched on a clod
of earth beside the toiling man. He looked at it
and smiled. The bird was reassured. It pounced
on a worm behind the clod and flew joyfully away
with the prize. Broome never forgot that robin.
“ Ain’t that cursed hole deep enough now?”
“Naw.”
Suddenly the chorus of the birds was drowned
in a discordant cataract of feminine screams, as a
slip of a girl came racing down from the yard,
shrieking and laughing alternately.
“What’s the matter with yer, yer fool?”'
growled Lefever.
“Oh, oh, oh ! Father, father, father! Ha, ha,
ha, ha ! ”
you she-devil,” said Lefever, catching
her and shaking her violently, “or I’ll cut the
hide off yer.”
“Oh, oh, oli ! Ted kissed me—oh, oh, oh!”'
and she laughed hysterically.
E EfeeS! &
and some milk slopped over the edge. At tms
moment Lefever appeared at the d " 0 -L wlth t g gJJe
“You clumsy dog, he said,
oath; “I’ll teach you to waste good milk, and
fi ß d iiey jumped a foot into the air spun round
twice, and, still keeping a hold on the bucket, fell
face down on the gravel. ... ~ j
Lefever put the gun back intheeJottage, and
then walked leisurely down the path. As he
passed the hoy’s body he stirred it with his foot.
There was no response. Then he raised his tiger-
yellow eyes and fixed them on Broome, standing
shoulders-deep in the post-hole, his face a grey-
gr»eWe must deepen that hole,” said the mur-
derer, hoarsely, taking a step forward.
Broome sprang to the surface, a look of terror
in his eyes, and grasped the iron-shod rammer.
“By Gord, master,” he said, if ya cooms a fut
nigher a’ll drop ya ! ” . ,
‘‘Come Bill,” said Lefever, in a softened
voice, “ what ails you, man ? I only want you to
deepen that hole.” . ... .
“ Deepen it ya’sef,” said the man. A cawnt
trust ya—ya dawg ! ”
They stood looking at one another. _
“ It’s growing late,” said Lefever, I hear the
box-cart at the ford. Come, Bill, we re both in it
—both, d’yer hear ? Help me to deepen the hole.
Broome regarded him with horror.
“Deepen it ya’sef,” he said, doggedly. xa
needen be a-feared o’ me I A’m no murderer,
thank Gord.” , . , ~ A
Lefever seized the spade, with an oath, ana
leaped into the hole. ,
It was quite dark when the hopeless-tacea
woman drove up in the box-cart. She called out
for Ted to take the horse.
“Ted’s cleared,” said Lefever, shortly.
“ What did he clear for?” There was ever a
suspicion of fear in her voice when speaking to
her lord and master. ~ _ ,
“He spilt the bucket of milk, said Lefever,
“ and I clouted him for it, and he cleared.”
“ For that ? ” The tone was as incredulous as
she dared make it. ,
“Yes, for that,” snarled her savage husband.
“Don’t stand gaping there. Get inside and stir
that little strumpet up to get supper, Or 111
quicken the two of yer.” .
Mrs. Lefever found her daughter crouched in a
corner of her sleeping-room, trembling with
terror. She put her lips close to the girl’s ear :
“ Nellie, where —where is Ted ? ”
For answer the girl clasped her round the neck.
They felt near and dear to one another then—if
never before. . ,
That night at supper Lefever glowered at the
three who sat together, bound by an unvoiced
tie; mother and daughter, white-faced and
trembling; the convict watching the murderer as
an experienced keeper watches an untrained tiger
that has smelt blood.
For a fortnight matters continued in this state
with.little variety. Every morning Lefever
remarked sullenly: “I must go and report that
young hound to-day ; he’s had plenty of time to
get back.” Yet ail day he .sulked round the
place watching the others. But on the fifteenth
morning he saddled the horse. ~
“Don’t ye leave the house till I come back,
he said to Broome: “and keep your eye on the
others, d’ yer hear ? ”
“ ’Ees,” said Broome.
The convict watched him out of sight, then ran
like a deer up the hill to a sandstone rock. From
this rock a particular bend in the road to the
township was visible a mile from the clearing.
Eyes as keen as a hawk’s kept guard over that
bend until a dull object passed slowly across the
grey streak and disappeared. >
“’E’s a-gawn slow,” muttered the hawk; a
can dew ut.”
In two minutes Broome had burst into the
kitchen where the two wretohed beings sat over
the fire. . ,
“Missus,” he said, “A’m gawn to gie ya t
orfice. Bolt! —d’ ya hear me ’—bolt! A’d take
ya masef, on’y a’ve bizness. Keep arf t’ road-
stick to tha crick. ’Twill bring ya oot. Y’ve
three hours. Twig ? Bolt l an’ good luck t ya.
Mr. Lefever was inexpressibly shocked on
meeting William Broome as he rode into the
township that morning. .
“What the hell are you doing here?’ he
inquired. Broome grinned.
“What am a’ doin’? Whoy, a’ wants ma
dischawge ; ’n’ a’ wants ma stoof ; that’s what
a’m a-doin’ ’ere, ma-aster. ”
“You skulker!” swore Lefever; “I’ll
take ye to the station—l’ll”
“ Naw ya wunt,” said William. “ Coom aside,
mun ; a want to tell ya summat!' ”
“Tell, and be damned!” said Lefever,
savagely. , . ,
“Tha wer’ a witness to that there burym.
“It’s a lie, you dog,” said Lefever, but his lips
turned white.
“It’s naw lie,” returned the convict steadily.
“ When ya wer’ draggin’ tha por body to tha
hole, a’ seed Nellie a-look.in’ throw the windy at
ya.”
“Not so loud, you devil!” said Lefever,
hoarsely ; “they’ll hear you.” Some men ivefe
passing. ;
“A’j wants ma dischawge,” said Broome,
sullenly, “ ’n’ ma stoof, ’n’ ma charackter, ’n’ a’
means ta have ’em.”
“All right, Broome” —he could hardly form
the words—“ I’ll do the business for you. I—l’ve
been expecting you all' the morning.”
“Ya cur ! ” said Broome. Lefever trem-
bled in the saddle.
It was dusk when Lefever rode up to the stable
and let his horse go. There was a peculiar still-
ness about the place. When he entered the
•cottage the ticking of the clock sounded like
hammer-strokes on liis brain. The rooms were in
disorder, telling of a hurried departure. Still he
would not believe, but went outside and shouted.
His voice echoed against the sandstone cliffs, and
the silence seemed to deepen. He returned to
the cottage, barred the doors, and producing a
bottle of rum from his pocket, drank himself into
oblivion. The subsequent week’s debauch of Mr.
Caleb Lefever on the “ Plains” is still a pleasant
memory to the oldest inhabitant. He treated
rich and poor alike. The whole district was
drunk for a week on rum. When funds ran dry
the cause of all the joy was put on his horse and
fifty blue and red devils chased him home.
It was late evening when Lefever staggered
towards the unfinished gateway. There was a
figure digging round one of the posts. Lefever
went to the Wood heap for the axe, and creeping
■stealthily up dealt the figure a terrific blow. The
■axe struck the ground and the Thing went on dig-
ging. It looked like Biley. Lefever carefully
measured the distance and struck again. This
time the axe glanced off the post, and the Thing
continued with great composure. It looked such
a queer object, with one side of its hat cut away,
pegging along at apparently nothing, that Lefever
laughed. The Thing looked up and grinned. It
was Riley. It only had half a face, and looked
more comical than ever. They both laughed.
Then the old dread came over Lefever, and to
avoid the Thing he stepped to the other side of the
post. There was another Riley at that side, also
digging. Lefever was annoyed, and decided to
leave them in possession. He fastened the cottage
door behind him, and after some trouble found
the slush-lamp, which he lighted. A third Riley
was sitting at the kitchen table. A dreadful sense
of suffocation began to oppress Lefever. He
backed slowly out of the room, locking the door
after him, and crept fearfully into Nellie’s room
at the back. Riley number four was standing by
the bed. With an awful yell the murderer
dropped the lamp and sprang at the Thing’s throat.
He missed it, shot beyond it, and crashed head
first into the slab wall beyond. The lamp had
rolled against some old papers and rags at the foot
of the bed, and the liquid fat formed a little
river of flame which slowly advanced towards the
middle of the room ; and in ten minutes the place
was a roaring furnace. When, from information
received, the police rode up to the “farm,”
nothing remained of the cottage but the stone
chimney, and a long search failed to bring the
mystery of Lefever’s demolished homestead to
light.
Then one William Broome was “ wanted.” For
some reason Lefever never reported Riley as
missing; it was, therefore, generally supposed
that he had perished with the rest of the family
in the flames. Broome promptly reported himself
at the station. His own statement to the police
on the occasion of his discharge from Lefever’s
service, also the fact of the latter’s presence in the
township on that and subsequent days, were direct
evidence in the convict’s favor. He also accounted
for every hour after he left the “ Plains ” ; and he
was discharged.
Nearly sixty years after an old man named
Broome confessed to a mate most of what is
written here. He said that Nellie Lefever never
saw the burial from the window. That was
Broome’s invention to intimidate Lefever; but
Nellie had heard the gun and guessed the rest.
Mrs. Lefever ultimately became insane, and for
many years, in a certain asylum, she crept slowly
round a walled yard inquiring for “Ted.” It
was the last word on her lips as she died.

Ladies in the Engine-Room.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Let’s stand back here by the boilers
Watch ’em through the open door
Trippin’ round with shoe-heels tappin’
On the noisy, iron floor.
(Mine’s the small ’un with the giggle,
Pointin’ like a semyphore.)
See the chief, all teeth and whiskers,
Showin’ ’em the way she goes.
(What about that one that’s solemn,
With the cunnin’-lookin’ nose?)
Hear their packin’ swishin ’ I swishin'!
What a blessed lot of clothes !
Shut up, Kid, yer breezy swearin’—
Where ye bin ? Be like a tomb.
Stand in here between the boilers—
They can’t see us in the gloom.
’Tisn’t every day there’s ladies
Strollin’ round our engine-room.
Now, they’re goin’ down the tunnel,
(Mine’s the little ’un as yet—
Makes good steamin’, pretty motion;
Keeps her coal bill down, I bet)
Ain’t the chaperong a monster ?
She’d drive through it —drippin’ wet.
Now they’ve stopped, and Mac is tellin’
How the Yank was killed last year
When his pants caught in the shaftin’,
Kind o’ cloth that wouldn’t tear.
Yankee Bill, he went to glory—
That was Yankee Bill’s affair.
Wish the “chief” would tell ’em other
Yarns about the flamin’ things—
Tell ’em what we’ve got to hang to
When we’re oilin’ and she swings—-
How it’s lovely in the tunnel
When the shaftin’ bends and springs.
Never mind ! They’re only women I
Very likely good ’uns too —
And they’ll never rightly savvy
What us hot-house blossoms do—
Real good women—proper women—
Much too good for me and you.
* * * *
“ Yes, sir ! Yes’m ! Them’s the boilers—
Double-ended boilers, mum.
When we’re goin ? Yes, it’s sultry.
Yes, at times we do sweat some.
Have to stoop here—them’s the bunkers;
Through here’s where the trimmers come.
“ No, miss ! No ! It never hurts us—
Easy sort of work for men.
(Seems to want to ask me questions)
Men get scalded ? Now and then.
Wouldn’t do for you to try it—
Might feel faint-like, now ’n’ again.”
* * * *
Sick of lookin’ at the boilers,
They stepped dainty through the door,
And I hear the shoes a-clickin’
On the soundin’ iron floor,
And their patent packin’ swishin’—
Hope to God there ain’t no more !
M.L.
WILL LAWSON.
The Horse-Breaking Electrician.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The man from Riverina smoked reflectively :
“ We had a chap at Burrabogie once that could
break in horses. His name was Mac Jones, but
we christened him Necessity, because he was the
father of so many inventions.
“The wilder the noddies were, the better he
liked the contract of breaking them in—used to
say it showed what he called ‘ ther complete domi-
nation of intellect over brute strength.’ He made
a special study of jibbing horses, and could tell
one at sight, and for years had gone on bringing
out all manner of curious contrivances to cure ’em.
“But his greatest invention killed him,” said
the man from Riverina, sadly. “It was like
this : He got an electric battery up from Sydney,
and he fixed it and a lot of wires and coils and
plates and things under the seat and about the
shafts of the long breaking-in gig. What took
the eye most about the thing, though, was a wire
about five feet long with a sponge on ther end
of it.
“This, he said, would shift Satan himself, if he
jibbed between the shafts, and he explained that
no hoss would keep on jibbing if he found hisself
backing up agen something that stung like ten
acres of nettles.
“Moroney, our storekeeper, had a mare that
absolutely refused to go past the station gates,
and Necessity undertook to cure that mare. He
took Billy, the black boy, with him and called him
his ‘electrician.’ M’oath ! that nig. was proud—
he’d as much as he could do ter keep from switch-
ing the current on, goin’ down ther drive.
“However, at ther gates she stops short and
sudden, and Billy got ther word ter make her git.
The shafts were long, so Billy got out over the
front-board and touched her smartly.
“ Guess Billy’d have hung on with his teeth and
claws if he had known how that mare liked bein’
electrocuted. He turned three somersaults, and
when he struck the ground the gig was out of
sight.
“ We carried Billy up to the house, and he come
round in about four hours, just as they were
bringing Necessity and the mare, quite cured,
safely home. She’d run him into a mud tank, so
nothing was broke, ’cepting the mare’s habit o’
jibbiji’.
“Billy looked at the inventor coldly, and,
raising himself on his elbow, remarked senten-
tiously, ‘By gosh ! you get some other fool
do “ trisbuh’s ” work. That blanky fire-wire send
um marei to Sheol.’
“ As none of us would go with him, Necessity
was forced to make the thing more automatic, and
finally he brought it to such a pitch of perfection
that, when he pulled a string, the worst jibber in
Australi® went. Immediately he stopped pulling

JAMES F. DWYER.
In the Dark.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Feom our position of comparative comfort on the
skylight, we could, despite the darkness, watch
the movements of our fellow passengers who were
trying their sea-legs with little apparent success.
It was a little tumbling’ steamer bringing pas-
sengers from Mackay to meet the passing northern
and southern coasting-steamers.
One poor girl, travelling alone, narrowly escaped
a serious fall. As she was hastily retreating to
the side a sudden lurching movement sent her
staggering on to the iron-work, round which the
fierce wind blew her flimsy dress. She could
morve neither one way nor the other, and her slight
body was bumped from side to side. A man came
and released her, and gently helped her back to
the ledge near to us.
Having settled her comfortably he made no effort
to leave, but stood where he might act as a kind
of breakwind. Notwithstanding a very half-
hearted response from her, he managed to make
her listen to what he was saying. It was so dark
that only the dimmest outlines of her face and
figure were visible to him. Perhaps the darkness
made it easier for him to say what was in his
mind. We were lashed to our corner, and could
not help overhearing. He started to tell her the
story of his present journey.
For many years he had been engaged to a girl.
All Rockhampton knew it and his friends rallied
mm frequently on his allegiance to his absent
love, She lived in Mackay. By dint of working
hard and saving diligently he contrived to buy and
furnish a cottage for the girl. All being ready
he got leave of absence to go and claim the bride!
The night before he left, the Lake’s Creek boys (his
fellow workmen) entertained him at a smoke-night
and handed him a cheque to give the “ little girl”
from them. When he got to Mackay he found
her married to the proprietor of the Signalman’s
Hotel. He had gone casually in to get a drink
soon after he landed. Behind the bar stood the
girl he had come to marry—-a wedding-ring on her
finger.
* * * *
It was all so fresh still—his voice failed him as
he tried to speak of that interview. At length he
went on : “The reason. I have told vou this, Miss
is that I want you to help me. Will you marry
me ? I’ll be perfectly straight with you. I can’t
face the boys without a wife. Marry me and I
swear you’ll never regret it.”
The girl was mute, and the man went on his
voice deepening: “Perhaps I’m too hasty—come
to Rockhampton with me and I’ll pay your ex-
penses wherever you like to live for three months..
You will have a chance of getting to know me
You will be perfectly free, and 1 will ask youi
again at the end of the time. I could tell the:
boys you had been ill and the marriage had been
put off for three months.”
Still the girl hesitated, and a voice was heard!
saying, “Any more for the North—hurry up
please.”
“ Are you coming ? ” we heard the man say in
eager entreaty. “ Speak, girl, speak ! They are
waiting for us.”
That bald “us” ruined his chance. The
girl drew herself up. “No! no !” she said, “I
couldn’t possibly—go away at once.”
An ejaculation and the man was gone in the
darkness.
In the morning I caught a glimpse of the proud
maiden—a sallow, badly-dressed slip of a girl with
bright red hair. She was a sewing-girl returning
to Brisbane with her engagement not fulfilled,
through illness, and no money in her pocket.

Mr. Bill Lyne is seemingly anxious to
bring into the Federal arena the petty, and
worse than petty, ideas of the “ ward ” politician
of America. Announced that in
a letter to the N.S.W. Minister for Works he takes
exception to the State member for the district being
informed of any public work which is to be carried out
for the Commonwealth.
Object—to degrade the Federal member into
the position of being a touter for post office
towers and “a good local member” generally.
Mr. Bill Lyne has had a vast experience in
dealing with the sort of member who so badly
wants a local work that he is ready to do almost
anything for it, and he seems to want to see that
sort of member in the Australian Parliament,

An original melodrama, written by a local man, was
recently played at Unley (S.A.). There weretheunor.gi-
nal villain, with a black moustache, who said Ha, la.,
and remarked “A time will come’’; hero, the usual
person of noble descent quite unable to earn his own
living ; and heroine, who goes out, quite unnecessar
into a snowstorm minus any head-covering. The origy
nality presented itself in the last act. when the heroine
voluntarily and happily married the villain—who seemed
at least capable of earning a living, the useless hero being
killed out of the way.

"Bunny”:'The German opal-buyers in White Cliffs
are not popular citizens, though they are extremely well-
conducted men. \ Attended a performance the other
night in the Mechanic’s Institute. Performer was the
late Dante’s brother (so the hideously-colored play-bill
said> Just as the curtain went up, Messrs. Saurkraut,
La-er, Tonner, Blitzenwhistlen and Co., sat flown in re-
served seats. Then the push at the back rebelled. They
yelled, “ Too plue, too plue, Blank, Dash, Sausage, Saur-
kraut,” &c. Performance was delayed 10 minutes. ‘‘Too
plue ” is what the German opal-buyers say when they
do not like the opal submitted.
A new adaptation'of “No Thoroughfare,” by Wilkie
Collins and Dickens, is a result of the boom in Dickens
dramas. This will set J. C. Williamson thinking of bis
very young days, prior to the birth of “ Struck,. Oil,
when he was a dialect and “broad” comedian in an
American co., and Joey Ladle was one of his parts.
Perhaps if he thinks long enough about it, J.C.W. may
persuade himself to produce the new “No Thoroughfare ’
play, and revive himself as Joey Ladle for the fi-rst time
in Australia. Why not?
News of Dan Godfrey’s death recalls to mind Biondi’s
picture of Daniel in hi's imitations of orchestra conductors
at Rickards’ some years ago. The Cockney bandmaster
was represented as conducting one of bis trumpery old
waltzes—“ The Mabel in a starchy military style, with
much shaking of the head whilst he wagged his baton.
Dan Godfrey, 72 when he died, dated back to the darkest
age of London dance music. Thirty years ago people
waltzed to the see-saw tunes of Dan the Guardsman, and
thought them sweetest melodies. They ruled alike at
Court balls and shilling ‘ hops.’ So simple and “ catchy. ’
Boys whistled them in the London streets, and doggerel
verses of an improper character were chanted to their
measure—six syllables to the line. Then a German com-
poser of waltz music—a comparatively deep and soulful
jir.gler named Strauss —came to the fore with his “Blue
Danube” Waltz, and the feeble songs of the Cockney soldier
com noser suffered a total eclipse. But he remained an
institution, and by request of Queen Victoria old Dan was
kept in his place of bandmaster to the Grenadier Guards
up to 1896, and took the rank of Honorary Second
Lieutenant at her Jubilee.
A mummer’s yarn : Fisher wasn’t his rijrht name, but
that doesn’t matter. Fisher and I were pals—pals down
in luck—the truest of pals ;at least Fisher said so.
Fisher in those bad days, even declared Jie’d share his
last drink with me. Each of us had a dirty room m
a dirty lodging-house, in a dirty Sydney street. But wo
oughtn’t to complain, considering. Returning un-
expectedly from my bath one morning I dis-
covered Fisher in my room. He looked guilty ( of theft.
“Now, what have you been taking, Harry/ I asked ,
whereat he naturally answered, “ Nothing, arid went on
fastening'a snow-white collar rouhd his neck. But *
suspicion attacked me when I observed an end of a dirty
collar sticking out of his pocket. I took a step towards
him, placed my hand on his shoulder, looked into his
blue eves, put some emotion into my voice, and said,
broken’v “ Harrv, I didn’t think you’d lie cad enough to
steal a pal’s last dollar.” “My dear Beau,” he answered,
“ I had no intention ot. beating you for it ; I only in-
tended to exchange it for my dirty one. They’re adver-
tising for panto, artists to-day. I was going down to
apply. Hadn’t a clean collar. That’s all.” I lectured
him, bringing up trivial kindnesses I had in my time
done him—trivial kindnesses that, in nyy “ eloquence,”
I made appear mountainous. My oration affected him
much. “ Beau,” he said, “ you are right in rebuking me,
old man. I’m always imposing upon your good nature.
Here, take your c 'liar, dear boy,” and he threw it on my
bed, and then slipping a pair of cliffs off his arms,
continued—“ and these too. They’re yours. I took ’em
out of the same drawer as the collar.” Together we went
to the Royal—l with a clean collar, he with clean cuffs—
and were engaged at 25s a week for the parts of a dragon
and an elephant’s hind legs respectively.
One of the minor mysteries of Melbourne has just been
cleared up. For several weeks past midnight revellers in
the very centre of the city have been alarmed by wild
shrieks, evidently from a female, proceeding from the
Town Hall. On the first occasion the police were them-
selves alarmed by cries of distress - apparently the last
endeavors of some damsel to save her honor or her life.
Four powerful constables battered on the door of the
hall while a sergeant kept his thumb pressed against
the electric-bell button until an angered caretaker,
arose from his warm bed to walk about a quarter
of a mile along chilly tiled corridors in his bare
feet to open the door. Followed b.v the shivering care-
taker in his pyjamas, the police scoured the building
from the tower to the basement, hut not a sign of the
suffering woman could be found. After two hours,
during which time all the surrounding beats were left to
the mercy of the burglar, the police were forced to leave
the building unsatisfied. The following night, about the
same time, the same wild shrieks were repeated with
increased vigor. The caretaker was again aroused
and another search made, but without avail. A
third night, Melbourne was once more horrified, and
the people began to wonder whether Sam Gillott, of
the Tossum Rug, had a secret chamber in which
sorrowing maidens were immured. The united skill of
the police force at last decided upon concealing two of
its most astute detectives behind the curtains of the big
hall. The first ’tecs, allotted to the job threatened to
hand in their resignations, memories of the banshees of
their native land being too st rong. At last two Australian
agnostics were chosen. Holding their breath they
waited until the witching hour, and then, sure enough,
their agnostic blood was frozen by the yells which seemed
to proceed from some tortured female only a few
feet away. Grasping their revolvers tightly, the
detectives struck a light and found nothing. At that
moment there was another howl, unmistakably from
the end of the hall. For the instant, they were about to
stampede in panic. Then, the thing explained itself.
The Town Hall organ is in a dreadful state of disrepair,
and the mechanism has of late developed a way of going
off by itself and emitting weird howls when the vox
humana stop happens to give way simultaneously with
something else.
